# 易林

**The *Yi Lin* (Forest of Changes)**

**Title:** *Yi Lin* (also known as *Jiao Shi Yi Lin*, *Master Jiao’s Forest of Changes*). Authored by Jiao Gong of the Han dynasty. The *Sui Shu·Jing Ji Zhi* records this work in sixteen juan, though the present edition comprises ten juan. Source of the base text: *Wanli Xu Daozang* (Continuation of the Daoist Canon from the Wanli Era).

**Preface to the Sequence of the Upper and Lower Hexagrams of the *Yi Lin***

All transformations and images, all numbers and symbols, are inescapably bound to the *Yi*. Yet of all things, the deceits and truths of human nature are the most difficult to discern. Diviners still practice their art, dwelling alongside the anxious, offering wisdom and foresight—thus was this method preserved.

Yu Yan tilled the fields in the eastern wilds, his vision clouded by hardship. A visitor, deigning to call at his thatched hut, presented him with several scrolls of Jiao’s verses. Yu had once read the biographies in the works of Ban [Gu] and Sima [Qian], as well as the genealogies of eminent ministers through the ages and the writings of various scholars. These texts all extolled how, after Confucius transmitted the *Yi* to Shang Qu, barely ten successors remained; how Yan Shou passed down the classics and commentaries to Meng Xi, who was his contemporary. During the reigns of Emperors Yuan and Cheng of the Western Han, when governance declined, Master Jiao would emerge or retreat as circumstances demanded, always presenting the Dao of the *Yi* to the Prince of Liang. Thereupon, he was recommended by the commandery and appointed magistrate of Xiaohuang. Yet even the hidden affairs of the district were foreknown to him, earning him special favor, promotions in rank, and ultimately, his death in office.

His work, *The Comprehensive Transformations of the Great Yi*, comprises a total of four thousand ninety-six hexagram entries. Though its scope is vast, it converges in simplicity; its phrases borrow from the classics and histories, yet their meaning aligns with the divine. Pure, precise, and single-minded, every judgment proves accurate. The words are plain, but their import profound—easily grasped yet difficult to exhaust. One must not dismiss them as mere verbosity. For later enthusiasts who seek to understand your conduct, Master Ziyun’s work shall endure as immortal.

**Preface to the Collated *Yi Lin* of Jiao Gong**

By Huang Bosi, Gentleman for Consultation and Collator of the Palace Library, acting in his official capacity:

In the collation of Jiao Yanshou’s *Yi Lin*, certain characters were found to be erroneous—*kuai* (快) written as *yang* (怏), *yang* (羊) as *shou* (手), *xi* (喜) as *jia* (嘉), *he* (鹤) as *que* (鹊)—though their meanings may be retained in both forms.

Jiao Yanshou, whose given name was Gong, was a native of Liang. Through his devotion to learning, he gained the favor of the Prince of Liang, who provided for his needs and allowed him to study without restraint. Upon completing his studies, he served as a commandery official and was appointed magistrate of Xiaohuang. Whenever thieves were about to strike, he knew of it beforehand, and thus none dared act. His merit earned him promotion, and the officials and people petitioned to retain him. The imperial decree granted him an increase in rank, and he died in office at Xiaohuang.

The world speaks of Yanshou’s method thus: when divining a hexagram, one observes the corresponding forest of changes to determine fortune or misfortune. If the hexagram and lines remain unchanged, one simply examines the verses of the original hexagram and its lines. There was a man named Wang Bi who, in the second year of the Yongxi era, encountered an extraordinary person and divined the hexagram *Guan* (觀) transforming into *Bi* (賁). The *Lin* stated: *"Eastward travel has no gate; westward, one exits Mount Hua. The path is blocked by peril, and the wanderer meets with calamity."* These words proved most accurate. Those who come after must not fail to discern their meaning.

The works composed by Yanshou, though belonging to the divination texts of the Yin-Yang school, were written in the mid-Western Han, not long after the Three Dynasties. Their language is refined and unadorned, offering much worthy of perusal. Here, they are respectfully recorded.

*Miscellaneous Records*
The Elder Fei Zhi of Donglai said: The method of divination through the sixty-four hexagrams’ transformations was compiled by Jiao Yanshou of Tianshui, styled Jianxin, during the reign of Wang Mang. The *Yi* is vast and great indeed. Speaking of what is distant, it cannot be exhausted; speaking of what is near, it is still and correct; speaking of what lies between Heaven and Earth, it is complete. Yet the *Yi* refers to the sixty-four hexagrams—when extended further, it surpasses even the *Shuogua* in what it reveals. Thus, the *Lianshan*, *Guicang*, and *Zhouyi* all differ in their words yet share the same hexagrams. Though the three schools circulated concurrently, they still represent but one corner. Gong excelled in Yin-Yang studies and further devised this method to illuminate what the *Yi* had not yet shown. His predictions of fortune and misfortune, survival and ruin, often proved accurate when they matched the category of the matter at hand. For trivial or unrelated affairs, however, they often failed. Gong’s mastery of subtle principles was but one corner of the sage’s wisdom. Yanshou alone inherited the teachings of the recluses.

The *Book of Han* states: Jing Fang, styled Junming, studied the *Yi* under Jiao Yanshou of Liang, whose given name was Gong. Gong was poor and of low status but devoted to learning, earning the favor of the King of Liang, who provided for his needs and allowed him to study to his heart’s content. After completing his studies, he became a local official and was later recommended for promotion to magistrate of Xiaohuang.

Having anticipated and detected treachery and villainy in advance, he prevented the outbreak of theft and banditry. He cherished and nurtured the officials and common folk, so that moral transformation flourished throughout the county. When selected as the most outstanding for promotion, the village elders and his subordinates petitioned to retain Gong, and an imperial edict granted him an increased stipend to remain. He eventually died in Xiaohuang.

Gong once remarked: “The one who will bring ruin upon himself through me is Jing.” His teachings excelled in interpreting disasters and omens. He divided the sixty hexagrams, assigning each to govern a day in sequence, using wind, rain, cold, and warmth as observational signs, each with its own prognostications. Fang applied these methods with particular precision.

Meng Kang notes: The method of assigning hexagrams to days operates such that one line governs one day, and sixty hexagrams account for three hundred and sixty days. The remaining four hexagrams—*Zhen* (Thunder), *Li* (Fire), *Dui* (Lake), and *Kan* (Water)—serve as the officials overseeing the regional lords and inspectors. The reason for employing *Zhen*, *Li*, *Kan*, and *Dui* is that they correspond to the days of the solstices and equinoxes, when the qi of the four seasons each holds sway in turn. Each hexagram governs a day, and the method of divination involves observing auspicious and inauspicious signs on its respective day.

*Recorded Verifications* by Cheng Jiong of Shasui.

During the closing years of the Xuanhe era, Chancellor Cui of Changqingfu served as prefect of a commandery. At that time, the realm was at peace, and he reverently consulted this sacred text, casting the hexagram *Daguo* (Great Exceeding), which read:

*"The canonical records and legal texts,*
*Stored in the Orchid Terrace.*
*Though chaos and ruin spread,*
*They alone escape disaster."*

He then drew the line statement for *Dun* (Retreat):

*"The seat still warm,*
*Trouble knocks at the door.*
*Over the wall to flee north—*
*Soldiers come behind,*
*Yet one escapes the tiger’s jaws."*

Ten days after his divination, the commandery fell into turmoil. Chancellor Cui scaled the wall and fled, his family unharmed and untouched by misfortune. Upon returning to the capital, he recognized that this book was the work of a sage. Though its meanings were initially difficult to grasp, upon later reflection, none failed to align with events. Those who encounter it should hold it in utmost reverence. Gold itself is precious, yet it cannot compare to the worth of this text.

At the end of the Shaoxing era, Wanyan Liang invaded. Someone then consulted Jiao Gong’s *Forest of Changes* and cast the hexagram *Jie* (Deliverance) transforming into *Dazhuang* (Great Strength), which read:

*"The arrogant Hu, fiery in form,*
*Devises evil and commits atrocities.*
*Nothing he undertakes succeeds—*
*He brings ruin upon himself."*

So precise was its correspondence that even the celestial net’s gentle breeze could not surpass it. Since the dawn of time, only Liang could be said to fulfill this prophecy. How did Yan Shou, in composing his work, foresee that later ages would produce such a figure? Was it because Han-dynasty Jiao Yanshou transmitted the *Changes* to Meng Xi? The account appears in the *"Biographies of Confucian Scholars."* This is the very book he authored.

At the front of the text, Fei Zhi inscribed a preface, stating: "The sixty-four hexagrams transform [into one another]." Later, Wang Yu of the Tang dynasty added a preface. Each hexagram transforms into sixty-four variations, totaling four thousand ninety-six verses, all composed in rhyming lines. These closely resemble the phrases recorded in the *Zuo Tradition*—such as *"Phoenixes in flight, harmonious their cries, resonant and clear"*—or those in the *Book of Han*—such as *"Great Cross, geng-geng, I am the Heavenly King."* Could it be that diviners of antiquity possessed such texts?

In the cyclical year *Zhaoyang Dahuangluo*, during the month of *Huangji*, on the day of the Supreme Ultimate, the divination ritual proceeds as follows:

**Divination Apparatus**
Select a clean, secluded space to establish the *shishi* (divination chamber), its door facing south. Place the *lin* (divination table) at the chamber’s center. The table should be roughly five *chi* in length and three *chi* in width, positioned not too close to the walls. Prepare fifty stalks of *shi* (yarrow), wrapped in fragrant silk and encased in a black pouch, then stored in a *du* (receptacle) placed to the north of the table. The *du* may be made of bamboo, sturdy wood, or lacquered cloth, with a circular diameter of three *cun*, matching the length of the *shi*. It should be half base, half lid, with a separate stand to prevent tipping. Set a wooden frame south of the *du*, positioned at the northern two-thirds of the table. The frame, constructed from horizontal wooden planks, should be one *chi* in height, spanning the table’s length, with two large notches carved at the center, spaced one *chi* apart. To the west of the large notches, three small notches are made, each spaced roughly five *cun* apart, with horizontal feet affixed to stand upright on the table. Place one incense burner south of the frame and one incense box south of the burner. Burn incense daily to show reverence.

Before divination, sweep and wipe the area clean. Prepare one inkstone with water, one brush, one inkstick, and one yellow-lacquered board, placing them to the east of the burner, aligned from east to west.

The diviner, having purified himself and donned clean robes and cap, faces north, washes his hands, and burns incense to pay respects. The diviner’s northward orientation follows the *Etiquette and Ceremonial*. If someone else commissions the divination, the host burns incense, then steps back slightly to stand facing north. The diviner advances, stands slightly west of the table’s front, and faces south to receive the inquiry. The host states the matter to be divined directly, and the diviner assents. The host turns right and stands facing west, while the diviner turns right to face north.

With both hands, the diviner lifts the lid of the *du*, places it south of the frame and north of the burner, removes the *shi* from the *du*, discards the pouch and silk wrapping, and sets them east of the *du*. Combining the fifty stalks, he holds them in both hands and passes them over the incense burner. The subsequent use of the *shi* stalks follows the explanations in the *Primer of Enlightenment*.

He then recites the invocation:
*"By your greatness, O stalks, divination has its constancy;*
*By your greatness, O stalks, divination has its constancy.*
*[Name and title of the inquirer] now seeks clarity on [matter in question].*
*Is it auspicious or inauspicious? Will there be gain or loss, regret or remorse, worry or peril?*
*You, who are spiritually attuned, I beseech to reveal the truth."*

With his right hand, he takes one stalk and sets it aside in the *du*. Then, dividing the remaining forty-nine stalks with both hands, he places them in the left and right large notches of the frame. This is the first operation, *"dividing into two to symbolize the Two [Forces]."*

Next, with his left hand, he takes the stalks from the left large notch and holds them, while with his right hand, he removes one stalk from the right large notch and *"hangs"* it between the little finger of his left hand. This is the second operation, *"hanging one to symbolize the Three [Powers]."*

Then, with his right hand, he counts off the stalks in his left hand in groups of four. This is half of the third operation, *"counting by fours to symbolize the four seasons."*

He then gathers the remaining stalks—whether one, two, three, or four—and *"binds"* them between the ring finger of his left hand. This is half of the fourth operation, *"returning the remainder to the bind to symbolize the intercalary [month]."*

Next, he returns the counted stalks from his right hand to the left large notch, then takes the stalks from the right large notch and holds them, counting them off in groups of four with his left hand. This is the other half of the third operation. He then gathers the remaining stalks as before and binds them between the middle finger of his left hand. This is the other half of the fourth operation, *"binding again to symbolize the second intercalary [month]."*

After the first change, the remaining stalks will follow this pattern: if one remains on the left, three must remain on the right; if two on the left, two on the right; if three on the left, one on the right; if four on the left, four on the right. Including the *"hung"* stalk, the total will be either five or nine. Five, when its four are counted as one, becomes odd; nine, when its four are counted as two, becomes even. Thus, there are three odds to one even.

He then returns the counted stalks from his right hand to the right large notch and places the one *"hung"* and two *"bound"* stalks onto the first small notch of the frame, with the east side as the upper position (subsequent placements follow this order). This completes the first change.

Next, he combines the stalks from the left and right large notches—either forty-four or forty stalks—and repeats the four operations as in the first change, placing the *"hung"* and *"bound"* stalks onto the second small notch of the frame. This completes the second change.

After the second change, the remaining stalks follow this pattern: if one remains on the left, two must remain on the right; if two on the left, one on the right; if three on the left, four on the right; if four on the left, three on the right. Including the *"hung"* stalk, the total will be either four or eight. Four, when its four are counted as one, becomes odd; eight, when its four are counted as two, becomes even. Thus, odds and evens each account for two of the four possibilities.

Once more, he combines the stalks from the left and right large notches—either forty, thirty-six, or thirty-two stalks—and repeats the four operations as in the second change, placing the *"hung"* and *"bound"* stalks onto the third small notch of the frame. This completes the third change. The remaining stalks after the third change follow the same pattern as the second.

After the three changes, he examines the *"hung"* and *"bound"* stalks obtained from each change and draws the line on the board. If the *"hung"* and *"bound"* stalks total five or four, they are odd; if nine or eight, they are even. If the three *"hung"* and *"bound"* counts are all odd (thirteen stalks), then the stalks counted through (*guoshe*) total thirty-six, forming an *"old yang"* line, drawn as ⚊ (a solid line), known as *"double."*

If the *"hung"* and *"bound"* stalks total two odd and one even (seventeen stalks), then the stalks counted through (*guoshe*) number thirty-two, forming a *"young yin"* line, drawn as -- (a broken line), known as *"split."* If the *"hung"* and *"bound"* stalks total two even and one odd (twenty-one stalks), then the stalks counted through number twenty-eight, forming a *"young yang"* line, drawn as 一 (a single solid line), known as *"single."* If the *"hung"* and *"bound"* stalks total three even (twenty-five stalks), then the stalks counted through number twenty-four, forming an *"old yin"* line, drawn as ⚋ (a double broken line), known as *"crossed."* Thus, every three changes form a line.

The first, fourth, seventh, tenth, thirteenth, and sixteenth changes—six in total—follow the same pattern. However, from the third change onward, the count is not announced, and only forty-nine stalks are used. The second, fifth, eighth, eleventh, fourteenth, and seventeenth changes—six in total—also follow the same pattern. The third, sixth, ninth, twelfth, fifteenth, and eighteenth changes—six in total—likewise follow the same pattern. In all, eighteen changes form a hexagram. Then, one examines the hexagram’s transformations to divine the auspiciousness or inauspiciousness of the matter. Diagrams and explanations of hexagram transformations are found in *Qimeng*.

When the ritual is complete, the diviner conceals the stalks, wraps them, places them in a case, covers it, gathers the brush and inkstone, and the black board. Then, he burns incense, pays respects, and retires. If someone else performs the divination, the host burns incense, bows to the diviner, and retreats.

**Alternative Method Using Objects**
Zhu Yong states: Divining the *Changes* with milfoil stalks is the ancient method. In recent times, coins have been used to cast lines for convenience, but this cannot fully embody the Way of divination. Traditionally, the side of a coin with characters was considered yin, and the blank side yang. Thus, two tails (yin) formed a *"split"* (--), a broken line, while two heads (yang) formed a *"single"* (一), a solid line. Zhu Xi, however, held that the side with characters was the *face* (yang), and the blank side the *back* (yin). Since all things’ faces belong to yang and backs to yin, he reversed the old method, and scholars of Jian’an universally adopted his view. Some say that in ancient times, metal was cast into cowries or coins called *dao* or *quan*, with the yin side bearing inscriptions like the reverse of a mirror. Whether one side was yin and the other yang remains uncertain.

Generally, divination must use milfoil stalks; if seeking simplicity, the method must still be exhaustive. I once crafted three wooden spheres, each with six faces. On three faces of each sphere, three lines were carved; on the other three, two lines. When cast, they encompass all variations of old/young yin and yang. The three spheres, each with six faces, embody the principle of eighteen changes. Three faces (three lines each) represent the nine of *Qian* (☰), while three faces (two lines each) represent the six of *Kun* (☷). This reflects the use of spheres and the number eight.

The three lines of *Qian*’s single line contain three, while the two lines of *Kun*’s single line divide into two—this is the doctrine of *"three heavens, two earths."* When all three spheres land on three, they form nine, the number of *"old yang."* When all land on two, they form six, the number of *"old yin."* Two twos and one three form seven, the number of *"young yang."* Two threes and one two form eight, the number of *"young yin."* The lines of *Qian* and *Kun* are used to form the eight trigrams, symbolizing *Qian* and *Kun* giving birth to the six children.

The spheres represent the One of the Supreme Ultimate. Three threes form *Qian* (☰), and three twos form *Kun* (☷), symbolizing the Two (yin and yang). The three spheres represent the Three Powers: Heaven, Earth, and Humanity. Each sphere’s total is fifteen, the number of the *Luoshu*’s supreme pole. The combined total of the three spheres is forty-five, the number of the *Hetu*’s nine palaces. If two appear above, three lie below; if three appear above, two lie below—movement and stillness both yield five. Thus, five is concealed within the operation, and when combined with forty-five, it becomes the *Dayan*’s number of fifty.

When three spheres form nine above, three spheres conceal six below—this is the body of *"old yang"* transforming into yin. When three spheres form six above, three spheres conceal nine below—this is the body of *"old yin"* transforming into yang. Two and three oppose each other, and each sphere contains three and five—this is the principle of *"interweaving and varying numbers."* The spheres are round and revolving, embodying ceaseless change. The six positions interact, circulating through the six voids. Three spheres cast six times complete a day, also reflecting the principle of eighteen changes.

This method avoids the ambiguity of coin faces and backs regarding yin and yang, while encompassing all variations of old/young yin and yang. I have shared this with like-minded scholars, who found it reasonable, and thus recorded the method for the world to follow.

When a hexagram’s lines are obtained, old lines change while young lines do not. If a line changes, a new hexagram is derived. The sequence of hexagrams in the *Yilin*’s *Upper and Lower Canon* concludes here.

The original text mistakenly writes "一" (one); based on the context, it should be corrected to "一" (one).

*Yilin* Canon Catalogue
**Upper Canon**
Qian, Kun, Zhun, Meng, Xu, Song, Shi, Bi
Xiaoxu, Lü, Tai, Pi, Tongren, Dayou, Qian, Yu
Sui, Gu, Lin, Guan, Shihe, Bi, Bo, Fu
Wuwang, Daxu, Yi, Daguo, Kan, Li

**Lower Canon**
Xian, Heng, You, Dazhuang, Jin, Mingyi, Jiaren, Kui
Jian, Xie, Sun, Yi, Kuai, Shen, Cui, Sheng
Kun, Jing, Ge, Ding, Zhen, Gen, Jian, Guimei
Feng, Lü, Xun, Dui, Huan, Jie, Zhongfu, Xiaoguo
Jiji, Weiji

*Yilin* Upper Canon, Scroll One

**Hexagram Qian, First**

**Qian**   The Dao climbs steep slopes, yet words falter and stumble.
Translators grow gaunt and deaf; none let the Way pass through.
Petitions go unanswered; seeking affairs yields no success.

**Kun**   Summoning calamity to sting, harming our state and land.
Illness injures hands and feet; no peace or rest is found.

**Zhun**   Yang stands alone at its peak, filled with regret and doubt.
The carriage overturns, its canopy lost; the body knows constant dread.
Yet at last the wish is granted, as male and female follow one another.

**Meng**   The crane, the duck, the turtledove—single-minded, without fault.
The noble person takes this as a model, long receiving fine fortune.

**Xu**   Eyes bright and feet swift, joy as the wish is fulfilled.
The whole household receives favor.

**Song**   The dragon-horse ascends the mountain, where no springs flow.
Throat parched and lips dry, the tongue cannot speak.

**Shi**   Granaries overflow with countless measures; it is time to sow millet and grain.
The year is abundant, the harvest ripe; the people rest in peace.
*Alternatively:* The state is rich, the people flourish and multiply—akin to *Bi*’s *Sheng* and *Kun*’s *Tai*.

**Bi**   At midnight the dog barks; thieves lurk beyond the wall.
The gods’ protection aids us; all scatter and depart.

**Xiaoxu**   Grasping the Dipper, turning the pivot, following Heaven without care.
Acts that cultivate virtue dwell with joy as their neighbor.

**Lǚ**   Empty fists clench and unclench, falling to the ground only to rise again.
Wealth abounds, abundance overflows; joy knows no end.

**Tai**   No wind, no rain; the white sun shines bright.
It is fitting to ride forth, to traverse the great Dao.
*Same as Kun.*

**Pi**   The sun’s radiance blazes; the pennants harness six dragons.
Rank and fate reach Heaven; enfeoffed as King of Yan.

**Tongren**   The child wails, begging for food; the mother goes in search.
Returning to find the nest empty, she curses her long-lived offspring.

**Dayou**   Heaven’s creation brings daily blessings and protection.
Cultivating virtue and practicing kindness, one finds joy and peace.

**Qian**   The mountain is perilous, hard to climb; the ravine holds many stones.
The carriage races, the axle strikes; the load breaks the shaft.
The burden shifts, the bowl falls, the right foot stumbles.

**Yu**   Yu carved Dragon Gate, opening the water’s source.
Channeling it to the blue sea, the people dwell in safety.
*Alternatively:* Preserved.

**Sui**   Riding the dragon to Heaven, two serpents as escorts.
Leaping amid the clouds, gazing over the blue sea, the people delight in peaceful dwelling.

**Gu**   Pengzu’s nine sons, steadfast in virtue, never faltering.
The pine of South Mountain endures, long receiving fine fortune.

**Lin**   The southern mountain, vast Heaven—piercing governance, pitying the self.
Sorrow for the innocent; the back is hated as an enemy.

**Guan**   The Yangtze, the Yellow River, the Huai, the sea—Heaven’s hidden treasuries.
Where all benefits gather, one may prosper and enrich the noble person’s joy.

**Shihe**   Solid ice, yellow birds, ever wailing in sorrow.
No sweet grain is seen, only flowering mugwort.
Repeatedly startled by magpies and crows, my heart is troubled.

**Bi**   The house hangs like an empty chime, perilous and near ruin.
The wise who see this early dwell in mountain valleys.

**Bo**   Great Yu warned of the road; Chi You cleared the way.
Encircling ten thousand li, no danger, no peril.
Wherever its influence is seen, nothing is beyond reach.

**Fu**   Three make a journey, together returning to the northern sea.
Entering the gate, ascending the hall, they pay homage to the mistress of the house,
who offers them wine in gratitude.

**Wuwang**   Rumors lead astray, not by the direct path.
Beating drums to chase the fox, they know not the border’s edge.

**Daxu**   For three years they contend for a wife, racing in pursuit.
All day without food, their essence and qi exhausted and weary.

**Yi**   Clad in pure yellow robes, crowned with the highest, they rise.
Virtue and righteousness thus born, all under Heaven turn to benevolence.

**Daguo**   Jie and Zhou dwell together, the people grieve in suffering.
Bearing arms and shouldering grain, they battle in Qi and Lu.

**Kan**   Yellow birds come to roost, wed but not lingering.
Longing for my father and elder brother, I yearn to restore the state.

**Li**   Born from the womb, nurtured at the breast, they grow to maturity.
Filling the imperial chambers, the state and household flourish in abundance.

**Xian**   Three seek the orange, but return with a great chestnut.
The woman is honored for her wealth, with a hundred *yi* of yellow gold.

**Heng**   Eastern Mountain and Western Peak meet to feast together.
A hundred clans follow in attendance, forging grace and fortune.

**Chi**   The hen lacks spurs, yet fights the magpie.
Wings broken, eyes blinded, she is harmed by the turtledove.

**Dazhuang**   The towering wall collapses, the moth-eaten beam snaps.
Wolf and tiger seize power—Heaven sends down punishment and retribution.
Gao meets death at Wangyi; Huhai perishes.

**Jin**   Three fools flee together, losing the road and the Way.
Bewildered, they know not how to return, but turn back into the jaws of calamity.

**Mingyi**   Bow and arrow both drawn, the sling’s string snaps.
The pellet flies but does not reach—on the road, harm and misfortune befall.

**Jiaren**   Three maidens seek husbands, yet do not see the pass again.
They wait by the mountain’s corner, longing and sighing in sorrow.

**Kui**   Scorching sun, blazing drought, harming the grain and millet.
The reaper has no food; the plowman sighs in despair.

**Jian**   Riding a piebald to chase the sheep, they see not what they seek.
Cutting through the tiger’s den, they lose the writ and the lamb.

**Xie**   Dark and obscure, whispered words—who is led astray?
The demon’s dwelling—who knows where it lies?

**Sun**   Ji and Jiang, virtuous and refined, dine as a pair.
Discussing benevolence and blessing, they bring peace to the royal house.

**Yi**   Lord Sun drives a black steed, bearing gifts to Qi.
Yanling praises Chan, sending Ji with embroidered robes.

**Yang**   The ruins of Guzhu—husband lost, wife gone.
Grieving for Xianhua, he sees not his young bride.
The Tang and Jiang clans perish; the Wu house falls.

**Ni**   Benevolent governance, no violence—the phoenix comes to roost.
The four seasons follow their course; the people dwell in peace.

**Cui**   Weak in strength, feeble and timid,
Like the dragonfly before the magpie, it dares not resist.

**Sheng**   The Marquis of Wei journeys east, bewitched by the young concubine.
My father and mother are lost—long gone, they do not return.

**Kun**   Steadfast in word, none match my walls.
Joy and firmness endure—here one may long abide.
*As in* **Dayou** *and* **Zhun**.

**Jing**   Thunder roars on Mount Qi, the turtle responds from the mountain’s depths.
Male and female unite in essence—all things transform and take life.
King Wen thus succeeds, opening the courts of Zhou.

**Ge**   Black and yellow, the viper hisses—travelers weary and worn.
The laborers grow gaunt; the appointed time passes, yet they do not return.

**Ding**   Feeble feet, heels severed, they dare not step beyond the gate.
The market’s profit is lost; ruin and misfortune follow.

**Zhen**   The fox hangs idle, dwelling where it does not belong.
Losing its carriage, its hut stripped bare—it rests, sitting in vain.

**Gen**   The people fear evil, and villains lie in wait.
Bandits and thieves arrive in force, entering our walled city.
Wives and children are bound and taken.
*Alternate text for* **Guimei**.

**Jian**   The sun bows its head; the moon lifts its face.
Water turns to misfortune, harming my treasure.
Advance brings no profit—it bears a child.

**Guimei**   Back turned to the north, mutual hatred—
Hearts at odds, like fire and metal.
*Alternate text for* **Gen**.

**Feng**   The Supreme Shrine, the Emperor’s hall—
Where the Yellow Emperor dwells.
Barriers and screens stand firm; none may enter.
Ever secure, ever free from harm.

**Lü**   Silkworm cocoons and sacrificial beasts, reverently offered to ghosts and spirits.
The spirits relish the feast, bestowing blessings and many descendants.

**Xun**   Leaving the gate, one meets evil—misfortune and resentment arise.
They strike and stab at one another, wounding my hand.

**Dui**   The parrot retreats mid-flight; affairs do not advance.
The people of Song fall into chaos.

**Huan**   The lame and the crooked follow one another,
Till dusk, when the ox tires.
The journey lags behind; profit is lost, the female is gone.
*As in* **Tai** *and* **Fu**.

**Jie**   The dragon’s horns, broad and prominent—
A rank reaching the dukes and ministers.
Hereditary emoluments endure; action brings peace.
*As in* **Zhongfu**.

**Zhongfu**   Shun ascends, Great Yu arises in the wilds of Shiyi.
They journey to the royal court, offering governance of water and earth.

**Shiguo**   Following the wind to set fire, reeds and mushrooms perish together.
Three harms gather in the chamber—Shuzi is struck down.

**Jiji**   Thorns grow on Mount Ke; fate ordains tribute and rank.
Robes are stripped away—summer’s heat, winter’s cold.
Hunger and emaciation; none pity the multitude.

**Weiji**   Long face, great nose—coming to resolve my woes.
He leaves me a blessed son, bestowing joy and delight.

**Kun (Hexagram 2)**
**Kun**   No wind, no rain—the white sun shines bright.
Fit for driving forth, the great road lies open and smooth.

**Qian**   The valley wind spreads its breath; all things are born.
Sprouts and seedlings grow, blossoms and leaves flourish.

**Zhun**   The Azure Dragon stands alone, clashing with stone.
Both horns broken—household and home are wanting.

**Meng**   A horse on the city wall—its own fault destroys the home.
Summoning poison and venom, it brings disaster to the state.
*As in* **Xiaoxu** *and* **Xie*.

**Xu**   Frost descends, doors close; creeping things hide.
They see not sun nor moon, bound to death as their companion.

**Song**   Heaven’s virtuous chamber, warm and benevolent, receives blessing.
Where robes and garments dwell, evil does not arise.

**Shi**   The imperial steps, ninefold—none may ascend.
They say Heaven’s canopy is high, yet the king’s lordship remains unseen.

**Bi**   Confucius’ virtue is like jade, emerging from the dark valley,
Soaring to the towering tree.
Beating its wings, its radiance illumines the state.

**Xiaoxu**   Five norms, four standards—abundance is restored.
Exerting strength in rank, Yu and Yu rejoice.

**Four**   Hooves without horns—the noble one’s mount.
Journeying south on official duty, to bring favor to our land.

**Lü**   Worn bamboo on the bridge, weaving and drifting without restraint.
The fisherman toils; the household’s mouths go dry.

**Tai**   Thunder rolls in pursuit, ceaseless and unrelenting.
Battle on the plain—trampled by the Yi.

**Pi**   Six dragons contend at the limit, their service unadorned.
Careful with bolts and locks, binding and forbidding all exit.

**Tongren**   Elder son and youngest daughter, facing one another in speech.
Cutting firewood at Ke—fortune and blessing bring joy.

**Dayou**   Delaying and hindering good men, virtue does not flow.
Fire rages as calamity—grain and millet suffer great harm.

**Qian**   Cultivating wings and feathers, following the wind northward.

To the lands of Yu and Xia, in harmony with Shun.
Years of great joy—no thieves in the towns.

**Yu**   A dull blade carves jade, firm and unyielding.
Exhausting all my strength, the silkworm’s cocoon brings illness.
*(Comparable to* **Daguo**)

**Sui**   Sleeves raised to cover the eyes, blocking sun and moon.
Clothing and skirts in disarray—long nights in the chamber.
*(Like* **Lin** *and* **Tong** *in* **Tai**)

**Gu**   Villains harm virtue, great wrath brings no blessing.
Severing the ancestral altars, losing their state and land.

**Lin**   White dragon and crimson tiger, battling in shared fury.
Chiyou flees in defeat, perishing at Fish Mouth.

**Guan**   The Northern Asterism’s Purple Palace, where robes and caps stand at the center.
Embracing harmony and establishing virtue, ever receiving Heaven’s favor.
*(Same as* **Jia** *in* **Xu** *and* **Jie** *in* **Kun**)

**Shihe**   Houji sent by Yao, west to meet the Queen Mother.
Bowing to request a hundred blessings, bestowed with a fine son.

**Ben**   Two men pursue different paths, turning back in confusion.
One body with five hearts—seeking, yet gaining nothing.

**Bo**   A great ape from Southern Mountain snatches my beloved concubine.
Timid, I dare not pursue, retreating to sleep alone.

**Fu**   Pursued by the crowd’s blame, transformed into a radiant marvel.

**Nine Bodies Without Heads**
Nine bodies without heads, souls startled, spirits fled—no place to dwell.

**Wuwang**
Stretching the neck to gaze afar, dimness blinds the eyes.
Failing to see Aunt Ji, Uncle’s heart is filled with sorrow.

**Daxu**
Canonical texts and legal writings, stored in Orchid Terrace.
Though chaos and ruin arise, alone they escape disaster.
As in **Dayou**.

**Yi**
From Wei returning to Lu, the time is not to be shared.
Ice and charcoal in separate chambers—has the Way of benevolence been blocked?

**Daguo**
Tumors, ulcers, baldness, scabies—harms to the body.
Sickness, disease, afflictions, and wounds never far away.

**Kan**
Bound in Qi, dwelling in Lu, wed to the Luo capital.
Outstanding and fine, the matchmaker’s profit doubled.

**Li**
Qi and Lu dispute in words, battling at Dragon Gate.
Grudges breed misfortune, three generations unsettled.
As in **Gu** of **Bi** and **Kui** of **Ben**.

**Xian**
Rich soil and fertile fields, farmers reap abundance.
Living in Chang’an, through the ages without harm.
As in **Shi** of **Qian** and **Sheng** of **Bi**.

**Heng**
Granaries overflowing, stores beyond count—fit for planting millet and sorghum.
Years of plenty, harvests bountiful, the people rest in peace.

**Chi**
Crows shatter the axe, the state’s peril begins.
By their loyal virtue, misfortune turns to fortune, danger restored to stability.

**Dazhuang**
Famine years without harvest, cruel governance harms the people.
Qianxi and Lishan—Qin and Chu forge enmity.

**Jin**
Chains of purity, layer upon layer, knots hard to undo.
Mother Mu’s marriage on the street—schemes fail, and she brings ruin upon herself.

**Mingyi**
Zizou opens the gate, cranes cry, caps are dusted off.
Zhangfu is employed, the *Shun* dances with harmonious bells.
Three assist in governance, the state knows no disaster.

**Jiaren**
Sisters dwelling together, kindred aiding one another.
Longing for the flock, never parting from their friends.

**Kui**
Deng Deng reverts to antiquity, father and elder brother suffer.
Crossing this sorrow and resentment, they die in the end, never returning.

**Jian**
Two pursue the hare, each vying for the prize.
The beloved concubine, swift of foot, bears many offspring.

**Xie**
The Northern Asterism’s Purple Palace, where robes and caps stand at the center.
Embracing harmony and establishing virtue, ever receiving Heaven’s favor.
As in **Guan** of **Kun** and **Jia** of **Xu**.

**Sun**
Kneeling to beg for mercy, yet no food is granted.
Ni Shou takes the bit, bows his head, and departs northward.

**Yi**
A crane steals my pearl, carrying it to the eastern corner.
Seeking my walled home, its dwelling is nowhere to be found.

**Jiong**
One reed, two tongues—reckless words and scheming plots.

**Three Falsehoods Make a Tiger**
Zeng’s mother casts her shuttle in doubt.

**Shen**
Elder Tiger and Second Bear, their virtue and righteousness truly fine.
They spread the five grains, yin and yang in orderly sequence.
Or: Dwelling alone and solitary, none to journey with—
body toils in hardship.
They spread the five grains, yin and yang in orderly sequence.

**Cui**
Tattered robes ford the river, the ravine’s flow swift and deep.
By fortune, a boatman appears—crossing safely, no mishap.

**Sheng**
Leaning on the river to climb the mountain, the path blocked and treacherous.
Seeking affairs, few prove convenient.

**Kun**
The hare feigns composure, never losing its reverence.
Harmony and humility bring joy—thus the noble one finds accord.

**Jing**
Three maidens seek a husband, waiting by the mountain’s edge.
No sign of *Fu Guan*—tears flow in streams.

**Ge**
Moths and worms turn traitor, harming my five grains.
Baskets and jars lie empty—nothing left to eat.
*Xu*’s *Ming Yi*.

**Ding**
Gazing up to Aligning Balance, the Grand Steward, Duke of Zhou.
Screen and shield, assisting ministers—blessings and fortune converge.

**Zhen**
Three years breed a dog, completing a *Xu*-born mother.
Jing and Yi press northward—the Lord of Ji flees.
*Xu*’s *Song*, *Pi*’s *Shen*.

**Gen**
Paths twist and roads are blocked—seeking affairs, none succeed.

**Jian**
Reaching into the bosom, grasping a flea—desires stray from the Way.
Or: Below lies emptiness—misfortune and sorrow. Dwelling in gradualness brings goodness.

**Gui Mei**
Flying ants meet the road, their feet stirring dust against walls.
Dwelling brings no peace—overturned, it becomes a burden.

**Feng**
Righteousness cannot overcome desire—self-ruin follows.
Nearly imperiled by favor and profit—horns break, head is crushed.

**Lü**
*Tong* and *Yu* of old, raising their heads to gather.
Sweat descends, torrents pour in cascades.

**Xun**
A white colt born of fodder, leaning on a jealous younger sister.
Illustrious and virtuous noble one—joy makes one forget sorrow.

**Dui**
Chariots race, men chase—armor rolled, they turn to foes.
Qi and Lu wage war, defeated at *Da Qiu*.

**Huan**
Raising my head to gaze at the city, I see not *Zi Zhen*—
it makes me rue my birth.

**Jie**
Dragons wrangle at the gate, losing principle and harming the worthy.
Internal rebellion breeds treachery—thus calamity arises.

**Zhong Fu**
Steadfast as Mount Tai, blessings and protection repeatedly arrive.
Though leopards and tigers lurk, none dare endanger me.

**Xiao Guo**
First sorrow, then joy—trading with fortune.
Eight rows arrayed in formation, feasting with noble friends.
*Song*’s *Chui*.

**Ji Ji**
Holding a knife, grasping meat—facing wine, yet not eating.
The husband marches to war, the youngest son enters prison—
hugging his knees, he sleeps alone.

**Wei Ji**
Yin wanes, old and exhausted—yang establishes its power.
Treading *Li*, wearing *Li*’s light, the world shines bright.
Yet the enterprise does not endure—shrimp and spider seize the throne.
*Pi*’s *Wu Wang*, *Da You*’s *Lin*.

---

**Third of *Zhun***

**Zhun**
Troops march on *Da Yuan*, north through the Jade Gate.
Clashing with the Hu raiders west of *Ping Cheng*—
seven days without grain, nearly perishing.

**Qian**
Adrift in a catalpa boat, drifting without cease.
Restless in waking and sleep, the heart holds great sorrow.
Virtue meets no timely fate—again, it hides in poverty.

**Kun**
Gathering firewood, one finds a qilin—great destiny falls.
Heroes vie for fame, the world divides fourfold.
*Yu*’s *Wei Ji*.

**Meng**
Mountains collapse, valleys sever—great fortune is exhausted.
The *Jing* and *Wei* lose their order, the jade calendar is spent.

**Xu**
The thatched hut of *Xia Tai*—where King Wen was confined.
*Gui Hou* offers bribes, the Shang king unshackles him.

**Song**
Mired in filth and disgrace, cast aside in ditches.
Those who once laughed and wept with you—none are recorded in glory.

**Shi**
Plums and wintersweet, treasures of the season—yet the state teems with thieves.
Chaos erupts in unison—the lord finds no rest.
*Tong Ren*’s *Sun*.

**Bi**
Deer and roebuck follow the herdsman, sated, they return home.
Turning back to their lodgings, none suffer illness or harm.

**Xiao Xu**
Marriage pledged across the river—yet when the time comes, no boat.
Lustful hearts lose hope—no sight of their beloved.

**Lü**
A hundred legs move as one, strength in mutual support.
Three sages assist the cause—the royal house shines in favor.
*Bi*’s *Wu Wang*.

**Tai**
Seated out of place, unable to settle.
Regulating and harmonizing awry—yin and yang overturned.

**Pi**
Ascending the dais, mounting the chariot, driving four steeds southward.
Alliances scatter and crumble—Yan and Qin grow strong.
*Tong Ren*.

**Da You**
The River Lord roars loudly—the ford cannot be crossed.
The boat lies empty, no one comes or goes.

**Qian**
Sweet dew and sweet springs, the mechanisms of great peace.
Virtue’s resonance brings response—years of joy, the people at ease.
*Da Guo*’s *Wei Ji*.

**Yu**
Layered mats, thick cushions—climbing high to pluck mallows.
Though stumbling, no fear—later, one returns home.
Or: Ten thousand apes fall from lofty trees, limbs numbed and hands crippled.
Yet they guard my gold and jade, returning to their chambers.

**Sui**
The Great One rides a six-dragon chariot, descending from heaven.
Summoning my younger brothers, he enfeoffs them as lords of Lu—
no misfortune or sorrow.
*Xu*’s *Bi*, *Pi*’s *Da Zhuang*.

**Gu**
The south is now secure, stone pestles reach the sky.
Aiming without cease—once aged, she marries again.
The old village lies ruined, the house decrepit—
a new home is built.

**Lin**
Households abound, people prosper—hymns rise in unison.
The four barbarians submit, weapons sheathed in their racks.
Or: *Guan* hexagram.

**Guan**
The eastern neighbor marries off her daughter—
she becomes the king’s consort.
Duke Zhuang builds a palace to honor the queen mother.
Returning to the capital, *Ji Jiang* rejoices.
Or: *Lin* hexagram, *Pi*’s *Ji Ji*.

**Shi He**
*Chen Wan* and *Jing Zhong*—northward to *Qi Jiang*.
They journey to *Ying Qiu*—eight generations flourish greatly.
*Shen*’s *Shi*, *Bi*’s *Yu*.

**Bi**
The road brims with thorns and brambles,
each step pierces my foot.
No boon for travelers—
it poisons the heart.
Same as *Sun* hexagram.

**Bo**
Celestial palaces array the stars,
five spirits dwell in halls.
Palace gates gleam, firm as stone—
the lord rests secure in his abode.

**Fu**
Shepherding sheep in rice fields,
a tiger’s growls resound.
Trembling in fear, I hold my breath—
yet escape disaster in the end.
*Pi*’s *Jie*.

**Wu Wang**
The calamity of wailing branches—
fleeing north to the great Hu.
Left robes as lords, the land named Xiongnu.
Their chieftain shaves his head,
enthroned as the exalted *Chanyu*.

**Da Xu**
Cultivating purity, refining the self,
encountering Yu’s imperial progress.
Bestowed a dark jade tablet,
I bow to receive its blessed protection.

**Yi**
Winter blossoms bear no fruit,
the land teems with bandits.
Sickness defies the healer—
ghosts wail within the house.

**Da Guo**
Escorting the youngest daughter
to the swaying road.
The Qi lord lingers day and night,
delaying, dwelling long.

**Kan**
Rotten roots, a toppled tree,
flowers and leaves fall away.
Suddenly met by flames and crowds,
swept by wind, they collapse in heaps.

**Li**
Yin transforms to Yang,
a woman becomes a man.
The Way of governance flows clear—
lord and minister align in succession.

**Xian**
The many sever the light’s thread,
yet brightness fades and returns.
Simplicity grasps the principle—
once more, Qian’s work is complete.

**Heng**
Burdened with heavy loads,
abandoned in the wilds.
Whose child is the Queen Mother?
Only self-inflicted toil.

**Guang**
Rivers, lakes, and seas—
abundant blessings, a secure home.
Wealth may flourish here,
feasting and hosting honored guests.

**Da Zhuang**
Gathering winter’s vetch and orchids,
the earth frozen, hard as stone.
Fleeing east and north—
by dusk, nothing gained.

**Jin**
Crows and magpies caw,
heaven’s fire will rise.
It scorches my house and hall,
disaster reaches the queen consort.

**Ming Yi**
Wasps swarm the door,
stinging my hands and feet.
No advance or gain—
harm and misfortune to the self.
*Lü*’s *Tai*.

**Jia Ren**
The towering northern peak,
a guest revered by heaven’s spirits.
Warm, benevolent, and upright,
the lord bestows grace and virtue.
Endless lament and sorrow—
yet great fortune descends.

**Kui**
Bo Yi and Shu Qi, blind,
unfit to guard or herd.
I lose my robes and furs,
another takes my mourning garb.

**Jian**
Seeking a wife for the youngest,
her home lies by the eastern sea.
The waters rise, no boat in sight—
my beloved remains unseen.

**Jie**
A tomb stands on the hill,
the soul’s abode lost.
Spirit and essence exhausted—
eternal slumber, no waking.

**Sun**
The ox loses its horns,
descending the mountain, the axle breaks.
Its emolument and rank forfeit—
who bears the fault?

**Yi**
Water bears the boat,
rootless, it drifts.
To and fro, endlessly—
the heart labors in sorrow.

**Gou**
A bird comes in flight,
perching on an ancient tree.
Its cry is loathsome—
the master departs.

**Dun**
Eastward migration untimely,
stumbling into woe and grief.
The well’s mud yields no moisture—
longing for the old home of Shu.

**Cui**
Born where the Yellow Emperor dwelt,
beneath Fuxi’s roof.
No blade reaches here—
a boon for dwelling in peace.
*Lü*’s *Jia Ren*.

**Sheng**
Eastern mountains quell disorder,
a wife pines for her husband.
My lord labors without rest—
his service never ceases.

**Kun**
Lame and stumbling, not yet risen,
losing profit in the late market—
the fawn remains uncaught.

**Jing**
A great serpent blocks the path,
filling me with dread.
Fire and scalding disaster
graze my skin.
By heaven’s grace,
I leap beyond the master’s hut.
*Sun*’s *Bi*.

**Ge**
Leisurely and at ease,
roaming the southern mountains.
Offering prayers to the spirits—
the gods spare me misfortune.
Or: *Zhen* hexagram.

**Ding**
Kangju, dwelling in distant lands,
admires virtue and enters court.
The joy of dew-drenched revelry—
three cups, the grace complete.
Returning to the wild hut,
awaiting my mother.

**Zhen**
Tortoises and softshells line the market,
rivers and seas overflow with plenty.
Long wealth favors the merchant—
Shanggui rejoices.
Or: *Ge* hexagram.

**Gen**
Years of constant blessing—
this year, fortune received.
Gathering *ba* in the triple summer heat—
surely, gains await.

**Jian**
Two men journey east,
their paths clashing in dispute.
Hearts at odds, discordant—
misfortune befalls me.

**Gui Mei**
Planting beans and millet,
deer and hares devour them.
The lord neglects protection—
autumn yields no harvest.

**Feng**
The yellow bird mourns,
grieving the unseen stars.
Ensnared by hawks and kites—
my heart trembles in fear.

**Lü**
Two birds seen in flight,
longing to return to the rice pond.
Crossing marsh and hill,
an arrow strikes—
my chest and breast wounded.
*Pi*’s *Jin*.

**Xun**
A guest too long without respite,
yearning for home.
Heavy rains overflow—
the road remains impassable.

**Dui**
The path is secluded,
south to eastern Liaodong.
Weizi’s eloquence—
the state knows no sorrow.

**Huan**
Sharing pillow and robe,
midlife they part.
Little profit, no gain—
alone, sorrow lingers.

**Jie**
The gods gather in counsel,
their voices intertwined.
The southern land oppressed,
its people grieve in suffering.
An army rises to chastise—
a worthy king enthroned.
*Xiao Xu*’s *Yu*.

**Zhong Fu**
The northern wilds sealed,
hidden, they do not emerge.
Eyes blind, ears deaf—
roads remain impassable.

**Xiao Guo**
Madness and delusion,
the heart deceives, beguiles.
Wandering astray, losing the way—
north from south, unknown.

**Ji Ji**
The ridgepole stands tall, the beams strong.
Favor and honor grow daily.
Blessings and goodness unite—
joy in lofty brightness.

**Wei Ji**
Cherishing my infant girl,
clutching her clothes, I refuse to part.
Hoping for high rank—
instead, she sinks into lowliness.

**Meng’s Fourth**

**Meng**
What plant does not yellow?
Even the darkest fades by year’s end.
Households sundered—
grief and sorrow fill the heart.

**Qian**
The sea reigns as lord of waters,
keen and sagely, radiant.
A hundred streams return to virtue,
none rebel or stray—
ever abundant, ever sufficient.

**Sun’s Lü**

**Kun**
Heaven’s protection—
disaster passes the household by.
Left and right, aides assist,
gold and jade fill the coffers.
Ever full, never lost—
wealth like a granary’s store.

**Tun**
Anxi and Kangju,
foreign lands of yurts.
Not our customs—
they grieve my heart.

**Xu**
Fan the potter, Tao the merchant,
skilled in trade, adorned in wealth.
Bound for camp, he sets forth,
exchanging his name for Zi Pi.
Pearls in hand, gold in tow—
blessings and profit return.

**Song**
The old poplar wanes,
its branches withered and dry.
Rank and station unmade—
thus it withers and decays.

**Tai’s Xian**

**Shi**
The little fox crosses the water,
sweat soaks its tail.
Grasping the subtle—
it aligns with the Dao’s tally.

**Bi**
Pigs born like spinning,
rats dance in the hall.
The boar waits for Jiang’s offering—
above and below, murky and dim.
The lord loses his state.

**Xiao Xu**
Heaven and Earth unite in offering,
the six positions shine bright.
Yin and yang follow their order—
thus their work is fulfilled.

**Song’s Zhen**

**Lǚ**
Forging steel wounds the heel,
the right toe stricken with palsy.
Missing the journey’s time,
profit flees—no return.

**Tai**
Thoughts of shared grace,
each to their own kin.
The widow of the western neighbor
seeks shelter in my chamber.
The mistress scolds Jin,
her child’s search in vain.

**Pi**
Tilling the fields of Fan’s acres,
praying for millet’s loan.
Food fills the center,
peace and harmony—no blame.

**Tong Ren**
Newly granted great favor,
fortune and blessing redouble.
Joy and daily enrichment,
the household celebrates wealth.

**Da You**
Raising the cup to drink,
the finger straight—yet misses the grasp.
No warmth against the cold,
no gain, no fear.

**Qian**
Sun and moon gaze upon each other,
radiance flourishing and grand.
The Three Sages thrive in succession—
their merit and virtue greatly exalted.
*Shi’s Jie, Pi’s Ben.*

**Yu**
Crafty men vie in strength,
the people flee their homeland.
Gongsun Shuzi
battles south of the city.

**Sui**
The ape falls from the lofty tree,
unharmed in limb.
Preserving my whole life,
I return to my chamber.
*Pi’s Lin, Gu’s Yu, Song’s Gen.*

**Gu**
Chasing the fox east of the mountain,
water blocks my path.
Too deep to ford,
profit lost—delayed advantage.

**Lin**
Boring a well to seek jade,
not the treasure of Bian’s house.
Name besmirched, body shamed,
toil without gain.

**Guan**
Yellow jade, warm virtue,
worn by the noble man.
Sweet dew soaks the heat,
all things flourish and thrive.

**Shi Ke**
Painting the dragon’s head and neck,
the pattern incomplete.
Honeyed words, fair speech,
persuasive phrases—yet no renown.

**Ben**
Summoning misfortune, inviting calamity,
it comes to harm my state.
Sickness in hand and foot,
no rest, no peace.

**Bo**
Treading the path of eastern power,
no end to ruin.
Ennobled as a bondservant’s chart,
ranked among the common folk.

**Fu**
Roebuck, deer, pheasant, hare,
gathered in the eastern land.
Black, yellow, white-backed,
all pursue the chase.
Nine in ten captured—
the noble man rejoices.

**Wu Wang**
Weaving gold unfinished,
the weft exhausted—no name.
The eldest son chases the hare,
the deer loses the trail.
Seeing profit, yet no gain.

**Da Xu**
Heaven rejects Zhou’s virtue,
command given to the land of benevolence.
With rites, the people are pacified,
weapons and armor laid to rest.

**Yi**
Through interpreters, the fungus is bought,
coming to dispel my sorrow.
Fair words pursue the good,
seeking joy in accord.

**Da Guo**
Rich soil, fertile fields,
the people at peace.
Fitting to dwell, fitting to stay—
thus Chang’an grows abundant.

**Kan**
White dragon, black tiger,
rising in sudden fury.
Battle at Banquan,
Chiyou routed in flight.
Dwelling secure, no peril—
the lord rests in his place.
*Tong Ren’s Bi, Yi’s Bi.*

**Li**
Guarding the gate, relaying words,
deaf and lame, sinews broken.
Breaking the thief without harm,
yet unsettled in his place.

**Cheng**
Sorrow and misfortune dissolve,
joy arrives with celebration.
Sitting, standing at the gate of mirth,
neighbor to delight.
*Xiao Xu’s Jing.*

**Heng**
Breaking the blade, sheathing the spear,
horses released to rest.
The hunting army camps in order—
the world at peace.

**Yu**
The lord of utmost virtue,
his governance kind and warm.
Yi and Lü as his limbs,
the state rich and ever secure.

**Da Zhuang**
Gazing a thousand *li* at the city,
no green mountains in sight.
Old hare, toad—
far removed, no home.

**Jin**
The youngest daughter of Youshen,
wife to Xia’s lord.
Noble husband, long-lived son,
Mother Ding of the Four Seas.

**Ming Yi**
Unforeseen disaster,
calamity arrives without warning.
Sudden and swift demise,
grief wounds my heart.

**Jia Ren**
The flying hawk retreats,
no pheasant consumed.
Anxieties of the heart dissolve—
the lord abides in peace.

**Kui**
Mourning bands, stumbling steps,
Shen and You bring misfortune.
Yanzi hides in obscurity.

**Jian**
The recorder vents his wrath,
plans and counsel lack the Way.
Xu and Hai overthrow Shang—
the people lose governance,
Yin’s sacrifices fail.

**Xie**
Gazing for the chicken, the chick is found;
seeking the horse, the colt is gained.
Great virtue begets the young,
Liao dwells in their midst.

**Sun**
Anxious, trembling,
as if life hangs in doubt.
The grace of millet and grain—
Ling Zhe preserved,
life secured, years protected.

**Yi**
Silent and still,
working by night, hiding by day.
Plotting to seize my wealth,
they come to attack my home.
My riches exhausted,
scarcely my own food remains.

**Yang**
What Heaven destroys,
none can prop up.
The multitude’s mockery falls—
though noble, peril is certain.

**Shen**
Eyes darting, cheeks flushed,
joy comes to rest upon me.
The whole household shares delight,
fortune and blessing—no harm.

**Cui**
Fragrant broth, steaming,
fingers dipped, sleeves brushed.
Hungry mouth, clapping hands—
the lord’s son resents slander.

**Sheng**
Heaven’s blessing abundant,
the omen like a soaring dragon.
Success in my aspirations,
the six in the second rises.

**Kun**
Meng Bo seeks marriage,
clutching cloth as his own matchmaker.
Abandoning rites for haste,
he ends in regret and sorrow.

**Jing**
Xia Si draws near,
her heart listens with gladness.
Profit in broad pursuit—
no word left unanswered.

**Ge**
Southern mountains, lofty skies,
the government’s sting pierces the body.
Sickness and sorrow without guilt,
turning from kindness to enmity.
*Variant: Gen hexagram.*

**Ding**
Three travel as one,
together they journey to the northern sea.
Entering the gate, ascending the hall,
they bow to the Queen Mother,
who labors to bestow wine.

**Zhen**
Yang’s excess brings drought,
harming the crops.
Reaping in sickness comes,
the farmer has no food.
*Variant: Jing hexagram.*

**Gen**
Snatching rice, grasping meat,
bringing it to the mouth.
Wherever they go, they gain—
no lack, no want.

**Variant: Ge hexagram.**

**Jian**
Birds fly without wings,
hares run with broken legs.
Though they wish to meet,
they are hindered and confused.

**Guimei**
Heavy-bodied, hard to fly,
unable to pass the frontier.
Sitting or walking in sorrow,
never leaving the courtyard walls.

**Feng**
Four heroes dwell together,
the people grieve in suffering.
Armed forces gather east and west,
no place to rest in peace.

**Lü**
A prison reopens,
seeking to resolve one’s cares.
The heart responds and succeeds—
dwelling with the good.

**Xun**
Troubles dissolve, sorrows fade,
the Queen Mother bestows favor.
Joy comes with her,
bringing me peace at home.

**Dui**
Born in winter, no blossoms,
an old woman without a home.
Frost clings to the thatched hut,
all wither into dry stalks.

**Huan**
Trembling in fear,
beset by dread.
The road is fraught with peril—
no crossing is possible.

**Jie**
Three men share one wife,
none claims the role of mate.
The child bears no name,
the elder’s kin unknown.

**Zhongfu**
Withered early by frost,
flowers and leaves do not grow.
Untimely disaster strikes—
the household suffers its woe.

**Xiaoguo**
East of the pheasants and hares,
wolves and tigers feed.
Greedy and fierce,
their violence knows no end.

**Jiji**
The horse bolts, the cart shatters,
the king falls into a deep ditch.
Body dead, soul departed—
his home and hall abandoned.

**Weiji**
Mountains and forests, lush marshes,
where the people gather.
Beasts and birds show no propriety—
my heart is pained.

**Fifth Line of Xu Hexagram**

**Xu**
Three years of drought,
no grass or trees grow.
Granaries stand empty,
nothing to offer the spirits.

**Qian**
Fire extinguished, then rekindled,
the lord brightens his virtue.
A benevolent man appears—
he receives blessings and emolument.

**Kun**
Warm mountains, pines and catalpas,
ever lush, never falling.
The luan and phoenix find shelter—
they know its joy.

*Variant: Pi hexagram.*

**Tun**
Four punished, none submit,
relying on strength and force.
Doubling back to rush the foe,
the army is routed.

*Variant: Yi’s Tongren hexagram.*

**Meng**
Three paths, five peaks,
Yangcheng’s great chamber.
Protected by divine light—
no war or weapons here.

**Song**
Three oxen bear a dog,
using the dog to bury their mother.
The Xianyu invade above,
the Earl of Ji flees.

*Variants: Kun’s Zhen hexagram, Pi’s Shen hexagram.*

**Shi**
Brightly roaming rivers and seas,
journeying a thousand li.
Thought lost to death,
yet seen again in hollow mulberry—
the land of long life and joy.

**Bi**
The Great One rides a black steed,
descending from heaven.
The lords Shu and Ji are summoned,
enfeoffed as marquises of Lu—
no misfortune or sorrow.

*Variants: Tun’s Sui hexagram, Pi’s Dazhuang.*

**Xiaoxu**
Ren and Su dwell alone,
widowed, without a husband.
Yin and yang lose their will—
made servants to others.

**Lü**
The army marches on Da Yuan,
north through the Jade Gate.
Clashing with the Hu raiders,
west of the road to Pingcheng.
Seven days cut off,
his life nearly lost.

*Same as Tun.*

**Tai**
King Ling of Chu, cruel and oppressive,
exhausted the people’s strength.
Disaster arose at Qianxi,
and Jizu wrought poison.
Leaning on a staff, he fled—
died in the chamber of Hai.

**Pi**
The hen dwells alone,
returning to her nest.
Her feathers wither,
her will like dead ashes.

**Tongren**
Two spears thrust at each other,
matched in courage and might.
Truces are tied,
unbroken, unchipped.

*Truces: a sign of retreat.*

**Dayou**
Boarding a boat to cross the river,
laden with water, met by fire.
Relying on the journey, disaster is averted—
my life preserved.

**Qian**
Losing favor, blame grows,
governance collapses, the house falls.
My clan loses the state—
Qin destroys the house of Zhou.

**Yu**
Winter hides no weapons,
spring’s flourishing is blocked.
Yin’s flow turns to harm—
the land suffers its scourge.

**Sui**
Field mice and pheasants,
intending to flee.
Bound in cages and pens,
they cannot stir.

**Gu**
Adorned with jade and waterweeds,
nothing to fasten them.
Fine wine in a single cup—
none to share laughter.
Alone and solitary,
ever grieving, ever bitter.

**Lin**
Diving into the river’s mouth,
seeking a boat for a home.
The household in peril, fearing for itself—
yet the path of life revives.

**Guan**
The river’s waters surge,
ruining my home.
Deep waters, no shore—
fish and turtles overturned.

**Shike**
Teaching sheep to herd hares,
making fish hunt mice.
Entrusting the wrong man—
wasted days, no success.

**Ben**
Ascending the door, entering the chamber,
drawing near warmth and food.
The frozen northern wilds—
cold cannot steal it.

**Bo**
The ruins of solitary bamboo,
husband lost, wife gone.
Wounded by envy’s bloom,
she never sees her young bride.
Shu’s wife, Lady Tang—
the Wu clan destroyed.

*Variant: Qian’s Yang hexagram.*

**Fu**
Disaster and calamity,
ever more manifest.
Fortune cannot be mended—
three retreats, the Yi are wounded.

**Wuwang**
Holding a jade tablet,
pleading at the river.
The Duke of Zhou, swift and keen—
the young lord recovers.

**Daxu**
Flying birds and magpies rise,
illuminating the eastern sea.
The dragon descends to the courtyard,
becoming the heir of Tao Shu.
Splitting the jade scepter, the hero—
treading fortune, blessed with abundance.

*Same as Qian’s Yi hexagram.*

**Yi**
Sitting in peril till dusk,
pleading, yet nothing gained.
No dew or rain descends—
governance perverse, the people confused.

**Daguo**
Marrying a wife in Yichang,
eastern neighbors sing and dance.
Feasting and joy unfold—
great delight in Chang’an.

**Kan**
Digging a well for jade,
not the treasure of the Bian clan.
Name besmirched, body shamed—
toil with no reward.

*Variant: Meng’s Lin hexagram.*

**Li**
The magpie longs for its mate,
wishing to follow the phoenix east.
Aligning feathers and wings with principle,
it emerges at the sun’s zenith.
Yet it lingers in the northern town,
then returns to its chamber.

**Xian**
Early frost and late snow
harm the wheat and barley.
Work is ruined, strength wasted—
hunger leaves nothing to eat.
*Same as Bi’s Lü hexagram.*

**Heng**
The bat bears young,
dark and ugly by nature.
Though adorned to appear fitting,
none among the crowd will take them.

**Dun**
Departing like a flying swan,
avoiding misfortune by heading east.
Thus escaping wholly unscathed,
it meets with good fortune.

**Dazhuang**
Marriage unites in harmony,
sharing the same pillow and bond.
With joy and blessings descending,
descendants are enfeoffed as lords.

**Jin**
Chengyang at *chen-si*,
Chang’an at *xu-hai*.
Hills rise where one settles—
not the fish market’s fin.
No way to evade or block,
yet in the end, no regret or blame.

**Mingyi**
Moths become pests,
devouring my five grains.
Baskets and jars lie empty—
the household has nothing to eat.
*Same as Kun’s Ge hexagram.*

**Jiaren**
Planning and pondering,
paying homage to virtue,
they return east to our land.
With fervent feasting and laughter,
joy and fortune abound.

**Kui**
Preparing a feast to ransom the fox,
it refuses to heed my words.
Tangled in the tiger’s whiskers,
it cannot be pulled free.
*Same as Pi’s Ge hexagram.*

**Jian**
Paired fish cling wing to wing,
delighted and in accord.
Moving or resting together,
they never leave the path.

**Xie**
One finger eats the meat,
yet the mouth gains nothing.
Soiling its own cauldron,
the tongue craves what fills the belly.

**Sun**
Dragging the line by the islet’s shore,
the hook snags a great carp.
The Duke’s grandson reaps the profit,
feasting with his honored friend.

**Yi**
At Shang Zhou’s Mu Ye,
defeat came swiftly.
Heavy and frequent levies—
the common folk grieve in suffering.

**Guai**
The Purple Palace of the North Star,
where robes and caps stand at the center.
Embodying harmony and attaining virtue,
they ever receive Heaven’s blessing.
*Same as Kun’s Guan and Lü’s Xu hexagrams.*

**Ni**
Light skirmishes prize valor,
yet know not the art of war.
Bound by the enemy’s will,
the marching army flees north.

**Cui**
A gaping mouth, a wagging tongue,
divine command bids speech unfold.
Yellow Dragon, radiant star—
emerging at Virtue’s Gate.
Ascending the hall of fortune,
the realm rests in peace and prosperity.
*Same as Da You’s Gu hexagram.*

**Sheng**
A wicked son brings ruin to his line,
brandishing his sword toward the gate.
Fierce litigation rages,
startling my household.
*Alternate reading: contentious litigation.*

**Kun**
Zhu Bo’s eloquent words
serve the spirits with skill.
His prayers for ten thousand years
grant the lord long life.

**Jing**
Jades of finest craft—bi, zong, and zhang—
held in tribute before the king.
A hundred li of nurtured kin,
answering the summons of Qi and Qin.
*Same as Pi’s Song hexagram.*

**Ge**
At dawn’s first light, mounting the carriage,
treading peril, stepping into the ditch.
Skirt and rail lost,
both axles snapped.

**Ding**
Stuck fast, not yet free,
the ox remains in its pen.
How then to feast on lamb?
The household dwells in peace.

**Zhen**
Silencing the tongue, withdrawing from the world,
the virtue of benevolence never abandons.
Three sages in accord—
the House of Zhou flourishes.

**Gen**
Millet and grain, ripening crops,
hanging ripe, just forming.
Midst drought, no rain falls,
the wind withers them to husks.

**Jian**
Crowned and sashed, journeying south,
meeting fortune’s joyous hour.
Summoned on a noble quest,
raised to the rank of minister.

**Gui Mei**
One nest, nine fledglings,
sharing the same lord and mother.
Gentle, yielding, favorable, and steadfast—
entering and exiting without peril,
fortune and blessing abide.

**Feng**
The eldest daughter of the Han clan,
wed to the Eastern Sea.
Fair of face and form,
she brings harmony to the house, wealth and long life,
gaining tenfold advantage.

**Lü**
Through misfortune, fortune comes,
joy fills my halls.
Putting others before self,
every wish is fulfilled.

**Xun**
Duke Ping of Jin fell ill,
summoning a physician from Qin.
The malady proved grave—
split into two hard masses.
Hiding above the diaphragm,
lurking below the fat,
even harmony could not cure.

**Dui**
The jackal flees, the gate stands open,
great woes dissolve.
Cultivating fortune, practicing goodness,
no harm befalls the self.
*Same as Tong Ren’s Fu hexagram.*

**Huan**
Pursuing the fleeing, chasing the routed,
calling back the youngest uncle.
Arriving at last, they reclaim
their home and hearth.

**Jie**
The crow caws at the reed’s tip,
one call, three tremors.
Shaken east and west,
peril and dread unsettle—
yet no illness or harm befalls.

**Zhong Fu**
The dragon transforms into a tiger
on the southern slope of Mount Tai.
Though many follow,
none dare offer shelter.

**Xiao Guo**
A sudden gust rises,
the carriage races, wheels clattering.
Abandoning fame, chasing the lost,
losing harmony’s rhythm,
the heart grieves in sorrow.

**Ji Ji**
Dwelling by Stone Gate,
salary secures the self.
Receiving fortune from the western neighbor,
returning to drink from Jade Spring.

**Wei Ji**
Ascending the high mountain,
I speak before the king.
Pleading my grievance,
I gain office and receive grace.
*Same as Bi’s Wei Ji hexagram.*

*End of the First Scroll of the Yi Lin*

These are textual variants and editorial notes from collated editions, not a continuous passage for translation. No translation is required for this section.
