**Jie** – King Wen’s four breasts, deeply kind and filial.
He raised ten sons, none met an early end.

**Zhongfu** – Fierce wolves and jackals lurk on the mountain’s shady and sunny sides.
Stalking deer for celebration, yet the path is fraught with hidden peril.

**Xiaoguo** – Frosted leaves wither, yet one alone remains unharmed.
Driving through the gate of joy, wedded to fortune.

**Jiji** – Yellow radiance, white sun—illuminating my four lands.
A luminous leader brings the people blessings.

Below: There is a Han maiden wandering—no man may attain her.

**Weiji** – Following the wind due north, in harmony with joy.
The year ripens in abundance; the village knows no bandits.
*Variant:* A grown daughter marries—her descendants do not prosper,
rushing brings misfortune as calamity.

---

**Daguo, the Twenty-Eighth Hexagram**

**Daguo** – Canonical texts and legal writings, stored in the Orchid Terrace.
Though chaos and ruin arise, they alone escape disaster.

**Qian** – The sun in the northern quarter, yin obscures yang’s gaze.
All things lie empty, no growth is seen.

**Kun** – Ghosts weep at the altar, mourning Shang’s lack of heirs.
At dawn on the *jiazi* day, the Yin people’s sacrifices cease.

**Zhun** – Treading treacherous paths brings no advantage to action.
Sitting calmly in the hall’s eaves—only then is there no misfortune.
Doors and gates open on their own; the lord’s worries bring no prosperity.

**Meng** – Yang loses its guiding principle; withered trees revive.
Blossoming in autumn, bearing fruit in winter—the lord finds no rest.

**Xu** – The great tree’s offspring, a hundred branches share one mother.
In the sixth month of summer, its boughs and leaves flourish.
The luan and phoenix find shelter; the Duke of Shao escapes the heat.
Graceful and poised, each attains its place.
*Kui’s Kun* – In dispute, grasping iron and holding reins, provoking battle as the vanguard.
Without the marshal’s command, defeat and ruin follow.

**Shi** – Unlocking the chamber, opening the gates—inspecting and redressing grievances.
The Xia Terrace and Beautiful Prison bring joy to Tang and Wen.

**Bi** – Decline and ruin draw near; drowning in the abyss takes but a moment.
The watchdog barks in the night—household peace is shattered.

**Xiaoxu** – The western neighbor’s young daughter, betrothed to none.
Her heart like a discarded garment, she does not leave her chamber.
With no place for her thoughts, what fault lies with the matron?

**Lü** – Dogs bark in the night, treading upon ghostly necks.
The imperiled do not fall; the troubled do not come to harm.

**Tai** – A widow in her youth, dwelling alone with the orphaned.
Orioles sing, dogs bark—none dare challenge her.
My life is ill-starred, suffering cold and hardship alone.

**Pi** – A ruler without the Way—ghosts wail at his gate.
His mandate and the lands below are severed; he gains no sustenance.

**Tongren** – Riding a dragon southward, passing the wine-mound by night—
escaping peril without worry.
*Below:* A broken crossbow wounds Yi; he does not obtain his broth.

**Dayou** – A horse stumbles, the cart breaks—long tongues destroy the household.

**Shuguan** – Two and five bound as one, the Lord of Jin flees in exile.

**Qian** – Gourd blossoms bear fruit, a hundred maidens share one chamber.
The brew turns bitter, unripe—no union is made.

**Yu** – The morning phoenix spreads its patterned wings, soaring toward warmth.
The hour passes my domain—Yi gains nothing.

**Sui** – Hurried, hurried, the road sinks to the axle’s hub.
Mud clings, rain halts progress—the tiger’s hunger goes unsated.

**Gu** – A glued cart yoked in haste meets the storm.
The reins slacken, the wheel breaks—sitting alone, sorrow brings no misfortune.

**Lin** – Six houses seize power, the royal house is split asunder.
The yin restrains its yang—Tang Shu loses his sight.

**Guan** – Leaving home, fleeing to the great capital.
The fire dies, light returns—the House of Ji prospers, the Shang lose their merit.

**Shihe** – Tending sheep in the rice fields, the tiger’s roar shakes the woods.
Panting in fear, yet in the end, no disaster comes.

**Ben** – The infant seeks milk, the mother returns to her child.
The yellow fawn rejoices—at last, sweet satiety.

**Bo** – Cast out, livelihood lost, misfortune shifts to fortune—
no gain is to be had.

**Fu** – Coming and going without order, calamity strikes as disaster.
The traveler loses his ox, profit departs and does not return.
The old horse sends away its colt—do not keep it long.

**Wuwang** – The wind rages, uprooting trees; the woman, moved, falls ill.
Yang loses its season, yin’s corruption turns to treachery.

**Daxu** – Chariot and horse are stricken—no advantage lies in distant lands.
The reclusive man seeks no gain, turning from darkness toward light.

**Yi** – Three marvels, six pairs, each has its master.
*Zhou Nan* and *Shao Nan*—where the sage abides.
Virtue and righteousness flow forth, the people rejoice.

**Kan** – Seated in strife, lodging suits, clamorous and contentious.
In the end, chaos erupts—disaster befalls the lord’s house.

**Li** – Misfortune gnaws like decay, leaving me unsettled.
Following it to the southern lands eases the heart’s affliction.

**Xian** – Loving my infant girl, I pull her close—yet she slips away.
Hoping for high rank, she turns her gaze to lowly things.

**Heng** – Best to trade in the market—what you take will surely double.
Embrace your child with joy, and profit becomes your friend.

**Dun** – The seat is barely warmed when sorrow knocks at the door.
Fleeing north past the jade wall, battle rages behind me—
yet I escape the tiger’s jaws.

**Dazhuang** – The Red Emperor’s chariot hangs idle, his duties abandoned, court unkept.
The calamity of Shu Dai confines him to a drifting hut.

**Jinzi** – Fearful in Kuang, beset in Chen and Cai.
Yet bright virtue knows no peril—he emerges unharmed.

**Mingyi** – Wild geese fly south, the horse falters, the ox tires.
The fishing pond vanishes—profit lost, danger looms.
The injustice of the prison’s door is escaped without harm.

**Jiaren** – Pushing the cart up the mountain, straining upward with great toil.
From dawn to dusk, the summit remains unseen.

**Kui** – Sorrow brings no harm; fortune dwells at the hall’s gate.
The lord rests in peace.

**Jian** – Spring peaches bloom, the youngest daughter finds a home.
Blessed for many years, her son becomes lord of the land.

**Xie** – At the mountain’s peak, a thousand *li* from the earth.

Though armed bandits assail, he stands firm in defense.

**Sun** – Beyond the appointed time, months pass in vain;
The laborer grows gaunt, his strength spent.
The maiden sighs in her chamber,
Longing for her elder brothers.

**Yi** – The Grand Culmen shines anew;
Joyful, he ascends Fu Rock,
And is hailed as High Ancestor.
*Variant:* Afflicted in head and neck,
Harmony fails to thrive—
His noble name is erased.

**Guai** – Flanked by many small stars,
Three or five cluster in the east.
Journeying at dawn and dusk,
Toil yields no reward.

**Gou** – The eastern village bustles,
Laughter and words exchanged.
Zi Ban whips the ox,
But the groom stirs trouble.

**Cui** – The nose shifts to the head,
Withered reeds revive.
The low decays, the high flourishes—
The household knows no peace,
Its dwelling left unfinished.

**Sheng** – Toads gather in throngs,
Pleading for rain from heaven.
Clouds and thunder assemble,
And at once their wish is granted.

**Kun** – Striding boldly into the carriage,
He journeys south to the house of joy.
A sable robe is bestowed,
And fortune arrives in full.

**Jing** – The thief of benevolence wounds virtue;
Heaven’s wrath denies blessing.
Altars are cut down,
Their lands lost.
*Variant:* The following lines belong to **Yi**.

**Ge** – Mistaking a fox for a tiger,
Though perilous, no harm befalls—
In the end, he avoids misfortune.

**Ding** – Walking in unsullied virtue,
He is ultimately blessed with protection.
Feasting alongside Yao,
The nobleman finds rest.

**Zhen** – Profit lies in the northern wilds,
But cold and hardship make it scarce.
Amidst peril and woe,
Fortune guards the Dao’s gate—
Shang Shu survives.

**Gen** – Four lame, six blind,
Feet pained, progress halting.
From dawn to dusk,
He never leaves his neighbor’s side.

**Jian** – The terrace bequeaths a dullard son,
Yet he knows the earth’s ways.
Damming marshes, proclaiming virtue,
He is enfeoffed by the Yellow River’s banks.

**Guimei** – Store water against the hour,
To guard against flood.
Axes bind the cart, the horse is tethered—
Journeying forth on an outing,
One may yet avoid blame.

**Feng** – Flowers fall at year’s end,
The lord’s virtue wanes.
Glory and favor fade,
As the yin flees to his chamber.

**Lü** – Xia’s fall grieves Cai;
A thousand *li* become a marketplace.
Yellow leaves choke the throat—
No profit remains.

**Xun** – In mid-spring, the royal progress,
Eastward to meet the feudal lords.
Virtue long radiant,
He loses not his place.

**Dui** – Tangled in pure bonds,
The knot defies unraveling.
The toad’s mother seeks a match,
But the go-between cannot sit—
She brings ruin upon herself.
*Variant:* **Kun**’s **Jin**.

**Huan** – The crow caws at night,
A warning of disaster.
Double gates, watchmen’s clappers—
Guard against the stranger’s woe.

**Jie** – Dew at dawn, mist at dusk,
My robes grow damp,
The road bereft of oxen.

**Zhongfu** – Holding uncut jade,
He shares cups with Jie.
Slandered, his grievance unvoiced—
No worthy man remains on the path.

**Xiaoguo** – Two hearts delight in one another,
Sharing a mansion and sturgeon.
Day and night in service,
Never leaving the chamber,
They win the lord’s favor.
*Variant:* Reeds or rushes.

**Jiji** – Bearing offerings to the fields,
The sacrificial platter breaks, the meal is lost.
The seedlings fail to thrive—
Alone, he hungers through the year.

**Weiji** – Sweet dew, sweet springs,
The mechanisms of Great Peace.
Virtue’s resonance brings response—
The year is joyful, the people at ease.
*Variant:* **Tun**’s **Qian**. **Kan**’s twenty-ninth.

**Kan** – A bird with yellow feet,
Returns, calling for Young Jade.
"Follow me to Suiyang,
Where blades and spears may be avoided.
Walk with fortune,
And your fate shall be long."

**Qian** – Great King as father,
Jili filial and kind.
Wen and Wu sagely and wise,
Virtue and power arise.
Kong Zhang pacifies the four lands,
Bearing blessings, profound and secure.

**Kun** – The ape falls from the lofty tree,
Yet its hands and feet remain unscathed.
Preserving my life whole,
I return to my chamber.

**Tun** – Chong’er, reverent and keen,
Slandered, he departs.
Northward to the Rong and Di he flees,
Passing through Qi and Chu.
With Qin’s aid, he reclaims his state,
Slays Ziyu,
And becomes lord of the realm.

**Meng** – Leaning on spears, gripping halberds,
They wound my breast,
Wasting and breaking without cease.

**Xu** – Dogs donning caps, chickens strutting,
The lord loses his place.
Exiting the gate, he meets the mountain,
The traveler fears hardship.
Water floods my garden,
The highland becomes a spring.

**Song** – Where flocks of birds soar,
There lies a great monstrosity.
Towering body, long neck—
It startles and grieves me.

**Shi** – Tigers prowl, chasing one another,
No rest in sight.
Battling on the plain,
They are overthrown by the Yi.

**Bi** – Yu carves Dragon Gate,
Channeling the waters’ flow.
Together they pour into the sea,
The people dwell in peace.

**Xiaoxu** – Yao and Shun, virtuous and kind,
Nurture the worthy, bring forth fortune.
Heroes gather in multitude,
The land knows no bandits.

**Lü** – Dwelling on high ground, scarce springs,
Mountains tower, clouds absent.
A cart travels a thousand li,
The road does not soil its wheels—
Thirst becomes my lament.

**Tai** – Dawn brings no light,
Night no brightness.
Groping in the empty bed,
The youngest daughter flees,
Her heart grieving.

**Pi** – Qi and Lu stand eternal,
Virtue and sagacity aid their power.
Refining rites and elegant speech,
Duke Ding finds stability.

**Tongren** – Bundled silk and dark jade,
The lord spreads his virtue.
Yi, Lü, and Baili,
Answer the call to serve the state.

**Dayou** – Brambles hook my robes,
Entangling me in bonds.
The spirit-medium bows in prayer—
Disaster does not become calamity.
In the town by Shu Mountain,
Rich soil lies within,
Enough to sate and satisfy.

**Qian** – The gate burns, the house collapses,
Ruined by those below.
Heading west, I step beyond the door,
Following the principle’s course.

The tiger lies unmoving—
oxen and sheep rejoice.

**Yu** – Walls rise high, blocking the eye,
Kunlun’s peaks veil the sun.
Far travel brings no light—
I do not see my fearless uncle.

**Sui** – Heaven and earth converge,
no inner or outer remains.
Ancestral rites of farewell,
parting forever from the world.

**Gu** – Testing the waters, crossing proves hard,
mud clings to the wheel’s hub.
The ox tires, refusing to advance—
mired in muck, it falls ill.

**Lin** – Sheep startle at tigers and wolves,
monkeys scatter in flight.
No help for the fallen—
fanged beasts tear at them.

**Guan** – Treading on snakes, stepping on vipers,
meeting ghosts eye to eye.
Terrified, qi scatters—
as if riding a tiger’s tail.

**Shike** – The cart startles, the rider topples,
both wheels break free.
The traveler does not halt—
life fills with dread.
Knots untie, bonds dissolve—
night’s brightness brings joy.

**Ben** – Southbound traders, northbound merchants,
striking deals with joy,
profit a hundredfold.

**Bo** – Yanling journeys to Lu,
observing music with the Grand Scribe.
Bound neighbors (or rumbling chariots) and white-haired elders
foresee Qin’s rise.
In the end, it seizes all states—
one ruler unifies all.

**Fu** – Stepping out, I meet misfortune—
fortune becomes disaster.

Fighting back with blades,
they wound my fingertips.

**Wuwang** – Roe deer in herds take flight,
naturally bringing joy like swallows.
The prince loves to wander—
others reap the bounty.

**Daxu** – Reverent, generous, trustworthy, diligent,
treading fortune without peril.
Following the realm’s bounds,
they find joy in harmony.

**Yi** – Longing to fly but wingless,
the cauldron’s weight snaps its leg.
Losing its blessing and gain,
the kitchen’s feast becomes a thief.
Below, a wife from a superior house—
her steward enriches the tomb.
Joy dispels my sorrow,
unraveling my anxious thoughts.

**Daguo** – The treasury’s wealth,
the king uses to relieve the needy.
Fishing in rivers and seas,
casting nets yields great catch.

**Li** – Yin spawns stags in the grove,
mice dance, ghosts wail.
The sacred tortoise dwells on land,
dust gathers on the cauldron’s rice.
Benevolence and wisdom hesitate—
the state is in chaos, joyless.

**Xianfeng** – Dust and rough paths,
south and north lost from sight.
The journey strays from profit,
returning home instead.

**Heng** – Bronze and leather, white and yellow,
fit for wares in the martial market.
Marriage and childbirth bring growth,
merchants rejoice.

**Dun** – The virtue of the gourd,
fit to be tied, not eaten.
The noble loses his carriage,
the official harbors sorrow.

**Dazhuang** – Boarding a boat to cross,
carrying water, fire is met.
Relying on escape from peril,
I hold what sustains me.

**Jin** – The road is treacherous, strewn with stones,
damaging the cart, snapping the axle.
Hostile to the market,
no profit for the traveler’s lodging.

**Mingyi** – Those who rely on others,
find no advantage in requests.

**Jielü** – Binding and restraint, wordless,
then come regret and misfortune.

**Jiaren** – Three years contending for a wife,
chasing each other in flight.
All day without food,
essence and qi exhausted, worn out.

**Kui** – Retreat from evil, guard against calamity,
rest at midday, untie the inverted hanging.

**Jian** – Two feet, four wings,
flying into the homeland.
Reuniting with my elder sister,
mother and I find each other.

**Xie** – Chilled by cold dew,
gradually turning to solid ice.
Grasses and trees bear sores,
flowers fall, leaves perish.

**Sun** – Hou Ji’s agricultural merit,
enriches and benefits our land.
Southern fields well-tended,
one household bears a hundred children.

**Yi** – Setting nets, spreading snares,
catching fish in garden pools.
Though the nets are well-ordered,
what is gained is lost again.

**Wei** – The peril of grievance,
receives a blurred hearing.

**Guai** – Roads and counties at rest,
the lords and chiefs indulge in arrogance.
The ruler loses his authority,
the Zhou house declines.

**Gou** – Chasing the fleeing, pursuing the lost,
reaching the Fusang.
Returning to see one’s homeland,
brings me regret and sorrow.

**Cui** – Treading on blessings, securing abundance,
receiving fortune and felicity.
From decline to resurgence,
generations endure long.
Illness holds no danger,
the people of Jin send word.

**Sheng** – Widows, widowers, orphans, the solitary,
their allotted fate bitter and meager.
Entering the palace without a wife,
Wuzi returns once more in sorrow.

**Kun** – Mountains sink, hills float,
land becomes water, fish.
Swallows and sparrows have no nests,
the people no homes.

**Jing** – Crowned and sashed, journeying south,
meeting fortune and joy’s appointed time.
Roaming a fine land,
receiving rank in season.

**Ge** – Bound on a journey, losing the sheep,
missing its auspicious sign.
The youngest daughter has no match,
sits alone in an empty hut.

**Ding** – Reaching into nests to seize fish,
plowing fields to seize eels.
Wasting days without success,
the right hand empty.

**Zhen** – Bound on a journey, drinking wine,
embracing joy.
Fortune comes to our house,
profit arrives from the mother.
Those by water and marsh,
dwell gazing toward the town.

**Gen** – Rash anger loses essence,
bringing fear and regret.
With gentle caution,
the noble one incurs no blame.

**Jian** – White clouds like a sash,
coming and going in their place.
Winds flying to send and greet,
great hail soon to fall,
striking our crops,
lying dead, unable to rise.

**Guimei** – On the day of southern solstice,
yang wanes without cease.
Northern winds fierce and cold,
the myriad things hide and lie low.

**Feng** – Fire in mid-summer,
wild geese shed their nests.
Weighty, difficult to move,
unable to rise high.
The noble one’s name shines,
his reputation unlost.

**Lü** – Going north, leaving the gate,
treading, stumbling, falling.
Short legs on the path,
soiling my weak chair.

**Xun** – Light chariot, offering at the ancestral shrine,
a violent wind suddenly rises.
Hastening the sacrificial vessels,
flying, dancing in the air.
Divine brightness descends in blessing,
the Way suffers no harm from bandits.

**Dui** – Wine as the lord of joy,
dispelling sorrow, bringing delight.
Fortune and happiness enter the gate,
seeking you, sir,
making me virtuous.

**Huan** – A three-legged solitary crow,
crying vainly as the supervisor.
Punishing transgressions, chastising evil,
it harms its own house,
ruin and defeat bring sorrow.

**Jie** – The three rivers converge,
waters rage, leaping.
Breaking our royal house,
the people hunger for food.

**Zhongfu** – Journeying south to the jujube grove,
a fierce tiger fears the pattern.
Wielding fire to melt blades,
keeping me from harm.

**Xiaoguo** – Seeking deer over the mountain,
enmity arises from profit.
Deaf and mute, silent,
who knows their fear?

**Jiji** – Travelers in distress,
losing sight, keeping to their lodging.
The anxiety of confinement,
opening the door to venture out.

**Weiji** – Grasping thorns, treading brambles,
stumbling, pierced by thorns, brings sorrow.
A resolute wife discordant,
disordering our good home.

**Li, the Thirtieth**

**Li** – Riding the six dragons of the times,
as the emperor’s messenger to the east.
Conveying the mandate, declaring the decree,
nothing is not reached.

**Qian** – Holding the reins of four black steeds,
the king makes him his teacher.
The brightness of yin and yang,
receiving the east in harmony.

**Kun** – In spring and autumn, offering prayers,
dispelling misfortune, resolving sorrow,
the noble one incurs no blame.

**Zhun** – Sitting in court, riding the carriage,
governing the state, guiding the people.
Yu Shu receives the mandate,
harmonizing the six relations.

**Meng** – Opening the door, descending the hall,
welcoming fortune.
Blessings in the public chamber,
descendants prosper.

**Xu** – A high tree’s rotten nest,
leaking, damp, hard to dwell in.
Not leaving the sweet pear,
brings me no worry.

**Song** – Three women conspire,
together walking in the high garden.
Doubling back at night,
laughing with the elder brother.
Unwilling to serve the mistress,
they prepare joyful wine,
to whom can the grievance be prayed?
*This is* **Jiji**.

**Shi** – A leaking vessel holds wine,
no means to nourish the aged.
In spring, borrowing millet and grain,
the year’s harvest is truly bountiful.
Treading the Way, level and smooth,
peaceful and without blame.

**Bi** – Pine and phoenix tree’s branches and leaves,
ever lush, never falling.
The noble one dwells in tranquility,
daily growing rich, seeking joy.

**Xiaoxu** – A resolute wife discordant,
like Yan attacking Qi.
A fine bow unstrung,
Qi Jie dies in sorrow.

**Lü** – Issuing orders without success,
reversed, becomes great disaster.
The strong cannot overcome the weak,
the ruler bears the sorrow.

**Tai** – Running to and fro, crossing paths,
defeating and disordering undertakings,
the people cannot work.

**Pi** – Bearing jade tablets, holding scepters,
pleading for the mandate at the river.
The Duke of Zhou’s diligence,
the young one recovers.

**Tongren** – A plain carriage for a horse,
unable to bear heavy burdens.
The king and lords go forth to war,
danger and peril bring blame.

**Dayou** – The great tree’s children,
a hundred branches share one mother.
Amidst the fire,
branches and leaves flourish.

**Qian** – Damming and blocking the dikes,
water cannot flow.
Fire blazes, yang’s light,
yin worms hide and cower,
fleeing back to their homeland.

**Yu** – The Five Peaks and Four Rivers,
their joined moisture forms virtue.
Acting without losing principle,
the people rely on grace and fortune.

**Sui** – Driving swift steeds southward,
tigers startle my oxen.
Yin fails to serve yang,
its light diminishes.

**Zi’s** humility and modesty,
upholding righteousness to dissolve calamity.

**Gu** – Early frost and late snow,
harming the wheat and barley.
Labor lost and strength squandered,
hunger finds no sustenance.

**Lin** – At Qizhou’s seaside corner,
alone in joy without sorrow.
One may evade disaster,
preserving life and wealth.

**Guan** – Yin obscures its yang,
the day darkens, unbright.
The lord worries for his state,
seeking refuge, he obtains a yellow colt,
with calves following behind.

**Shihe** – A city of gold, walls of iron,
united in strength above and below.
Governance is level, the people rejoice,
bandits dare not plunder.

**Bi** – Duke Ping fell ill,
summoning physicians from Qin.
He could not be cured,
the people of Jin grew suspicious.

**Bo** – Carrying Yao, supporting Yu,
following Qiao and Pengzu.
In the west, they met the Queen Mother,
the road was smooth and easy,
none dared oppose them.

**Fu** – Lambskin and leather,
the lord’s court robes.
Assisting governance with heavenly virtue,
to unite the myriad states.

**Wuwang** – Striking bells and drums in joy,
the general receives fortune.
A household at peace,
yet tigers and wolves bring worry.
Treading peril without danger,
what fault lies in the army’s march?

**Daxu** – Legitimate and base unclear,
calamity arises from disorder,
Chen loses its state.

**Yi** – The crow startles the lone swan goose,
the state is unsettled.
The strong below, the weak above,
punished by yin.

**Daguo** – Wearing embroidered robes by night,
no pattern is seen.
Sitting calmly in the hall,
then no misfortune befalls.
The eldest son leads the army,
attaining proper order.

**Kan** – In the sixth month, gathering *ba*,
punishing the lawless.
Zhang Zhong and Fang Shu,
triumphant, they drink wine.

**Xian** – At dusk, riding a carriage,
bound for Uncle Bo’s home.
Crossing the bridge over the river,
safely delivered, no harm.

**Huan** – The east wind melts the frost,
harmonious qi begins to rise,
the year’s harvest is abundant.

**You** – Three wildcats seize the rat,
blocking front and rear.
They die in the walled garden,
unable to escape.

**Da** – Cultivating virtue with great clarity,
treading fortune for long years.
Noble and radiant,
yet illness and sorrow linger.

**Jin** – Two tigers grapple with a wolf,
their strength unequal.
Like crushing rotten wood,
one strike shatters them.

**Mingyi** – Sending Bo to gather mulberry,
the servant refuses to go.
Quarreling with Shu in litigation,
they slander and wound each other.

**Jiaren** – Embracing emptiness, grasping at void,
the magpie startles my crane,
profit departs and does not return.

**Kui** – The plum tree blossoms again,
swan eggs descend and gather.
With benevolence and wisdom,
the state prospers, unharmed.

**Jian** – On the eastern mountain’s slopes,
fierce and unyielding.
Jade ornaments and fine treasures,
clothing becomes the body’s bane,
silk and hemp remain unspun.

**Xie** – Flying gnats soil the body,
led astray by evil.
The green oyster divides the white,
true filial piety is exiled.

**Sun** – The great tree of the southern mountain,
eight fathoms tall, six eyes.
Issuing commands, producing texts,
proclaiming and spreading through the eastern villages.

**Revering the Lord, dwelling in peace,**
the State of Zheng knows no peril.

**Yi** – Springs rise from Kunlun,
flowing west through Jade Gate.
Branching into the Nine Rivers,
no sorrow or suffering remains.

**Guài** – Life is brief, not long,
cut short in middle age.
Thoughts turn to the mourning hall,
once returned, the son is gone.

**Gòu** – Lord and minister discord,
high and low misaligned,
the heir laments in song.

**Cuì** – Harsh governance daily grows,
moths devour the blossoms and leaves.
The low consume the high,
the people suffer its harm,
autumn yields no harvest.

**Shēng** – Journeying south with a mirror in tow,
ascending the nine stars of Kui.
The cart breaks, the ox falters,
day’s end brings sighs of woe.

**Kùn** – Spring east, summer south,
following the sun brings merit,
encountering fortune along the way.

**Jǐng** – Head and tail inverted,
the thread’s end lost,
the lord forfeits his state.

**Gé** – Words lack binding force,
no contract is sealed.
Yin Shu and Ji Ji,
Gongsun contend for it.
Forcibly entering to present the betrothal gifts,
Zinan is displeased.

**Dǐng** – Flawed and incomplete,
embryos and eggs fail to form,
their shape unseen.

**Zhèn** – Seeing serpents entwined,
regretting the dragonfly’s dread,
the heart feels no remorse.

**Gèn** – The river’s holes and crevices
ruin my chamber.
The waters run deep without bound,
fish and turtles topple over.

**Jiàn** – The Five Peaks and Four Marshes,
the earth finds stability.
Lofty yet not perilous,
fear and caution avert disaster.

**Guīmèi** – When the southern solstice arrives,
yang wanes without cease.
The north wind bites with cold,
all things hide and lie low.

**Fēng** – Five blessings, four fortunes,
all till the high fields.
Millet and sorghum flourish,
abundant harvests of fine rice.

**Lǚ** – The grandson rides his chariot,
traveling east to Qi.
Yanling’s Zichan,
bestows upon me a patterned robe,
grief and sorrow in sickness.

**Xùn** – Flood dragons block the path,
the people suffer in distress.
Gazing at the flock, setting snares,
the eldest son stands at the gate.

**Duì** – Gold and jade fill the hall,
the loyal and upright face peril.
The Three Elders freeze and starve,
ghosts seize my home.
Seeking fish in rivers and seas,
cast the net and you shall catch.

**Huàn** – The sun sets, brightness concealed,
yang’s radiance hides away.
The petty man’s heart toils,
seeking affairs yet gaining nothing.

**Jié** – Frequently invited to drink,
losing profit, later fortune.
Better to tend to one’s children,
than old vessels and worn pestles.
If the request is made properly,
success comes without great regret.

**Zhōngfú** – In the south, fine fish abound,
riding yellow steeds to roam.
Silken robes fluttering,
profit comes without worry.

**Xiǎoguò** – Yellow skirts establish the era,
cultured virtue resides within.
Blessings and protection overflow,
enfeoffed as Lord of Qi.

**Jìjì** – The mouth does not follow the heart,
desiring east, turning west.
At odds with intent,
every move loses its advantage.

**Wèijì** – In the land of tigers and wolves,
daily quarrels and violent disputes.
Though you claim seniority,
you cannot settle the case.

*The Upper Canon of the Forest of Changes, Scroll Five, concludes.*

*(This section consists of textual collation notes rather than continuous Classical Chinese text for translation. As such, it does not require a literary or interpretive translation. Below is a direct rendering of the editorial apparatus in a scholarly format, preserving the original structure and intent.)*

---

**Textual Variants and Emendations**

1. Under *Yuè* (悦), the character "一" is superfluous; deleted following *Baizi Quanshu*.
2. Originally written as *chūn* (春); emended to *chōng* (舂) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
3. *Zhǔ* (主); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *wáng* (王).
4. Originally written as *sū* (稣); emended to *qiū* (秋) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
5. Originally written as *wéi* (惟); emended to *jù* (惧) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
6. Originally written as *táo* (逃); emended to *yíng* (迎) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
7. Originally written as *yǒu* (有); emended to *xún* (循) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
8. Originally written as *zhēn* (真); emended to *zhēn* (贞) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
9. Originally written as *wū* (乌); retained as *wū* (乌) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
10. *Zhèn* (振); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *rǎng* (攘), though *lǚ* (旅) may be more appropriate.
11. Originally written as *nán wéi* (难为); emended to *suī wēi* (虽危) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
12. Originally written as *xián* (咸); emended to *zāng* (臧) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
13. Originally written as *qiān* (千); emended to *shí* (十) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
   - Below, the character *lí* (骊) was originally written as *xiāng* (瓖); emended following *Baizi Quanshu*.
14. Under *jūn* (君), the phrase *bēn zǒu táo* (奔走逃) is missing; supplied following *Baizi Quanshu*.
15. Under *shī* (师), the phrase *bēn zǒu táo* (奔走逃) is superfluous; deleted following *Baizi Quanshu*.
16. Under *hūn* (婚), one edition includes *zhèn yī tán guān* (振衣弹冠).
17. Under *jī* (机), the repetition *jī fā* (机发) is superfluous; deleted following *Baizi Quanshu*.
18. Under *jiāng* (僵), the phrase *dùn zhì* (遁至) is missing; supplied following *Baizi Quanshu*.
19. Originally written as *què* (鹊); emended to *yào* (鹞) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
20. *Shū diān* (疏颠); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *diē dùn* (跌顿).
21. Originally written as *jiù* (厩); emended to *bān* (瘢) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
22. *Zhǔ* (主); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *wáng* (王).
23. Originally written as *qiú* (求); emended to *lái* (来) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
24. *Fāng kǒu* (方口); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *kǒu fāng* (口方).
25. *Xī rèn* (锡刃); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *qiān dāo* (铅刀).
26. *Qí* (岐); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yōu* (攸).
27. Originally written as *cāo* (操); emended to *zào* (噪) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
28. *Liǎn fāng* (脸方); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *kǔ* (苦).
29. *Zhì* (志); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *zhì* (智).
30. *Shàng* (上); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *shān* (山).
31. *Lì* (莅); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yí* (疑).
   - In the *Wèijì* forest verses, *gǔ* (股) and *qiě* (且) were originally written as *yīn* (殷) and *jù* (具); emended following *Baizi Quanshu*.
32. Originally written as *xiǎo* (小); emended to *dà* (大) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
33. *Jī bó* (姬伯); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *Jī zǐ* (箕子).
34. *Xī* (烯); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *shì* (饰).
35. Originally written as *gàn* (淦); emended to *tú* (涂) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
36. *Zé* (泽); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *dàn* (惮).
37. Originally written as *fú* (伏); emended to *xiāng* (相) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
38. *Biàn* (便); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yí* (宜).
39. Originally written as *yù* (遇); emended to *guò* (过) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
40. Under *yǐ* (已), the variant *wèi yī zuò huì* (畏一作晦) is missing; supplied following *Baizi Quanshu*.
41. Originally written as *fèn* (愤); emended to *fèn* (愤) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
42. Originally written as *shēng bù sī* (生布思); emended to *zhǔ bù ēn* (主布恩) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
43. *Yí* (宜); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *zhí* (直).
44. *Dù* (杜); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *lín* (林).
45. Under *yōng* (壅), *Baizi Quanshu* includes the variant *lín yī zuò dù* (林一本作杜).
46. Originally written as *tǔ* (土); emended to *shàng* (上) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
47. Originally written as *yǎn* (眼); emended to *fú* (服) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
48. Originally written as *cuàn* (篡); emended to *qín* (秦) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
49. *Chèn* (趁); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *qū* (趋).
50. Originally written as *rǎng* (攘); emended to *ráng* (禳) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
51. Originally written as *zhōng* (忠); emended to *huàn* (患) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
52. Originally written as *zài* (在); emended to *zhèng* (正) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
53. Originally written as *yǐn* (饮); emended to *yōu* (攸) following *Baizi Quanshu*.

---

54. *Yù* (郁) originally written as *pō* (颇); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *ér* (儿).
55. *Xiōng* (凶); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *xù* (酗).
56. *Yù* (郁) originally written as *kāi* (开); emended to *dòu* (斗) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
57. *Cān* (参) originally written as *qiē* (切); emended to *shū* (叔) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
58. *Yù* (郁) originally written as *dé* (得); emended to *dé* (德) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
59. Originally written as *jiàn* (见); emended to *sī* (思) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
60. *Chóu* (仇); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yàng* (样).
61. Originally written as *táng* (糖); emended to *kāng* (糠) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
62. Originally repeated *shuāi* (衰) below; superfluous character deleted following *Baizi Quanshu*.
63. Originally written as *cuì* (翠); emended to *cuì* (萃) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
64. *Quǎn* (犬); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *bù* (不).
65. *Miàn* (面) originally written as *fèi* (吠); emended to *fèi* (吠) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
66. Originally written as *chéng* (承); emended to *shǐ* (豕) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
67. Originally written as *xiū* (休); emended to *tǐ* (体) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
68. Originally written as *chē* (车); emended to *lián* (连) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
69. Originally written as *jīng* (经); emended to *suī* (虽) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
70. Originally written as *yuē* (约); emended to *diào* (钓) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
71. Originally written as *dài* (待); emended to *fù* (傅) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
72. Originally written as *dòng* (动); emended to *hé* (何) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
73. Originally written as *fàn* (饭); emended to *yǐn* (饮) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
74. Originally, no appended text followed *huàn* (患) [now includes: "举事不遁，商旅作愤"].
75. Originally omitted *xiōng* (凶) below *yōu* (忧); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
76. *Shí* (食); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *sūn* (飧).
77. Originally written as *hàn* (汗); emended to *wū* (污) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
78. Originally written as *lìn* (吝); emended to *gè* (各) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
79. Originally omitted *gāo fēi míng míng* (高飞冥冥) below *guó* (国); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
80. *Wú* (吾); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *wǒ* (我).
81. Originally written as *qíng* (情); emended to *fèn* (愤) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
82. Originally omitted *xíng* (行) below *yè* (夜); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
83. Originally written as *xī* (西); emended to *zhú* (逐) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
84. *Yù* (郁) originally written as *rén* (人); emended to *yíng* (盈) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
85. *Wǒ* (我); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *dé* (得).
86. Originally written as *xī* (夕); emended to *duō* (多) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
87. Originally written as *wáng* (王); emended to *yù* (玉) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
88. Originally written as *zé* (则); emended to *yuè* (刖) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
89. Originally written as *yíng* (赢); emended to *léi* (赢) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
90. Originally written as *xiǎn* (鲜); emended to *jiě* (解) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
91. Originally written as *ráng* (攘); emended to *nóng* (秾) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
92. Originally written as *fèn* (愤); emended to *zhài* (债) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
93. *Cān* (参) originally written as *xǐ* (喜); emended to *gū* (辜) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
94. *Shuì* (睡) originally written as *shuō* (说); emended to *chán* (谗) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
95. *Bó* (亳); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *háo* (毫).
96. *Guāng* (光); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *nǚ* (女).
97. *Shǐ* (始); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *rú* (如).
98. *Cháng* (场); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *qiáng* (墙).
99. Originally written as *tā* (他); emended to *guó* (国) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
100. Originally written as *yīng* (英); emended to *yīn* (阴) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
101. Originally written as *wén* (闻); emended to *mén* (门) following *Baizi Quanshu*. Below, *zhī* (织); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *chì* (炽).
102. Originally omitted *cái bǎo dí guó* (财宝敌国) below *dé* (得); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
103. Originally written as *liáng* (梁); emended to *liáng* (粱) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
104. Originally written as *qiú* (囚); emended to *kùn* (困) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
105. *Fēn* (分); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *fāng* (方).
106. Originally written as *mìng* (命); emended to *qín* (禽) following *Baizi Quanshu*.

107. Originally written as *fèn* (愤); emended to *jì* (绩) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
108. Originally written as *zhì* (至); emended to *shì* (室) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
109. *Wū* (乌); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *wū* (乌), but *mù* (目) is deemed correct.
110. Originally written as *xù* (绪); emended to *xù* (绪) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
111. Originally written as *miǎn gōng* (免功); emended to *jiàn gōng* (见公) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
112. Originally written as *shì* (士); emended to *wáng* (王) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
113. Originally written as *xì* (系); emended to *bēn* (奔) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
114. Originally written as *yìng* (映); emended to *yāng* (殃) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
115. *Shāng* (商); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *shāng* (伤).
116. Originally written as *yè* (叶); emended to *huá* (华) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
117. Originally written as *shī* (失); emended to *xī* (息) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
118. Originally written as *huá* (华); emended to *yè* (叶) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
119. Originally omitted *suǒ* (所) below with the appended text *kuí zhī kùn* (睽之困); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
120. Originally written as *bān* (般); emended to *shòu* (受) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
121. Originally written as *xiàng quǎn* (项犬); emended to *qǐng fèi* (顷吠) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
122. Originally written as *chuán* (传); emended to *fù* (傅) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
123. Originally written as *céng* (曾); emended to *wú* (无) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
124. Originally written as *zuò* (作); emended to *xià* (下) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
125. Originally written as *lǔ* (鲁); emended to *nǔ* (弩) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
126. *Huā* (花); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *huá* (华).
127. *Wǎnwǎn* (晚晚); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *cáicái* (才才).
128. *Chén* (沉); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *ní* (泥).
129. *Yǔ* (雨); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *mǎ* (马).
130. *Bùdé* (不得); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *wǒ zú* (我足).
131. Originally written as *shì* (筮); emended to *shì* (噬) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
132. Originally written as *dēng* (蹬); emended to *kuà* (跨) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
133. *Biàn* (变); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *dù* (度).
134. Originally written as *ruò* (若); emended to *lǎo* (老) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
135. *Qiǎn* (遣); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yí* (遗).
Below, in the *Wuwang* Hexagram Forest Rhymes, the character *piāo* (漂) was originally written as *lì* (溧); emended following *Baizi Quanshu*.
136. Originally written as *xúnxún* (询询); emended to *xúnxún tǎng* (询询倘) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
137. Originally written as *yuè* (月); emended to *mù* (目) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
138. Originally written as *shì* (市); emended to *yǒu* (友) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
139. Originally written as *jí zǐ jì* (极子记); emended to *jū yú fàn* (居于泛) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
140. Originally written as *kùn* (困); emended to *è* (厄) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
141. Originally written as *tiǎo* (窕), a variant of *táo* (桃); emended following *Baizi Quanshu*.
142. Originally written as *tóu* (头); emended to *jǐng* (颈) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
143. Originally written as *gé* (革); emended to *luò* (荦) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
144. Originally omitted *yǔ* (宇) below with the appended text *yī běn cǐ xià jiē yí zhī* (一本此下皆颐之); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
145. *Huā* (花); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *huá* (华).
146. Originally written as *xuàn* (渲); emended to *dèng* (瞪) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
147. *Liè* (洌); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *lì* (例).
148. *Léiléi* (缧缧); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *lěilěi* (累累).
149. Originally omitted *huò* (祸) below with the appended text *kūn zhī jìn* (坤之晋); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
150. Originally written as *lū* (噜); emended to *wū* (呜) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
151. *Xíng* (行); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *zhōng* (中).
152. Originally written as *chāi* (拆); emended to *tuò* (柝) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
153. *Chǎng shāng* (厂觞); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *xiāng chù* (相触).
154. *Rú* (茹); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yuàn* (苑).
155. *Rú* (茹); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yuàn* (苑).
156. Originally written as *rú* (茹); emended to *rú* (如) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
157. Originally written as *jī* (机); emended to *jī* (饥) following *Baizi Quanshu*.

158. Originally written as *ǎi* (矮); emended to *qī* (蹊) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
159. Originally written as *bǐ* (比); emended to *běi* (北) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
160. Originally written as *hào* (耗); unchanged as *hào* (耗) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
161. Originally written as *yòu* (佑); emended to *guài* (怪) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
162. Originally written as *huì* (烩); emended to *chuàng* (怆) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
163. Originally written as *yāo* (要); emended to *ān* (安) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
164. Originally written as *fú* (浮); emended to *nìng* (泞) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
165. Originally written as *hóu* (侯); emended to *hóu* (猴) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
166. Originally written as *gè* (各); emended to *duō* (多) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
167. Originally written as *jiā xìng* (家幸); emended to *zhǒng zǎi* (冢宰) following *Baizi Quanshu*. In the *Daguo* hexagram forest phrases below, *bù* (布) is written as *gǔ* (罟) in *Baizi Quanshu*.
168. *Chí* (持); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *shì* (恃).
169. Originally written as *zhì* (置); emended to *jìn* (晋) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
170. The hexagram name was originally omitted before *tuì* (退); supplemented with *kuí* (睽) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
171. *Jiā* (家); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *jiā* (嘉).
172. *Zhì* (致); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *zhì* (至).
173. *Huā* (花); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *huá* (华).
174. Originally written as *mǒu* (某); emended to *yè* (叶) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
175. Originally written as *xǔ* (许); emended to *sù* (诉) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
176. Originally written as *niǔ* (忸); emended to *qí* (圻) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
177. Originally written as *zhǐ* (旨); emended to *jìn* (晋) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
178. Originally written as *qǐ* (起); emended to *fù* (赴) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
179. Originally written as *fǔ zhào* (辅照); emended to *bǔ yú* (捕鱼) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
180. Originally written as *cháng* (长); emended to *zhèn* (震) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
181. Originally, another *suǒ* (索) was written below *suǒ* (索), a redundancy; deleted following *Baizi Quanshu*.
182. *Dé* (德); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *dé* (得). In the *Huan* hexagram forest phrases below, *wū* (乌) was originally written as *wū* (乌); emended following *Baizi Quanshu*.
183. *Chú* (除); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *jiě* (解).
184. *Jiě* (解); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *chú* (除).
185. Originally written as *mén* (门); emended to *kāi* (开) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
186. *Rěn* (忍); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *rèn* (认).
187. Originally written as *wú* (无); emended to *shè* (设) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
188. Originally, no appended text *lǚ zhī jì jì* (履之既济) was written below *táo* (梼); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
189. Originally written as *zhū xù* (诸绪); emended to *xù yè* (绪业) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
190. Originally written as *běi* (北); emended to *bǐ* (比) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
191. *Miè cù* (灭蹴); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *xiǎn yáng* (显扬).
192. *Zī* (訾); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yán* (言).
193. *Rì* (日); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *mù* (目).
194. *Kuò* (廓); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *guō* (郭), and *guō* (郭) is deemed correct.
195. *Cóng* (从); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *sōng* (松).
196. Originally written as *xíng* (行); emended to *xíng* (刑) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
197. Originally, no appended text *yī běn zuò lí zhī kǎn* (一本作离之坎) was written below *cháng* (常); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
198. Originally written as *lái* (来); emended to *cǎi* (采) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
199. Originally, no appended text *yī běn zuò lí zhī dà guò* (一本作离之大过) was written below *jiǔ* (酒); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
200. Originally written as *lái* (来); emended to *shǐ* (使) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
201. Originally written as *láng* (狼); emended to *hěn* (狠) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
202. Originally, *lái* (来) was omitted below *bù* (不); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
203. *Huā* (花); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *huá* (华).
204. Originally written as *mǎo* (卯); emended to *luǎn* (卵) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
205. *Yīng* (瑛); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *jué* (决).
206. Originally, the text *dòu sī má bù zuò* (斗丝麻不作) was omitted below *zéi* (贼); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.

207. Originally written as *jué* (崛); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *yíng* (蝇).
208. *Xī* (西); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *dōng* (东).
209. Originally written as *mìng* (命); emended to *qín* (禽) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
210. Originally written as *yú shì* (於是); emended to *bù yuè* (不悦) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
211. Originally written as *zhǐ mǎo* (指卯); emended to *tāi luǎn* (胎卵) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
212. Originally, *huǐ* (悔) had no appended text *wù yī běn zuò wài* (悟一本作外); supplemented following *Baizi Quanshu*.
213. Originally written as *rǒng* (冗); emended to *xué* (穴) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
214. Originally written as *shì* (是); emended to *yán* (延) following *Baizi Quanshu*.
215. *Qì* (器); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *jū* (居).
216. *Chǔ* (杵); *Baizi Quanshu* reads *chǔ* (处).

*Yilin*, Scroll 1 (Lower), Hexagram *Xian* (咸), Thirty-First.

**Hexagram Xian (咸), Thirty-First**

**Xian (咸):**
A lone hen dwells apart, returning to her native nest.
Her feathers wither, her spirit like dead ash.

**Qian (乾):**
A small window shines bright; roads and paths are open and smooth.
With benevolent and worthy gentlemen, the state stands firm, unshaken.

**Kun (坤):**
The heart loathes strange omens—why fear their surging might?
Yan Yuan and Zi Qian, taught by the Sage, the Master’s holy instruction.

**Zhun (屯):**
The crow cries, "O child! I feed you with wine and dried meat."
Perched high by the water, she returns to her mother.

**Meng (蒙):**
The state’s horse grows horns; yin’s evil sprouts and stirs.
Changing into common garb, the lord loses his dwelling.

**Xu (需):**
Entering the eaves, much regret—plowing stone brings no wealth.
The crossbeam gate stands empty again and again, leaving scholars disheartened.

**Song (讼):**
Young lads travel as merchants, crossing distant mountains and perilous passes.
Trading with fellow travelers, they face no danger nor harm.
Profit yields tenfold gain.

**Shi (师):**
The beam breaks, the bridge collapses; the waters run deep, filled with dread.
Between Chen and Zheng, passage is cut off—no way to advance.

**Bi (比):**
Two phoenixes take flight, returning to the rice pond.
Crossing the marsh of Qu, they are struck by arrows, wounding my breast and heart.

**Xiao Xu (小畜):**
Slobbering and drooling, words fail; the beam’s pattern fades away.
A man bargains for an ox—three men contend for it.
Losing profit and missing the hour, Lord Sun is filled with sorrow.

**Lǚ (履):**
Famine and calamity strike the southern land; the people eat chaff and husks.
The youngest son, trapped in capture, gains no advantage.
A dog barks at its non-master; wolves and tigers stir at night.
Startling me east and west, they bring no misfortune to the household.

**Pi (否):**
Gazing at the dragon, it has no eyes—hands and feet unseen.
Diving into water to seek jade, one loses what was desired.

**Tong Ren (同人):**
Taking a deer for a horse, deceiving and misleading their lord.
Hearing the words yet disbelieving, three mouths bring blame.
The three sons of the Yellow Dragon dwell in joy, free from peril.

**Da You (大有):**
Raising the young, newly wed, they cannot yet leave the home.
Climbing Song to gaze at Qi, they do not see the Grand Preceptor.

**Qian (谦):**
The king’s grandson, a noble child, befriends one another.
Bright, trustworthy, sincere, and honest, they rise through recommendation.

**Yu (豫):**
Mountains and rivers rage in fury, breaking beams and snapping pillars.
Travelers delay, lingering in sorrow and distress.

**Sui (随):**
The turtledove moves its nest, west to Pingzhou.
Struck by thunder and lightning, my reed hut is shattered.
The household suffers hunger and cold, longing for our former home.

**Gu (蛊):**
Ascending high, the axle breaks; climbing the slope, grain is abandoned.
Selling grain brings no profit; the merchant suffers loss.

**Lin (临):**
The hunchbacked Lord Sun serves ghosts and spirits with skill.
Practicing frugality and soothing the people, he preserves the state,
Living long, blessed with many years.

**Guan (观):**
Nine li, ten mountains—the path is steep and treacherous.
Oxen and horses will not advance; they turn back and return.

**Shi He (噬嗑):**
A withered tree bears no blossoms; the empty abyss holds no fish.
The old crow takes flight, abandoning me for profit.

**Bi (贲):**
The male fox climbs the mountain, cautious and slow.
Proclaiming his merit, great fortune truly arises.

**Bo (剥):**
Laughter and joyful words, drinking wine with delight.
Passing the cup in endless merriment, dancing for a thousand autumns,
They bow to receive great fortune.

**Fu (复):**
A great cart’s hub breaks; a long tongue sows chaos in the state.
Words from the bedchamber bring three generations of unrest.

**Jian (蹇):**
Man and woman share a chamber; three clans eat together.
Marriage and kinship flourish, blessing my filial descendants.

**Da Xu (大畜):**
A wall a thousand *ren* high bars the gate—no way to enter.
A golden cage refines the coarse; it is profitable to evade war.
Wishing to ascend the southern slope, the axle fails to turn—
The cart returns, retracing its path.

**Yi (颐):**
Flowery words and windy speech mislead one another.
Yet no misfortune arises; peace and tranquility remain as before.

**Da Guo (大过):**
A drifting boat adrift, flowing without cease.
Deep in waking and sleep, the lord harbors great sorrow.
Virtue unmet by the times, he retreats to a secluded abode.

**Kan (坎):**
A large tail, a small head—too heavy to sway.
The upper is weak, the lower strong; yin restrains the yang.

**Li (离):**
One body, three mouths—speech without direction.
East, west, south, north—bewildered, the path is lost.

**Heng (恒):**
Journeying south in search of fortune, one meets with joy.
Enfeoffed and rewarded, they stand as a tripod, aiding the state.

**Zhu (逐):**
Missing the time to return, male and female grieve in sorrow.
Wandering in foreign lands, parted from their mother.

**Da Zhuang (大壮):**
Yao and Shun rule the state; yin and yang harmonize in virtue.
Zhuo gathers robes and garments—the people of Jin suffer no harm.

**Jin (晋):**
The glory of the Duke of Zhou; Yue and Shang barbarians come to pay tribute.
Sickness and many curses—ghosts wail for their lord.
The wolf cub’s wild heart; the guest and host do not agree.

**Ming Yi (明夷):**
In *shen* and *you*, garments are cast off; oxen and horses rest.
The nobleman is at ease; the laborer finds respite.

**Jia Ren (家人):**
The southern wind lacks a mother—what can one rely on?
The young orphan, a weak child, is tormented by others.

**Kui (睽):**
Stepping out the door, entering the hall, then leisurely to the windowed chamber.
Not losing their constancy, heaven’s prison and earth’s gate—
The laborer endures hardship.

**Xian (蹇):**
Heaven rejects Zhou’s virtue, bestowing its mandate on the southern state.
With ritual, the people are pacified; weapons and grasses rest.

**Jie (解):**
The hall’s mulberry snaps its crossbeam; those aiding the fight are wounded.
The violent minister harms the state; the good minister suffers calamity.

**Sun (损):**
A land of harmonious joy, my fortune is blessed.
From the eastern peaks to the western mountains, morning and evening, grace is complete.

**Yi (益):**
Plowing stone yields no growth; abandoning ritual brings no renown.
Sewing a robe, the needle is lost; the cowardly and weak achieve nothing.

**Guai (夬):**
Deaf and blind, lame and leaning—unable to walk.
Sitting corpses vie for bones; the body suffers great disaster,
Beset by many sorrows.

**Gou (姤):**
Born in great peace, benevolent governance flows far.
The four directions return to virtue; the altars of soil and grain thrive in glory.

**Cui (萃):**
Jie and Zhou dwell together; the people grieve in sorrow.
Wielding weapons and shouldering spears, they battle in Qi and Lu.
United in settlement, they secure stability—Ji and Jiang dwell as one.

**Sheng (升):**
Southward, misfortune accompanies; the broken cart loses its axle.

**Westward journey brings no boast, losing its treasured bribes.**

**Kun (困):**
An empty trough pours into vessels, yet no suckling pig arrives.
Drawing the bow to curse the chicken, the rooster flees in flight.

**Jing (井):**
Gazing toward Shang’s pillar, the Grand Steward, the Duke of Zhou.
A screen for Tang and Wu, enfeoffed as lord and marquis.

**Ge (革):**
The land of Chaoxian, where Lord Ji found refuge.
A home blessed for its people, its legacy enduring for descendants.

**Ding (鼎):**
Worries dispelled, laughter restored—once poor, now rich.
Bearing joy and treading goodness, sharing in fortune’s favor.

**Zhen (震):**
Younger brother welcomes elder brother, meeting in the lane beneath the sun.
The lord’s lady and Ji sit together, striking up the zither’s song.

**Gen (艮):**
Riding the wind to set fire, mugwort and orchids perish together.
The Three Offices gather in the chamber, a thousand sons struck down.

**Jian (漸):**
Driving eight li in a chariot, seeking fresh bream and carp.
Not my shop’s place—yet I lose the market myself.
Where the noble one dwells, safety knows no peril.

**Guimei (归妹):**
Drawing the sword, wounding the hand—facing the foe, unable to rise.
A loyal minister lacks support, beset by hardship and shame.

**Feng (丰):**
At the wayward lord’s gate, aiding the fight, heels are wounded.
Pursuing private gain, impoverishing office—his body bears ruin’s mark.
Bound beneath Mount Tai, I meet my beloved.

**Lǚ (旅):**
A loving mother awaits her son, her longing endless.
A guest long abroad in the wilds, her heart aches with sorrow.

**Xun (巽):**
Spinning hemp from Huai’s banks, one twist becomes a hundred.
Circulating through the four seas, free from harm or ill.

**Dui (兑):**
Sweet dew and sweet springs, the great peace’s mechanism.
Virtue’s resonance brings response—years of joy, the people at ease.

**Huan (涣):**
Gathering fern and setting out, the fish’s array recalls its beginning.
High and low pressed by labor, the noble one escapes care.

**Jie (节):**
Pigs birth fish and silk, mice dance in the hall.
The fierce and flattering spread poison—above and below sink into chaos.
The lord loses his state.

**Zhongfu (中孚):**
Three heads, six eyes—the Way fears perilous lodging.
In a land of cold and hardship, no profit is to be found.

**Xiaoguo (小过):**
Swallows and sparrows carry thatch, nurturing their brood.
Six brothers in harmony, revering filial piety and fraternal love.
Each shares the same wish, delighting in one another’s joy.

**Jiji (既济):**
The cultured lord’s virtue and justice, the sagely bring forth fortune.
No miscarriages or early deaths, the state rich, the people full.
Outsiders dwell within the chamber, multiplying gains ever more.

**Weiji (未济):**
The autumn bridge unfinished, no way to reach Chen.
Waters deep and hard to ford, they bar my advance.

---

**Heng (恒), the Thirty-Second:**
Born of the Yellow Emperor, dwelling in Fuxi’s realm.
No blades reach here—profit lies in settling.

**Qian (乾):**
Ascending the hall, feet stumble—southward journey breaks the axle.
The chamber of endless night meets no loyal or upright soul.

**Kun (坤):**
Swallows and sparrows grow old, their sad cries enter the sea.
Grief in neglect, their feathers askew.
Jostling above and below, they dwell alone in meager station.

**Zhun (屯):**
The inner gate lifts sorrow—my elder brother comes from without.
Pressing cares dissolve, my heart finds joy.

**Meng (蒙):**
Plowing the outskirts, choosing the plowshare—yet hesitation remains.
Empty and childless, benighted and unknowing.

**Xu (需):**
Baring fangs and gnashing teeth, glaring in fury.
Calamity rises within the walls, dragging my son into it.
The trouble cannot be undone, my mother’s alarm and sorrow.

**Song (讼):**
Shoes too tight for feet, the southern mountain thick with thorns.
The mother (or: *Do not*) leaves the chamber—then no illness befalls.

**Shi (师):**
The ox parts with its calf, lowing in the wilds.
The Lady Shen’s influence wanes, returning to her mother.

**Bi (比):**
A dragon born in the deep, rising to heaven on the wind.
The striped tiger’s patterns gleam, as prey is brought low in the chariot.
Emerging from the warm valley, resting at dusk on Kunlun.
A lifetime without peril, its radiant essence shines—unscathed by hardship.

**Xiaoxu (小畜):**
Married and settled, auspicious—coming and going without blame.
Three sages dwell together, the state secure and free from disaster.

**Lǚ (履):**
The northern land lies in yang’s concealment, black and white unknown.
The noble one grieves slander, the upright harmed by the good.

**Tai (泰):**
One body, two heads—reaching toward two households, chaos beyond cure.

**Pi (否):**
A mare and a colt, breeding endlessly.
The noble one’s robes and garments profit from abundance.

**Tongren (同人):**
Southward journey brings sorrow, the golden chariot breaks.
Sitting in the old dwelling, no misfortune or harm befalls.

**Dayou (大有):**
The troubled man’s affliction, treading wounds the floating face.
For the body’s ruin, steadfastly guard the heart—embracing joy as one.

**Qian (谦):**
Chengyang’s Chen and Si, Chang’an’s Xu and Hai.
Hills and mounds stir the heart—not the market for fish and eels.
Here one may escape the flood, ending without misfortune.

**Yu (豫):**
I know not whose grandson comes knocking at night.
I guard against external foes—arms and warfare draw near.

**Sui (随):**
Dawn dims, unbright—the sun darkens, without light.
Ruin and loss of constancy grieve my heart.

**Gu (蛊):**
By the Yangtze’s yin banks, boats and oars break and fail.
The fox cannot go south, the leopard has no way north.
Though they wish to meet in alliance, the river’s bridge is severed.

**Lin (临):**
The spirit’s presence in the north brings misfortune in opposition.
No profit in the southwest—the merchant halts his trade.

**Guan (观):**
Promises unkept, deceit and litigation mislead.
They leave me to lodge in emptiness, returning at night to the warm chamber.
The spirits rage at injustice, ghosts strike the eyeless.
Seeking fortune and profit, one instead harms oneself.

**Shike (噬嗑):**
Baring the arm to aid the elbow—rage cannot be stilled.
Fierce and cruel, doubling their savagery—none will trade with them.
Selling horses and buying oxen, they meet with emptiness.
Little profit gained, lingering brings sorrow.

**Ben (贲):**
Dwelling in a lofty tower, sheltered from wind and rain.
Exploring the hall’s deep chambers, the lord rests secure.
The hen’s offspring brings me fortune and blessing.

Requests unfulfilled.

**Fu (复):**
The chief minister dons the carriage,
Taiyi sets forth on the journey.
Escaping the times, passing through lodgings—
all sought brings prosperity and flourishing.

**Jian (蹇):**
Fortune flies in,
entering my dwelling,
to secure my state.

**Daxu (大畜):**
The calamity of filial impiety—
descendants become cruel.
The aged and infirm lack care,
sitting alone amid crumbling walls.

**Yi (颐):**
Passing south through the Thorn Gate,
a colt tears my cap.
Rending my robes, wounding the weak—
the lord is left with grief and sorrow.

**Daguo (大过):**
Shooting through layered gates at level ground,
uncertain where to settle.
Consulting tortoise and milfoil,
which path avoids the fire?
The luminous spirits respond,
instructing with sacrificial offerings—
best to halt and remain in place.

**Kan (坎):**
Unicorns, river deer, and phoenix fledglings—
secure and joyful, without care.
Fishing in rivers and seas,
profit lies in relocating.

**Li (离):**
New fields yield millet,
the diligent farmer reaps grain.
The noble holds fast to virtue,
thereby securing all blessings.

**Xian (咸):**
Hairpins too short, sashes too long—
dark thoughts breed bitter hardship.
Emaciated and frail,
plagued by lingering illness.

**You (游):**
The gate of litigation—
no neighbor should dwell near.
Entering and exiting with purpose,
worry saps my breath.
*(Alternatively: worry gives rise to my suffering.)*

**Dazhuang (大壮):**
Rotten roots and withered stumps,
failing to nourish flesh.
Sickness festers in the heart and bowels,
daily burning to desiccation.

**Jin (晋):**
The Rain Master weds his bride,
the Yellow Rock’s youngest daughter.
The rites completed, the marriage consummated—
they call to each other as they ascend south.
They anoint the earth below,
bringing great abundance to the years.

**Mingyi (明夷):**
Winter gathers bracken and orchids,
the frozen ground hard and unyielding.
Fleeing north brings loss—
by dusk, nothing is gained.

**Jiaren (家人):**
Blindly bound for distant lands,
mistakenly passing the Tiger District.
I lose my well-cooked bear—
hungry, with nothing to eat.
*(Variant: "well-cooked" may read "bear.")*

**Kui (睽):**
At dusk, do not close your eyes—
follow the sun and rest.
Thus did Jizi,
and thereby received his fortune.
Undertake affairs with much speech,
and regret and ruin will surely follow.

**Jian (蹇):**
The rustling wind yields and defers—
the nobleman’s dragon-like radiance.
A distant cry, faint and soft—
fortune and blessing arrive as one.

**Xie (解):**
The bird flies wingless,
the hare runs with broken legs.
Though they do not meet,
neither finds advantage or success.

**Sun (损):**
The five phases clash and devour,
fire quenched by water.
Essence and light vanish,
unable to continue.

**Yi (益):**
Gathering wealth in Qi and Lu,
gaining fine steeds and eloquent words.
The glib and obsequious,
the merchant’s cunning—
neighbors share a wall,
yet refuse to lend fire.
The human path is blocked,
ghostly omens haunt their home.

**Guai (夬):**
Fighting over chickens, losing sheep,
the golden purse gone—
profit cannot endure.
The troubles of Chen and Cai
relied on Chu to find peace.

**Ni (妮):**
Nine ascents, ten descents—
the horse stumbles and halts.
With Guanzi’s aid,
it can at last climb the mountain.

**Cui (萃):**
The eastern neighbor’s sorrow and suffering—
the lord disrupts heaven’s order.
Daily craving rank and favor,
he will surely meet his doom.
*(Variant: Intentions align, aspirations shared—
within and without, they follow my uncle.)*

**Sheng (升):**
Three wildcats hunt the rat,
blocking my path.
Dying in the ruined precinct,
unable to escape.

**Kun (困):**
Wolves and tigers vie for dominance,
rites and justice abandoned.
They devour the state whole—
Qi and Lu left kingless.

**Jing (井):**
The Five Peaks and Four Waterways
unite their moisture in virtue.
Acting without straying from principle,
the people rely on their grace and fortune.

**Ge (革):**
Planting millet in the sixth month,
the year ends without rain.
Autumn brings no wine for libation—
the spirits lose their place.
First hardship, then ease—
fortune follows in its wake.
*(Variant: "wine" may read "cleansing.")*

**Ding (鼎):**
A startling mare with a dragon’s form,
daily yielding three thousand.
Ascending south to Cangwu,
she weds fortune in marriage.

**Dao/Li (道理):**
The Dao/Way is level and smooth,
the body at peace, free from harm.
*(Variant: "取疑" may read "趣," meaning "pursuit.")*

**Zhen (震):**
Going out and coming in, resting in ease,
dwelling in safety, free from care.
Fear of the upper chamber—
tyranny ruins the youngest.

**Gen (艮):**
Southern Mountain, Vast Heaven,
criticizing governance, pitying the self.
Sickness and affliction without guilt—
hatred turned to enmity.

**Jian (漸):**
Bound and weary, shifting paths,
the road twisted and lame.
The day’s omen ill-fated,
illness becomes a curse of misfortune.

**Gui Mei (归妹):**
The elder marches to eastern Yan,
the younger campaigns in western Liaoxi.
Great victory and triumphant return—
enfeoffed between the rivers.

**Feng (丰):**
The wheel’s rim breaks, its spokes snap,
the horse cannot proceed.
The slander of a stubborn ox—
betraying father and elder brother.
The commoner unharmed,
lives out life without calamity.

**Lǚ (旅):**
Driving to the southern sea,
day and night without rest.
The nobleman wearied,
the servant burned by toil.

**Xun (巽):**
Resentment piled high, scorched by flame,
rage breeds disaster.
A narrow heart acts—
intentions stir rebellion.

**Dui (兑):**
Arrogant and reckless,
stealing rice and grain.
The dog barks at its non-master—
gluttony wounds my foot.

**Huan (涣):**
The road cleared of faults,
the vanguard dispels blame.
The queen personally tends the mulberry,
leading the people’s labor—
peace for our ancestors.

**Jie (节):**
The household lacks food,
starving to death—who to tell?
The opposite door sealed,
where to seek urgent aid?
Store up the five grains,
one blossom with a thousand petals—
the market yields profit.

**Zhong Fu (中孚):**
Broken things mended,
the imperiled find safety.
Villages unharmed,
merchants profit—
fortune enters the door.

**Xiao Guo (小过):**
Layer upon layer,
like the chambers of Mount Qi.
One breath, ten children—
Lord Gu治 settled the town.

**Ji Ji (既济):**
Three crones govern the people,
unfit for their task.
Two horses fight over the chariot,
ruining the household.

**Wei Ji (未济):**
The mirror veiled, without light,
patterns unseen.
The youngest daughter does not wed—
abandoned in longing.

---

**Dun (遁), Hexagram Thirty-Three:**

**Dun (遁):**
The Three Paths and Five Peaks,
Yangcheng and Mount Tai.
Where the divine resides—
alone free from war.

**Qian (乾):**
Weak and unsupported,
unable to govern oneself.
Intentions lie in the wilds,
the heart burdened with toil—
though troubled, not endangered.

**Kun (坤):**
The prosperity of Zhou Cheng,
punishments set aside, no misfortune.
The multitude praises and aids—
the nobleman acts with benevolence.

**Zhun (屯):**
The den holds foxes and crows,
the marsh breeds toads.
The image departs—
impossible to capture.

**Meng (蒙):**
All are heaven’s people,
clouds pass my face.
Governing the people with envy—
no kindness to me.
*(Variant: "民" may read "门," "gate.")*

**Xu (需):**
Three hands, six eyes,
governance rife with confusion.
The chariot’s axle breaks, the horse goes lame—
chaos cannot be followed.
*(Variant: "手" may read "首," "heads.")*

**Song (讼):**
Virtue accumulated, not slight,
words from a true heart.
The matchmaker ceaseless—
great fortune comes to fruition.

**Shi (师):**
Firm and close,
daily use, daily completion.
The six relations unchanged—
who ensures safety?
The Rain Master drives west,
drenching my wheel hubs.
Zhang Bo and Li Ji,
each gnaw at the gate.
*(Variant: "日完" may read "自完," "self-completion.")*

**Bi (比):**
Within the domain, no progress—
spokes break, wheels shatter.
The horse restrained, the dike breached—
loving the moment.
*(Variants: "堤与" may read "提携," "support"; "爱" may read "受," "receive.")*

**Xiao Xu (小畜):**
The mare bears no foal,
the hen hatches no chick.
The flock of sheep, three years old,
bears no twins.

**Lǚ (履):**
Old and feeble, exhausted,
no uprightness to take.
The suspended carriage retires—
returning to one’s homeland.

**Tai (泰):**
Tangled threads, a snarled skein—
the hand brings disaster.
Chasing rabbits through the fields,
severing the reins of indecision.

**Pi (否):**
The sea ages, its waters dry,
fish and turtles wither.
The heights fall, no ravine—
only sand and stone remain.

**Tong Ren (同人):**
Entering the market seeking deer,
no head in sight.
From dawn to dusk,
nothing gained.

**Da You (大有):**
The gate built up, the door blocked,
a tiger in the road outside.
Startling my black steed—
no advantage in going forth.

**Qian (谦):**
Tao Zhu and Bai Gui,
skilled merchants amassing wealth.
Princes and nobles,
rich and noble, never poor.

**Yu (豫):**
Wang Liang, master of chariots,
Bo Le, knower of horses.
Circling in measured steps,
moving within the compass and square.
Resting with purpose ahead,
life extended, longevity attained.

**Sui (随):**
Yao inquired of Great Shun,
augmenting his sagely virtue.
Making the people unafraid,
secure and without ceaseless dread.

**Gu (蛊):**
Duke Zhao lost constancy,
the Ji clan grew perverse.
Wandering to Qi, dwelling in Zou,
he forfeited favor and self.

**Lin (临):**
Not proceeding, awaiting dawn’s light.
Hesitating, pausing behind,
unable to follow.

**Guan (观):**
At rest in fitting office,
promoted nine times in a day.
Rising above rank,
governing and nurturing Changshan.

**Shi He (噬嗑):**
Abandoning good for ill,
east and west beset by lawsuits.
The traveler achieves nothing.

**Bi (贲):**
The old horse droops its ears,
no longer seeing a hundred *li*.
The nobleman does not rely on it,
the merchant dares not acquire it,
no market to be made.

**Bo (剥):**
The blazing snail bears young,
deeply black and ugly.
Resembling its mother,
though some may approach,
the multitude rejects it.

**Fu (复):**
A hundred legs move as one,
mutual support makes strength.
Three sages assist the cause,
the royal house honored and radiant.

**Jian (蹇):**
Nurturing the people, gathering the multitude,
treading virtue with trust.
The great receive fortune,
the ignorant and young are troubled,
no profit to be gained.

**Da Xu (大畜):**
Stumbling left, faltering right,
tripping forward into the mulberry.
His fingers,
wounding his brothers.
The old painter does not work,
the household lacks silk.
Valuing goods over self,
long detaining the guest.

**Yi (颐):**
The dim-witted at dawn,
selling food, growing old and prosperous.
The state’s destiny bound in mourning,
hailed as the Grand Duke.

**Da Guo (大过):**
A worn-out basket on the beam,
the net slips, unchecked.
The fisherman toils in vain,
throat parched, mouth dry,
empty and with nothing.

**Kan (坎):**
Full at first, then stumbling,
the weak reclaim what was taken.
Overflow may bring ruin,
the sick grow lean, the bold grow timid.
Zheng, bright, lost its state,
Chong’er rose to power.

**Li (离):**
Breaking, perishing, shattering the jar,
plunging me into hardship and poverty.
Parted from the mother who bore me,
separated, dwelling apart.

**Xian (咸):**
The wilds hold stacked grain,
the miser harnesses and takes it.
Not meeting wolves or tigers,
he returns at dusk to his home.

**Heng (恒):**
Swaddled orphan, grown to manhood,
capped and sashed, establishing a household.
Exiting the gate like a guest,
what worry for his parents?

**Da Zhuang (大壮):**
Applying strength in rank,
office and duty unneglected.
Hands unequal to the basin,
losing favor at the gate.

**Jin (晋):**
Deep snow, bitter cold,
the myriad things do not grow.
Yin restrains the many officers,
the season’s root is cold and poor.

**Ming Yi (明夷):**
Dragons battle at the sea gate,
losing principle, harming the worthy.
Internal rebellion breeds treachery,
a malady of the heart.

**Jia Ren (家人):**
Unafraid of fierce tigers,
relying on others for aid.
Three men seize the beast,
it crouches, dare not rise—
the self secure, without blame.

**Kui (睽):**
Southern mountains, lofty ridges,
four barriers hard to climb.
The road distant and long,
the traveler achieves nothing.
Will sorrow not turn to misfortune?
Even ill may dissipate.

**Jian (蹇):**
Meeting the time, the sun ascends,
wealth and honor attained.

**Xie (解):**
Seeking what I desire,
gaining its profit and blessing.
A lifetime without disgrace,
a house of abundance.
The high roof gleams, covering all—
the lord loses his stronghold.

**Sun (损):**
Sitting at ease until dusk,
calamity and disaster do not arrive.
Profiting from inquiry and marriage,
the imperial pardon does not forgive.

**Yi (益):**
The glued cart harnessed,
encountering rain.
Five chestnuts fall,
the load suddenly lost,
lodging alone,
worry becomes personal misfortune.

**Guai (夬):**
Choosing the day to soar high,
journeying far to eastern Qi.
Meeting the sage Confucius,
he makes me harmonious.

**Gou (姤):**
Chen Mao and Jing Zhong,
portents of Qi Jiang’s rise.
Proceeding to Yingqiu,
eight generations flourish greatly.

**Cui (萃):**
The broken vessel fears nothing,
difficult to follow east or west.
Shattering my basin,
floating away, discarding food and drink.

**Sheng (升):**
Midnight dogs bark,
thieves outside the hut.
Divine light aids,
dispersing them, they depart.

**Dun (遁):**
The thunder chariot not concealed,
rumbling westward.
Continuous rain for thirty days,
flowing into rivers and streams,
bringing me misfortune and ill.

**Jing (井):**
The old river empty and dry,
the ancient well holds no fish.
Profit not abundant,
fleeing calamity to the eastern neighbor.
Misfortune enters the gate,
making me regret what remains.

**Ge (革):**
The man of fortune and virtue,
daily joyful and glad.
Yi Wu as minister of state,
three returns to serve—
nobility flows to descendants.

**Ding (鼎):**
The pure man, Gao Zi,
long detained in the wilds.
Wandering, not returning,
longing for my kind mother.

**Zhen (震):**
The sharp-eared, black-maned,
eastward returning to the lofty village.
The White Tiger pushes the wheel,
the Azure Dragon grasps the crossbar.
The Vermilion Bird guides the way,
the numinous crow bears the journey.
Far knocking at Heaven’s gate,
entering to see the True Lord—
horse whole, man safe.

**Gen (艮):**
The road thick with brambles,
ahead, thorns pierce my feet.
Unfavorable for travelers,
poisoning the heart.

**Jian (渐):**
Upright conduct breeds trouble,
worry enters the gate,
making me uneasy.

**Gui Mei (归妹):**
The market of Xiao Zou,
profit not worth delight.
Two generations of accumulated goodness,
honoring their ancestors.
Words not for self—
madness and perversity bring misfortune.

**Feng (丰):**
Ascending heights to survey the time,
seeing joy without sorrow.
Seeking profit in the southern land,
obtaining treasure in return.

**Lǚ (旅):**
The lame rest their shoulders,
fearing difficulty ahead.

The city walls are gilded, its gates forged of iron,
with bronze as its bolts.
Barriers and screens defend the self,
securing the high without peril.

**Xun (巽):**
The Yangtze swells and surges,
longing to follow the noble man.
Elder and younger brothers dwell in the marketplace,
yet refuse to glance my way—
nieces and sisters left in sorrow and regret.

**Dui (兑):**
Shoots and sprouts grow forth,
yang’s song resounds without.
Left hand grasping the key,
the lord speaks, bestowing rank.

**Huan (涣):**
The gardens of Yunmeng teem,
all things flourish and thrive.
Rhinoceros and elephant, hawksbill and tortoise—
the people of Jing grow rich.

**Jie (节):**
The Qurong march a thousand miles,
day and night in bitter toil.
Selling armor, donning common garb,
though burdened, no harm befalls.
The scourge of hawks and falcons
cannot bring injury.

**Zhong Fu (中孚):**
Stepping forth, journey bound,
the day auspicious, the hour fair.
Foot and horse, chariot and team,
passing through the capital’s thoroughfares.
At dusk, lodging in northern Yan,
meeting joy along the way.

**Xiao Guo (小过):**
Riding a fine steed, gray and dappled,
trading southward across Mount Taihang.
Encountering dragons, fierce tigers,
swallowed whole—
buried on the northern bank of the Wei.

**Ji Ji (既濟):**
Iron foundations meet their time,
Hou Ji, Xie, and Gao Yao rise.
The steadfast and good fulfill their wishes,
Weizi freed from his bonds.
The marketplace fears no tiger—
idle words weave false knots.

**Wei Ji (未濟):**
Wine, the joyful elder,
dispels sorrow but brings no delight.
Fortune and goodness enter the gate,
seeking the lord’s company,
granting me gain.

**Da Zhuang (大壯):**
To the left, a ravenous bear gnaws;
to the right, a roaring tiger sings.
Ahead, it clashes with a spear of thorns;
behind, it stumbles on a drawn crossbow—
none can withstand.

**Qian (乾):**
Teeth of gold, fangs of iron,
long life and a prosperous home.
Years of plenty in store,
the greedy profit, yet bear
their own woes and sorrows.

**Kun (坤):**
Households content, people fulfilled,
hymns of praise rise as one.
The four barbarians submit in awe,
weapons laid to rest in their racks.

**Zhun (屯):**
Monkeys in caps and sashes,
thieves usurping rank.
The pack of dogs barks wildly,
madly dashing, tripping over their feet.

**Meng (蒙):**
The heart afflicts the self,
heedless of peace or safety.
Loyal ministers, filial sons,
rid the state of its perils.

**Xu (需):**
The lord lacks bright virtue,
his ministers sow chaos and delusion.
The chief minister commands the chariot,
while Hu Hai loses his place.

**Song (訟):**
Bound westward, the path ends;
north and south, no merit gained.
Zhang Bo buys a deer,
his followers lose their sheep.

**Shi (師):**
The deer descends the western hill,
seeking to guard its herd.
Meeting Yi’s arrow’s tip,
it dies at the shaft’s end.

**Bi (比):**
The first omen of Ming Yi,
three dawns bring disaster.
Returning, its name is Jian Niu.
With slander, it strips and ruins Shusun,
leaving the starved to perish on barren mounds.

**Xiao Xu (小畜):**
Qin loses its fine abode,
the River Earl stirs trouble.
Returning the jade he seized,
the gods rage, offering no protection.
Woven silks lack pattern,
burnt incense yields no fragrance.

**Lü (履):**
A lord of utmost virtue,
misfortune does not cross his neighbor’s threshold.
He ensures his lineage endures,
his person free from harm and woe.

**Tai (泰):**
The hall of myriad evils,
where villains gather to wreak havoc.
They poison the good,
blocking the Way from passage.

**Pi (否):**
Three fools, six madmen,
yearning for a peaceful village.
Bewildered, they lose the path,
unable to tell dusk from dawn.

**Tong Ren (同人):**
The old and weak childless,
unable to care for themselves.
Though Guo’s house grieves,
the fault never departs.
When Guanzi governs the state,
the feudal lords submit.
Eight hundred chariots in procession,
honoring the ancestral virtue.

**Da You (大有):**
The later-born serpent of Bao,
the sun of Jing grows dim with age.
Pursuing the frail and senile,
it extinguishes the radiance of Huang Li.

**Qian (謙):**
Ears sharp, beard black,
bound for the southern homeland.
The White Tiger pushes the wheel,
the Azure Dragon grasps the crossbar.
Yet it reaches calamity and harm,
never escaping woe.

**Yu (豫):**
Trusting the cunning Long Ju,
he dams the river’s upper flow.
Mid-crossing, he slits the sack—
the Chu army drowns in ruin.

**Sui (隨):**
The youngest daughter of Youshen,
becomes queen and consort.
