Moistening ten thousand miles,
People rely on its joy.

**Weiji (未济)**
Bronze men forge pillars,
Exposed to toil and hardship.
Year’s end, month’s close,
No rest, no respite.
*Huan* changing to the fifty-ninth.

**Huan (涣)**
Hopes for favor unmet,
The literary carriage unfinished.
A lamb pursues a hare—
The hound’s quarry slips away.
*Weiji* changing to *Dui*, *Qian* changing to *Jiji*.

**Qian (乾)**
Winds block the path,
Chariots race in haste.
Abandoning fame to chase the lost,
Losing harmony and measure,
A heart heavy with sorrow.
*Xu* changing to *Guo*.

**Kun (坤)**
Serpents find marsh grass,
No fear of peril.

**Zhun (屯)**
Two dogs fight,
Thighs wounded, no refuge.
No feud formed,
They part and depart.

**Meng (蒙)**
Through misfortune, fortune received,
Joy fills the house,
All sought affairs attained.

**Xu (需)**
The river holds a precious pearl,
The sea teems with great fish.
Hasten swiftly—
Wealth may be gained.
*Jie* changing to *Zhongfu*.

**Song (讼)**
Three oxen bear a dog,
Taking the eleventh as mother.
The Jing barbarians invade from above,
Lady Ji flees in flight.
*Kun* changing to *Zhen*, *Pi* changing to *Shen*, *Xu* changing to *Song*, *Bi* changing to *Fu*.

**Shi (师)**
Anxi and Kangju,
Foreign lands of wretched huts.
Not our accustomed ways—
The elder is troubled and perplexed.

**Bi (比)**
Journeying, one strikes the heavenly net,
Horse dead, chariot broken.
No means of support,
Beset by poverty and hunger.

**Xiao Xu (小畜)**
Naked, one chases the fox,
Becoming a laughingstock.
The hen pheasant crows at dawn—
The mistress brings disorder to the household.
*Dayou* changing to *Xian*.

**Lǚ (履)**
Seeking a wife for the youngest,
Her family dwells by the eastern sea.
The waters rise without crimson,
No sight of what was sought.
*Zhun* changing to *Jian*.

**Tai (泰)**
Man and woman share the chamber,
Two clans eat as one.
Marriage is most felicitous,
Fitting for our many descendants.

**Pi (否)**
The Gate of Heaven,
The Emperor’s hall—
Yellow Thearch’s appointed place.
Barriers and guards encircle,
None may enter.
Ever secure and long abiding,
No misfortune or calamity.

**Tong Ren (同人)**
Weighing gold at the market,
One wishes to buy a steaming pot.
A cunning thief strikes,
Stealing my yellow treasure.

**Dayou (大有)**
Three travel together,
Longing to return to their homeland.
Gazing at the village, they enter the gate,
Bowing to meet their kin.

**Qian (謙)**
Wedded to a maiden of the Jiang clan,
A carriage welcomes the new bride.
Young Qi stands at the gate,
The husband rejoices with delight.

**Yu (豫)**
Eldest and second brothers journey together,
Seeking great omens in the south.
The eldest’s feet ache with pain,
The youngest shoulders the pack.
The steadfastness of Liuxia—
Our state remains unlost.

**Sui (隨)**
Pure in body, white of tooth,
Declining age revives once more.
Many grandsons and numerous sons,
Fitting for mother- and father-in-law.

**Gu (蠱)**
Sleeping alone, hating the night,
The toad-mother fears the day.
King Ping banishes Jian,
The scion of Jing trembles with dread.

**Lin (臨)**
Pursuing the fleeing, chasing the routed,
Calling back the youngest uncle.
Reaching the mountain, they find him,
Returning him to his chamber.
*Weiji* changing to *Daguo*, *Xu* changing to *Huan*.

**Guan (觀)**
A horse flies without wings,
A hare runs with broken legs.
Though they wish to meet as one,
Their effort comes to naught.

**Shi He (噬嗑)**
Embracing emptiness, grasping at void,
A magpie startles my fledglings—
Profit departs, nothing achieved.
*Li* changing to *Jiaren* (or *Cheng* as *Lai*).

**Bi (賁)**
Mountains become vast pools,
Dry land turns to sea—
Each finds its proper place.

**Bo (剝)**
Devoured by a tiger,
On the southern slopes of Mount Tai.
Though many follow,
None dare to save or shelter.

**Fu (復)**
Four steeds wind and wander,
Longing for home, missing their mother.
Royal affairs know no rest,
No peace to be found.
*Lǚ* changing to *Jiàn*.

**Wu Wang (無妄)**
The gibbon’s words,
Empty and incomplete.
My white horse led astray,
Its mouth never to arrive.
(Alternatively: *Qian*’s mouth comes.)

**Da Xu (大畜)**
Not flying far away,
Humbly serving within—
Wealth and sustenance not yet abundant.
*Fēng* changing to *Huàn*.

**Yi (頤)**
Thick tail, slender waist,
Too heavy to sway.
Yin’s power rules the state,
Pingzi banishes Zhao.

**Da Guo (大過)**
Born at dawn, dead by dusk,
Called the infant ghost—
No sacrifice may be offered.
*Xiǎo Xù* changing to *Shēng*, *Wèi Jì* changing to *Qián*, *Zhèn* changing to *Kūn*.

**Kan (坎)**
The Master feared in Kuāng,
Besieged in Chén and Cài.
Bright virtue unshaken,
At last escaping peril.
*Duì* changing to *Tài*, *Dà Guò* changing to *Jìn*, *Shī* changing to *Dǐng*.

**Li (離)**
Fearing darkness, dwelling in hiding,
Waiting for the hour of brightness.
At last meeting the white sun,
Becoming the world’s honored lord.

**Cheng (成)**
A white bird bearing food,
Calling to its young.

Wheeling branches spread their wings,
Coming to follow their mother.
*Jìn* changing to *Zhèn*.

**Dá**
Gong, shang, jue, zhi—
The five notes rise in harmony.
Ruler and minister, father and son,
Younger brothers in proper order.
The dragon virtue of Yao and Shun,
The state knows no disaster or blame.

**Suì**
Lady Ji hesitates,
Gazing toward the corner of Meng’s city.
From dawn till dusk,
She does not see the Marquis of Qi.
*Humility* changing to *Xùn*.

**Dà Zhuàng**
Ghosts wail at the altar,
Grieving for the childless.
At dawn on *jiǎzǐ*,
The Yin people sever ancestral rites.
*Dà Guò* changing to *Kūn*.

**Jìn**
What Heaven bestows,
Fortune and blessing ever abide,
No fear of peril or danger.
*Xiǎo Xù* changing to *Fǎn*, *Sǔn* changing to *Zhuī*, *Lín* changing to *Fù*.

**Míng Yí**
Paired eyes, joined wings,
Following one another to good fortune.
Jiang’s youngest daughter,
Her virtue harmonizes with the lord’s.
*Jié* changing to *Suí*.

**Jiā Rén**
Gathering, gathering—brilliant and tangled,
Gradually collapsing, falling in ruin.
Its good name destroyed,
Long lost, never whole.
*Tài* changing to *Qiān*, *Pǐ* changing to *Lí*, *Yì* changing to *Kǎn*.

**Kuí**
Broken chopsticks blind the eye,
The youngest uncle unseen.
A three-legged flying crow,
Far gone from home.
*Shī* changing to *Méng*.

**Jiǎn**
The sheep’s gut path winds ninefold,
Urging one another forward.
Cease your vexation, royal scion—
Only then may you ascend to Heaven.
*Fēng* changing to *Shēng*, *Lǚ* changing to *Shī*, *Gǔ* changing to *Bō*, *Sǔn* changing to *Tún*.

**Jiě**
The earth’s thick virtue,
All things flourish and multiply.
Peaceful, upright, and straight,
Thus securing great fortune.
*Tài* changing to *Jiě*, *Xùn* changing to *Kùn*, *Bì* changing to *Lǚ*.

**Sǔn**
In the wilds beyond Shēn,
Not meeting the lord of Yao.
Returning to a desolate place,
Heart laboring, will in bitterness.

**Yì**
Long shadows stretch on the path,
Coming to behold the mulberry and jujube.
The elder rejoices,
The younger proves worthy.
*Yāng*

**Zhōng**
The Zhou army attacks Zhòu,
Battling at Mùyě.
At dawn on *jiǎzǐ*,
All under Heaven rejoices.
*Qiān* changing to *Shì Kè*, *Jié* changing to *Shēng*.

**Shēn**
Seeking oranges by the Yu River,
Alongside great chestnuts obtained.
Boiling lamb, roasting pig,
Feasting, singing, and laughter.

**Cuì**
A broken bamboo basket on the weir,
Fish slipping through, unrestrained.
The fisherman toils in vain,
Mouth parched, throat dry,
Empty-handed, with nothing.
*Chí* changing to *Dà Guò*.

**Shēng**
Born of yin’s corruption,
Ruling the household without yang.
Thus receiving retribution.
The Zhang family’s fortune wanes,
Worry and grief compound misfortune.

**Kùn**
A distant land, a strange road,
Many are the monstrous and foul.
It makes me startle in fear,
Longing for my old home.

**Jǐng**
Wandering lost, losing the way,
Neither horse nor ox to be found.
Bó Jiǎ flees,
The market vanishes, leaving nothing.

**Gé**
A female luan gives birth to a peng,
Divine marvels arise.
Riding clouds, the dragon soars,
The people revere him as father.

**Dǐng**
Layer upon layer, a splendid storehouse,
Like the chambers of Qí.
Raising one to nurture ten,
The ancient lord governs the city.
*Dá* changing—passing through *Zhèn*, sores and scabs afflict,
The filial widow unrecognized.
The lord bears many warts and growths,
Disasters arise in all four seasons.

**Gèn**
A ram’s head, a rabbit’s feet,
Emaciated, with little flesh.
A leaking sack of spoiled grain,
Weighed (or bartered) to no gain.
*Bō* changing to *Dá*, *Jì Jì* changing to *Sòng*.

**Jiàn**
Creeping thorns shift and spread,
Empty—who is there?
Lǚ and Jì lack understanding,
Rejoicing in evil, slow to comprehend.

**Guī Mèi**
The younger sister’s face grows too familiar,
Ruining the lord’s countenance.
Nothing accomplished,
She becomes her own heart’s enemy.

**Fēng**
Four horses share a yoke,
Bound together to ascend Mount Tài.
The black stallion and team unite in strength,
No great hardship remains,
Laughing and speaking with the lord.
*Bō* changing to *Jiě*?

**Lǚ**
Yin transforms to yang,
Woman becomes man.
The Way of governance flows freely,
Ruler and minister protect one another.
*Fēng* changing to *Jié*, *Wèi Jì* changing to *Fū*, *Tún* changing to *Lí*.

**Xùn**
The youngest son of the southern state,
Talented and of fine character.
Seeking my eldest daughter,
She is deemed unworthy, not given.
Instead, the ugly is obtained,
And later, great regret follows.
*Bǐ* changing to *Jiàn*, *Tài* changing to *Zhèn*, *Jiàn* changing to *Kùn*.

**Duì**
Duke Zhao loses his way,
The Ji family raves in madness.
Exiled to Qi, dwelling in the wilds,
His favor and glory lost.
Driven away by *Gǔ*.

**Jié**
Purple mushrooms on Wén Mountain,
Vermilion grasses on Yōng and Liáng.
Born of harmonious qi,
The king treasures them.
The ancestral spirit joins the feast,
Fortune and blessing arrive.
*Tóng Rén* changing to *Bō*, *Fēng* changing to *Jiā Rén*.

**Zhōng Fú**
Leading the sheep, it will not advance,
Turning away in opposition.
Hearing words, it does not believe,
Mistakenly deceiving the elder.

**Xiǎo Guò**
East Mountain, West Mountain,
Each rests in its own place.
Though hearts may long for one another,
They never share the same hall.
*Gòu* changing to *Kūn*, *Jiàn* changing to *Tún*, *Zhōng Fú* changing to *Bì*.

**Jì Jì**
The deer seeks its young,
In the tiger’s den.
Táng Bó and Jì Ěr,
Greedy, they do not grant my request.
*Suí* changing to *Pǐ*.

**Wèi Jì**
Three tigers climb the mountain,
Roaring at one another.
Hearts and wills not aligned,
Like enemies filled with hatred.
*Shēn* changing to *Xiǎo Guò*.
*Jié*, Hexagram Sixty.

**Jié**
The sea reigns as lord of waters,
Keen of hearing, sagely and bright.
A hundred streams return to its virtue,
None rebel or turn against.
Ever abundant, richly sufficient,
Yet jade is not to be attacked—
What one seeks will not be gained.
*(The last two lines appear to be an interpolation.)*

**Qián**
Tigers and leopards roar in fury,
Beware the perils without.
Above and below both in turmoil,
Scholars and people find no peace.

**Kūn**
Reaching into the nest, one finds fledglings—
Doves and magpies both descend,
Filling my heart with sorrow.

**Zhūn**
Gazing each day for a single meal,
Ever fearing insufficiency,
For one’s allotted fortune is meager.

**Méng**
A fine steed runs swiftly,
A thousand *li* in a single night.
Fleeing to another land,
Who can pursue and reclaim it?

**Xū**
The magpie’s nest in the turtledove’s city,
Above and below lack kinship.
Within and without turn against one another,
The child flees, heedless of return.

**Sòng**
Clouds and dragons gather in assembly,
Marching to subdue the western Rong.
Summoning the frontier, settling the masses,
Who dares stand against the vanguard?

**Shī**
Spring brings rich moisture,
Summer’s bounty is abundant.
Crops ripen in the fields,
Each *mǔ* yields a hundred *hú*.
*Lín* changing to *Míng Yí*.

**Bǐ**
A young servant sleeps alone,
The eldest daughter remains unwed—
No profit is to be gained.
*Yù* changing to *Yì*, *Gǔ* changing to *Lǚ*, *Guān* changing to *Méng*.

**Xiǎo Xù**
The four wilds are not at peace,
East and west bring calamity.
Withdraw and halt—do not leave the state’s bounds,
Thus may one remain whole,
Relying on the blessing of life.
*Guān* changing to *Xiǎo Guò*, *Lǚ* changing to *Yù*, *Dà Yǒu* changing to *Kuí*.

**Lǚ**
Long nurturing, treading in fortune,
Pacifying my hundred domains.
Noble guests ascend the hall,
Sharing the same grove with the youngest.
*(Domain may also read as "valley.")*

**Tài**
The qilin and Lü Er,
Brilliant, Zao Fu drives them forth.
Bo Shu presents his tribute,
Continuing the declining line.
Aiding King Wen to achieve hegemony,
He becomes the chief minister of Jin.
*Gé* changing to *Yāng*.

**Pǐ**
Zhang and Chen devise fine plans,
Assisting in the establishment of the Han capital.
Ruler rejoices, people delight,
Their joy is boundless.
*(Zhang refers to Zhang Liang; Chen, to Chen Ping.)*

**Tóng Rén**
A broad face and long head,
Come to dispel the lord’s cares.
Bestowing upon me good fortune,
Granting me fine favor.

**Dà Yǒu**
Dreading the dark, one does not proceed,
Waiting for dawn’s radiance.

Fires blaze in the hunt, yet branches and leaves remain unscathed.
*Guài* changing to *Sǔn*.

**Qiān**
The elder departs with my bundle,
His hair wild as tumbleweed.
Through the long night I cannot sleep,
Tossing on an empty bed.
Within, melancholy lingers,
Grief gnaws at my liver and bowels.
*Shēn* changing to *Lǐ*.

**Yù**
Rotten branches, frayed ropes,
Unfit for use.
Abide in stillness, await the hour,
To meet kin as dear as oneself.

**Suí**
Paired eyes, four wings,
Mutual reliance brings fortune.
Jiang’s youngest daughter,
Her virtue harmonizes with the lord’s.
*Huàn* changing to *Míng Yí*.

**Gǔ**
Ascending lofty heights,
Fortune and blessing overflow,
Dependent on Heaven’s might.

**Lín**
Treading the steps, mounting the hall,
Extravagance and stinginess—
The spirits do not bless.
Divine wrath descends,
Ghosts peer into his chamber.

**Guān**
Striding boldly into the carriage,
Southward to the Ji household.
They gift me a fox-fur robe,
Its fortune arrives in full.
*Zhōng Fú* changing to *Tài*, *Wèi Jì* changing to *Kūn*.

**Yì**
Boyi and Shuqi,
Paragons of integrity.
With virtue they ward off calamity,
No sorrow or disaster remains.
*Gé* changing to *Pǐ*, *Guī Mèi* changing to *Lín*, *Zhōng Fú*’s sustenance.

**Guài**
One female, two males,
The son knows not his father.
Disordering my kin,
Stirring my heart to fury.

**Shēn**
The lord abides in abundant fortune,
Where Heaven’s bounty lies concealed.
Dwelling in favor, prosperity flourishes,
The noble one shines with glory.

**Cuì**
A thousand-year-old locust root,
Its body scarred by axes.
The tree withers and bends,
Branches and leaves fail to emerge.

**Shēng**
The Zhou army rises to strike Zhòu,
Triumphing at Mùyě.
At dawn on the jiǎzǐ day,
All under Heaven rejoices.
*Qiān* changing to *Shì Kè*, *Huàn* changing to *Fù*.

**Kùn**
Sun races, moon strides,
They do not lodge together.
Husband and wife glare,
The lord loses his place.
*Fēng* changing to *Tóng Rén*, *Xiǎo Xù* changing to *Tóng Rén*, *Yù* changing to *Kuí*.

**Jǐng**
Proclaiming toil, exerting strength,
Serving as the king’s steward.
Pacifying the land, establishing millet,
All under Heaven receives blessing.

**Gé**
Chanting virtue, reciting merit,
Exalting Zhou’s flourishing might.
Shì and Dàn lend aid,
Illuminating the young lord.
*Míng Yí* changing to *Méng*, *Yì* changing to *Shēng*.

**Dǐng**
Three nights without sleep,
Worry deepens.
Warned of peril, flee,
Seek safe dwelling.

**Zhèn**
Longing for one’s desire,
Now the time arrives.
Washing away old sorrows,
Dispelling fears anew.
*Kuí* changing to *Gèn*.

**Gèn**
Crouching, whispering,
Moving by night, hiding by day.
Plotting to seize my wealth,
They come to assault my home.
Exhausting my resources,
Nearly undone.
*Jiàn*
The calf strays, the ox loses its young,
Bellowing in the wilds.
Queen Shen’s dark schemes are thwarted,
Returning to her mother,
Relieving my regrets.

**Guī Mèi**
Wang Liang excels at driving,
Bo Le knows horses.
Circling, stepping in rhythm,
Moving within the compass and square.
Resting with measure,
Prolonging life’s span.
*Zhú* changing to *Yù*.

**Fēng**
Freed from distant blame,
Avoiding harm, transforming peril.
In the field, three foxes are caught,
Cowrie shells become treasure.
*Lǚ*
Where the benevolent beast dwells,
The land knows no misfortune.
Market profits tenfold,
Returning to the kind village.

**Xùn**
Six eyes gaze together,
Each with their own intent.
A single word discordant,
Strife and contention arise.

**Duì**
Fu Yue’s brilliance,
Harnessing four dragons.
Traversing ten thousand *li* of Zhou,
No peril or misfortune.

**Huàn**
Eldest, second, third, youngest,
At dusk they sleep.
Drunk and waking blind,
Losing their cowrie pouch,
Prostrate by the roadside.
*Qiān* changing to *Gǔ*.

**Zhōng Fú**
The river holds a precious pearl,
The sea teems with great fish.
Hasten swiftly,
Wealth may be gained.
*Huàn* changing to *Xū*.

**Xiǎo Guò**
Gazing a thousand *li* afar,
Yet seeing not what is sought.
The keen sight of Lí Lóu,
No aid to the ear.

**Jì Jì**
Feeble feet, severed heels,
No profit in venturing forth.
Market trade yields no gain,
Loss and ruin become woe.
*Qián* changing to *Dǐng*.

**Wèi Jì**
Profit spent, matchmakers secured,
Yet the time comes not to me.
Wailing in deep waters,
Few lodgings, solitary dwelling.

**Shì Kè**
Bound eastward, pacing west,
Losing step, lagging behind.
The wild well of Qián Hóu,
Duke Zhāo loses his home.
Setting a tryst with them,
Unwitting till nightfall.

**Bīn**
Joyous leaps and bounds,
Welcoming the glad guest.
A magpie’s nest, a hundred carriages,
Bringing forth great fortune,
Abundant profit reaped.

**Bō**
Seeking what reason denies,
Who would assist?
Coming and going, no gain,
Vain toil upon the road.

**Fù**
Northern hordes of the Xiōngnú,
Repeatedly raid the frontier.
Left lapels as leaders,
The land yet knows no joy.

**Wú Wàng**
Continuing affairs in peaceful lands,
The sealed letter breaks, unfulfilled.
An infant’s stunted tongue,
Words lack renown.
*One version:* Marching without reason,
Speech thus nameless.
Though capturing subjects,
The elder’s merit fails.

**Dà Xù**
The star of virtue shines in the hall,
Unicorns roam, phoenixes soar.
Benevolence spreads far,
Hymns of praise arise.
*Yù* changing to *Jié*, *Sǔn* changing to *Kūn*.

**Yí**
In an age of civilized light,
Melt the blades, forge the plowshares.
By the Dao, prosperity follows,
A hundred kings unchanged.
*One version:* Burning fat, smelting blades,
Quelling peril and woe.
Invoking cultured virtue,
The lord secures his realm.

**Dà Guò**
The crow flies featherless,
The parrot battles, talons broken.
Idle sighs alone,
Who would lead the way?
*One version:* Defying virtue,
Self-inviting misfortune.

**Kǎn**
Grass hides tigers and wolves,
Snatching oxen and sheep.
Roads blocked,
Obstructing travelers, harming merchants.

**Lí**
The Lord of Shāng drunk and sunk,
The younger brother flees afar.
The wandering woman, the wayward husband,
Virtue and kindness both orphaned.

**Xián**
Three wildcats seize the rat,
Paths blocked fore and aft.
At such a time,
None can escape.

**Héng**
Táo Shū, Kǒng Yǔ,
Would not dwell in a troubled land.
Though signs were not yet sprouted,
Later they received blessings.

**Zhú**
Flapping wings, beating pinions,
Soaring in distant lands.
Carefree in the northern realm,
Not entering the warm chamber.

**Dà Zhuàng**
Pān’s virtue, vast and resonant,
Ascending the royal court.
The eight directions receive protection,
Blessings and emoluments bestowed.

**Jìn**
When change demands authority,
Meet and resolve hardship.

**Huàn**
Ice melts away in clarity,
The great state finds peace.
*Rising to Zhèn.*

**Míng Yí**
Feathers stir, horns savor sweetness,
Twin vines entwine the trees,
Years of abundance, harvests ripe.

**Jiā Rén**
Heaven’s aid and blessing,
Fortune comes, misfortune flees—
What worry for the lord?

**Kuí**
Square beak, broad mouth,
Sagely wisdom, benevolent depth.
Releasing the inverted, suspended,
The land of Táng finds great peace.
*Small Livestock to Biting Through.*

**Jiǎn**
Gé and Wàn tangled in thorns,
Flowers bear no fruit.
Slander and flattery disrupt governance,
Kindness blocked and stifled.
*Peace to Ignorance, Decay to Brightness Wounded, Army to Inner Truth.*

**Jiě**
The August Mother, abundant in grace,
Nurtures the filial grandson.
Freed from swaddling,
He is made lord.

**Sǔn**
Gathered waters, damp and low,
Northern wilds bitter with cold.
Exposed to dew, buffeted by wind,
The noble one’s heart aches.

**Zhōng Fú (Sixty-First)**
**Zhōng Fú**
Crows caw in measured steps,
Heaven’s fire descends.
It burns my house and hall,
Disaster reaches queen and consort.
*Joy to Revolution.*

**Qián**
In the land of yellow rainbows,
The worthy lord resides.
Guǎn Zhòng as minister—
The state knows no calamity.

**Kūn**
Shū Liáng’s renown,
Teeth meet in harmony.
Qián and Kūn favor constancy,
Six sons born from the breast.
Grown and accomplished,
Their words like a mother’s.
*Joy to Great Exceeding.*

**Zhūn**
Locusts gnaw my rice,
Driven yet not gone.
Full ears are nowhere,
Only hollow stalks remain.
*Abundance to Army.*

**Méng**
The infant seeks the breast,
The mother returns to her child,
The yellow fawn rejoices.

**Xū**
Breaking chopsticks to blind the eye,
The younger uncle unseen.
The traveler lost, the people scattered,
Far from home and hearth.

**Sòng**
Auspicious sheep with a mound-like head,
The noble one goes unfed.
Years of famine, dire want,
Scholars and commoners in peril.

**Shī**
The sacred tortoise on land,
Wandering, lost its place.
Yī Zǐ retreats to till,
Jié’s chaos finds no aid.
*Returning Maiden to Well, Returning Maiden to Peeling.*

**Bǐ**
Bound and imprisoned,
Falsely accused by others.
Gāo Yáo judges fairly—
Nearly freed and spared.

**Xiǎo Xù**
Crows ascend, magpies take flight,
Illuminating all to the eastern sea.
The dragon descends to the courtyard,
Successor to Táo Shū.
Enfeoffed at Liáojiū,
Fortune and favor deep and lasting.
*Humbling to Nourishment, Waiting to Great Livestock, Viewing to Nourishment.*

**Lǚ**
Four eyes meet in gaze,
Gradually drawing close, joining hands.
After the sun’s glow,
I see my elder sister.
*Increase to Waiting.*

**Tài**
Great strides mount the chariot,
Southward to the house of joy.

**Sòng**
Bestowed a fox-fur robe,
With fortune it arrives.
*Moderation to Viewing, Unfulfilled to Kun.*

**Pǐ**
Boring through walls to meet in secret,
Dare not show my face.
The matchmaker is unkind—
I am left unsavored.
*Fellowship*

**Tóng Rén**
Wild geese fly along the land,
The lord departs, never returns.
My elder brother sojourns afar.
*Peeling to Rising.*

**Dà Yǒu**
Relieved from garrison, the deadline missed,
Trouble comes, no solace left.
Fear sparks strife—
For me, it lays the foundation,
Yet the state is plunged in sorrow.
*Humbling*

**Yù**
The elder brothers quarrel,
Battle at Dragon Gate.
Grudges breed disaster,
Three generations know no peace.
*Contemplation*

**Zhōu’s governance nurtures thieves,
Severing the people’s limbs.
Land travel unsafe,
The state endangered by calamity.
*Following*

**Suí**
Cicadas and toads rejoice,
Grasses and trees flourish.
A hundred fruits burgeon,
Daily wealth grows.
*Humbling to Release.*

**Gǔ**
Minor disasters, sudden cruelty,
Wind scatters the clouds.
Aspiring to rise, denied—
Returning to its dwelling.
*Approaching*

**Lín**
Mounting a black mare, driving a black steed,
Journeying to eastern Qí.
Encountering travelers,
They gift me with goods—
I return home richly rewarded.
*Viewing*

**Guān**
The phoenix bears ten chicks,
Sharing a nest, nursing together,
Joyful and secure.
*Biting Through*

**Shì Kè**
Peach sparrows steal fat,
Nesting on slender branches.
Unsteady, trembling,
Blown by the wind.
My heart chilled, adrift,
Ever fearing peril.
*Decrease to Dispersion.*

**Bì**
East mountain, west mountain,
Each abides in its place.
Though they gaze across,
They never share a hall.
*Tarnish to Kun, Gradual to Initial Difficulty, Dispersion to Minor Excess.*

**Bō**
Crawling forth in flight,
Terrified, panicked.
A white tiger bears cubs,
Dusk gathers behind.
*Return*

**Fù**
Double crossbows aim at the boar,
Uncertain where to strike.
Consulting the tortoise oracle,
It speaks of plumpness.
The bright spirit answers:
"Fitting to settle here."
*Without Illusion*

**Wú Wàng**
Opening the gate to welcome fortune,
Joy draws near.
Blessed with good omens,
It becomes my home.
The palace city, Luòyì,
Summons cultured virtue.
*Great Accumulation*

**Dà Xù**
Crows take flight, foxes howl,
The state in chaos, unrest.
The low grow strong, the high weak,
Punished by yin’s force.
*Providing Nourishment*

**Yí**
Three parrots peck at grain,
Eight chicks follow to feed.
A hungry hawk strikes suddenly—
Two brothers lost.
*Great Exceeding*

**Dà Guò**
Sighing, displeased,
Worry wells from within.
My golden jar is lost,
Without Illusion forfeits its place.
*Abysmal*

**Kǎn**
Firm and yielding call to each other,
Two clans become one household.
Frost descends, they unite—
Bestowing kindness upon me.
*Dwelling People to Decrease, Gradual to Separation.*

**Lí**
Escorting my youngest daughter
To the swaying path.
Qí’s daughter, day and night,
Lingers long in delay.
*Mutual Influence*

**Xián**
Lowering my gaze, stealing glances,
Fearful of being seen.
My actions bring misfortune—
Wine turns sour, fish spoil,
None desire them.
*Cauldron to Release.*

**Dá**
Canonical texts and legal writings,
Stored in Orchid Terrace.
Though turmoil erupts,
Alone they escape disaster.
*Ground to Friend’s Decay, Kun to Great Gain, Great Possession to Dá.*

**Yóu**
Drunk at dawn, sick with wine,
By dusk, restored—
Alone, no blame follows.
*Great Strength*

**Dà Zhuàng**
Painting a dragon’s head and neck,
The pattern remains unfinished.
Sweet words, fair speech,
Persuasions lack substance.
*Rising to Youthful Ignorance, Youthful Ignorance to Biting Through, Dwelling People to Adorning.*

**Jìn**
Sun and moon in motion,
One cold, one heat.
Radiant glory blazes bright—
Yet cannot be preserved.
Stumbling, falling, cast down,
Reduced to common rank.
*Xùn to Zhèn.*

**Míng Yí**
Contending for profit in the royal market,
The court abounds with noble men.
The six states of Sū,
Gain their splendor and favor.

**Jiā Rén**
Six serpents dart and flee,
All into dense grass.
Startled on the long road,
Dreading the pecking beak.
*Jǐng to Duì, Fēng to Xùn.*

**Kuí**
Hanging fox, idle meal,
Eating what it does not earn.
Losing its carriage, stripped of its wheels,
It rests, then moves its seat.
*Yí to Yì.*

**Jiǎn**
Nine joyful children,
All meet with great delight.
Carrying blessings in hand,
The king’s grandson grows rich.

**Jiě**
Bó Yí and Shū Qí,
Paragons of integrity and purity.
With virtue they ward off calamity—
Worry and misfortune do not arise.
*Gé to Pǐ, Guī Mèi to Lín, Jié to Yì.*

**Sǔn**
A mighty sage hides his name,
Men conceal the startled qilin.
The phoenix flees north in flight,
Chaos unchecked, unrest unceasing.
*Pǐ to Dà Guò, Bō to Lín.*

**Yì**
Long widowed, without a mate,
He seeks a woman to know.
Pursuing what is not his desire,
He dooms himself to solitude.

**Yāng**
A siege of ruin,
Heaven does not bless.
Hard to halt or still.

**Shēn**
The old, slothful, and covetous,
Corrupt governance as thieves.
Ē Páng and Lí Shān,
Zǐ Yīng loses his state.

**Cuì**
Three offerings, six omens,
Following one another in turn.
Bewildered, they enter the empty marsh,
Crossing the tiger’s den.
Wounded and harmed,
They die in its jaws and belly.

**Shēng**
Whispering in the glare,
Groping in darkness, locked in struggle.
Much talk, little substance,
Words accomplish nothing.
*Míng Yí to Yù, Qiān to Qián.*

**Kùn**
Wǔ Yáng and Jiàn Lí,
Striking the zither, singing well.
Admiring Dān’s righteousness,
They aid Yān and Kē.
The plot fails in secret,
Huo’s eyes meet death—
What use is their fame?

**Jǐng**
Yǐn Shì and Bó Qí,
Father and son torn apart.
Innocent yet condemned,
A long tongue brings disaster.

**Gé**
Five essences in chaos,
Governance defies the emperor’s grace.
Tāng and Wǔ blaze in wrath,
Together they strike our land.

**Dǐng**
Westward to Jade Mountain,
Eastward through Jade Gate.
Ascending the hall of fortune,
Drinking the wine of ten thousand years.

**Zhèn**
Acting against taboos,
Offending the Controller of Fate.
Seized, bound, and restrained,
Held fast by the officers—
Yet the lost man finds joy.
*Bō.*

**Gèn**
A father unworthy,
The court teems with slanderers.
The lord loses his rule,
I remain long in poverty.

**Jiàn**
Three men journey together,
Seeking great omens in the north.
The eldest’s foot is ailing,
The youngest bears the load.
The steadfastness of Liǔ Xià—
Our state is not lost.

**Guī Mèi**
The crane longs for its mate,
Wishing to follow the phoenix east.
Following the pattern of feathers and wings,
It emerges at noon.
Though lingering in the northern town,
It returns to its chamber.
*Yì to Guān, Xū to Lí, Fēng to Tài.*

**Fēng**
Virtue constant and true,
No misfortune or disaster befalls.
As ever.

**Lǚ**
The white magpie soars and gazes,
The noble one finds peace.
Treading virtue without fault,
Fortune and blessings come to fruition.

**Xùn**
Virtue of keen perception,
Striving, forgetting to eat.
Capturing leopards and tigers,
Bringing the king good fortune.
*Dà Yǒu to Kùn.*

**Duì**
A hundred legs move as one,
Strength in mutual support.
Three sages assist the cause,
The royal house shines in favor,
The state prospers, the people thrive.
*Tún to Lǚ.*

**Huàn**
Born out of time,
Beset by hardship and woe.
Aged, worn, and frail,
The heart grieves in sorrow.

**Jié**
Leaving the gate, stumbling,
Looking back at the road behind.
The sheep bought has fled,
The goods taken have escaped.
Empty-handed, clenching fists,
Sitting in regret and blame.

**Xiǎo Guò**
Tending sheep in the rice fields,
Hearing the tiger’s roar.
Fearful, trembling, holding breath—
Yet in the end, no disaster.
*Suí to Jiàn, Jǐng to Pǐ, Bō to Sǔn.*

**Jì Jì**
The dragon lurks in the phoenix pond,
Jī Zǐ changes his garb,
Dark treachery begins to sprout.

**Wèi Jì**
No neighboring state to compare,
All vie for strength.
Chaos and strife,
The world in turmoil.

**Xiǎo Guò**
Though first alarmed and frightened,
Afterward unharmed.
Receiving its blessings,
Everlasting good fortune.

**Qián**
Accumulating virtue and benevolence,
Divine aid and trust follow,
Fortune grows day by day.

**Kūn**
Cautious in speech,
Though misfortune strikes,
Zhōu and Shào recount their duties,
Escaping the prison gate.

**Tún**
Birds take flight, flapping wings,
Rejoicing in Yáo’s virtue.
Yú and Xià record their deeds,
The distant lands submit in tribute.

**Méng**
Teeth sprout from gums,
The hall’s doors open wide.
The secluded one finds advantage in constancy,
Flapping wings, rising in dance.

**Xū**
Sending the elder east to seek,
He refuses to go.
Quarreling with his younger brother,
They slander and wound each other.

**Sòng**
Hands and feet reversed,
Head and tail inverted.
The lord becomes a woman,
Confusing early understanding.

**Shī**
The craftsman wields his axe,
The camphor tree in peril.
The robe stripped away,
His office and life end.

**Bǐ**
The Heavenly Maiden squats on the bed,
No pattern is formed.
Southern Dipper lacks a tongue,
The rice is full of grit and husks.
Oppressing the masses, stealing fame,
Male and female break their necks.
*Dà Xù to Yì.*

**Xiǎo Xù**
The great mallet shatters the wheel,
A long tongue disrupts the state.
Words like thorns and brambles—
Three generations know no peace.

**Lǚ**
Accepting the mandate, bearing humiliation,
Unable to fulfill the task.

Amidst the fall, burdened anew.

**Tài**
Three serpents share a chamber,
Kin finding kin.
Sweet dew descends in season,
Nurturing our hundred grains.

**Pǐ**
Adorned in brocade, roaming by night,
Encountering you, my lord.
Removing harm, dispelling doubt,
Freeing you from care.

**Tóng Rén**
Hair unbound, beastly heart—
Hard to dwell beside.
Arriving like wind and rain,
Departing like a snapped string,
Devoured by wolves.

**Dà Yǒu**
Hard and soft call to each other,
Two clans become one household.
With frost’s descent, they unite,
Bestowing kindness through virtue.
*Jiā Rén to Sǔn, Zhōng Fú to Kǎn, Jiàn to Lí.*

**Qiān**
Ox ears, dragon’s snort—
Deaf to sound and taste.
Entrusted with the cauldron’s charge,
Yet chaos reigns, befuddled.

**Yù**
Head bowed, eyes averted,
Fearful of being seen.
Travel brings no profit—
Wine sours, fish spoil,
None dare to partake.
*Dǐng to Xiè.*

**Suí**
The Rain Master weds his bride,
Huángyán’s youngest son.
Rites completed, marriage sealed,
They call each other southward.
Enriching the earth below,
The year yields great abundance.
*Pǐ to Kūn, Héng to Jìn, Fēng to Bǐ, Fēng to Dà, Guò.*

**Gǔ**
Wearing a basin, gazing at heaven—
Stars remain unseen.
Fixating on trifles, losing the grand,
Fleeing beyond the wall.
*Bì to Méng, Xùn to Jiàn, Yì to Zhōng Fú.*

**Lín**
Two ride the carriage,
Moving their household away.
The well boils, the cauldron wails—
No place to dwell.

**Guān**
Arms flailing, elbows turned,
Rage unstoppable.
A wolf’s heart, treacherous core—
None dare trade with him.

**Shì Kè**
From fire and scalding woe,
Joy arrives in turn.

**Bì**
Loyalty and trust lend aid,
The king’s rule stands firm.
Gōngliú lays the foundation,
Wén and Wǔ bring peace.

**Bō**
Climbing high, breaking branches,
Stumbling into peril.
Cart overturned, horses spent,
Uncles sigh in distress.

**Fù**
The mulberry withers and falls,
Yellow leaves scatter.
Losing power, teetering,
As if no ground remains.

**Wú Wàng**
Phoenixes soar and wheel,
Gathering in state and home.
Thinking of my elder sister,
Reunited with our mother.

**Dà Xù**
Yin’s excess lingers,
Overflowing past measure,
Damaging the harvest.

**Yí**
Towering peaks, perilous paths—
The king’s grandson exhausted,
Stranded on the slope.

**Dà Guò**
Harmonious jade, gleaming pearls,
Consumed by fire.
Darkness blinds, light stolen,
Vital essence lost—
Discarded by the roadside.

**Kǎn**
The lord of Yú reveres the spirits,
Blessing my aged kin.
Reverently serving the ancestral shrine,
Though displeased, he does not leave,
Restoring my inner duties.

**Lí**
Warriors of claw and fang,
Resentful, curse their father.
Shifting woe to themselves—
No harm befalls their mother.
*Qiān to Guī Mèi.*

**Xián**
Granaries overflow, stores in the millions,
Fit for planting millet and grain—
The year brings growth.

**Héng**
Windows and doors stand open,
Brightness and clarity flow.
Wise counsel aids the sage,
Virtue spreads far.
Households prosper, people thrive,
The land flourishes.
*Dà Xù to Shēng.*

**Guàng**
Looming peril,
Misfortune heaped upon woe—
Devoured by the tiger.

**Dà Zhuàng**
Waters devoid of fish,
Land becomes sea’s edge.
The noble lose their place,
The lowly cling together.

**Jìn**
Jiǔyí’s misty forests,
Damp and unfit.
Loathed by phoenixes,
The noble depart.
*Wú Wàng to Xùn.*

**Míng Yí**
Six wings scatter in flight,
Returning too late.
Escaping to the royal house,
Losing their leader.

**Jiā Rén**
Not as upright as Duke Zhuāng,
Contending with me in dispute.
The matchmaker lacks propriety,
Bringing obstruction upon himself.

**Kuí**
Granaries overflow with grain,
The Duke of Sòng in peril.
The son of Hào perishes at the gate,
Losing his life and place.

**Jiǎn**
Losing the sheep, seizing the ox—
No loss, no worry.

**Xiè**
Summer wheat, coarse and wild,
Frost strikes its awns.
A sickly lord ruins the state,
Forcing me to kill and grieve.
*Tài to Zé.*

**Sǔn**
Dim and dark,
Unsure of black from white.
Wind and rain bring chaos,
Light hides, vanishing—
King Yōu loses his realm.

**Yì**
Wielding the axe, splitting kindling,
Sending the matchmaker to seek a bride.
Two clans unite,
Parents and in-laws drink together.
Fair as fur and walls,
The elders rejoice.

**Guài**
Six illnesses breed madness,
Foolish wandering, reckless acts.
Northward through the gate of woe,
Neighboring disaster.

**Shēn**
Driving sheep to the flock,
The ram refuses to advance.
Qìng Jì rejects counsel,
Bringing calamity upon his son.

**Cuì**
Two take different paths,
Rushing westward.
Beyond ten li,
They know not each other’s whereabouts.

**Shēng**
Duty yields to desire,
Self-serving greed.
Eyes fixed on gain,
Horns break, necks snap.

**Kùn**
Restless, disturbed,
Unsettled in bearing.
Illness in neck and nape—
Peril and woe loom.

**Jǐng**
Three rivers converge,
Waters surge in fury.
The royal house collapses,
The people starve.

**Gé**
Scorching sun, drought’s blight,
Damaging the harvest—
Farmers go hungry.

**Dǐng**
Floating, they leave to eat,
Bearing offerings home.
Reins loosed, horses tethered,
Beneath the southwest hemp.

**Zhèn**
A dwelling by the gate,
A place to rest one’s stay.
The scholar advances without peril,
Secure and joyful, they preserve each other.

**Gèn**
Beyond the hour, he does not return,
Male and female grieve in sorrow.
Roaming a foreign land,
Parted from his mother.
*(See* Jiān *and* Yù’s *Dà Zhuàng.)*

**Jiàn**
Fields hold a hut,
Borders bear melons.
Offered to the august ancestors,
The great-grandson enjoys long life.

**Guī Mèi**
Missing the time, bereft of friends,
The household collapses, he flees—
What use is a lost dog?

**Fēng**
A crooked nose, a forked path,
Three widows dwell alone.

**Lǚ**
Clothes worn inside out,
Summoned by the king.
Achieving Zhou’s completion,
He is enfeoffed and receives great fortune.

**Xùn**
It flies not far,
Returning to its former place.
In affairs, much regret.

**Duì**
A bloodied fleeing bird,
Deaf to the five notes.
Pa and Ba pluck the zither,
Yet the heart finds no delight.

**Huàn**
Seeking jade, he finds stone,
Not what the heart desires.
Prayers and wishes go unfulfilled.

**Jié**
Mountains collapse, valleys sever,
Great fortune comes to an end.
The Jing and Wei lose their order,
Jade and stone are already *(so)*.

**Zhōng Fú**
Mixed eyes, fear, and anger,
No peace in their dwelling.
Treasuries scattered and dispersed,
No profit to be gained.

**Jì Jì**
Crowds of evil fill the sides,
The phoenix’s wings are broken.
The Viscount of Wei returns north,
Leaving his state.

**Wèi Jì**
In the sixth month, one gathers *ba*,
Marching to punish the lawless.
Zhang Zhong and Ji Shu,
Filial and fraternal, drink wine.
*(See* Lí *and* Kǎn. *Jì Jì’s* sixty-third.)

**Jì Jì**
A black hare on the palm,
Gazing at the feet.
Cross-examined and interrogated,
False accusations prove self-vindicating.
Deathly slander is hard to refute—
The mouth brings disaster upon the body.

**Qián**
A colt roams the Stone Gate,
Green-Ears returns safely.
Receiving fortune from the western neighbor,
He retires to Jade Spring.

**Kūn**
In the yang spring, grasses grow,
All things flourish abundantly.
Where the noble one dwells,
Misfortune and calamity do not reach.

**Zhūn Rén**
Without feet, yet following the law,
The sacrificial ox is led out.
The male flees, the sheep startles,
Yet the household remains unlost.

**Méng**
The Great Mountain rushes upward,
Transforming to appear in the Grand Tenuity.
Chen Hao’s neglect and haste
Bring about disaster.

**Xū**
Riding a dragon, it spits forth light,
First dark, then bright.
A great hunt yields much—
The six armies prosper.

**Sòng**
A ram’s head, a hare’s feet,
Emaciated, with little flesh.
A leaking sack stores grain,
No profit is to be gained.
*(See* Huàn’s *Gèn, Bō’s* Héng.*)*

**Shī**
Through misfortune, fortune is received,
Joy fills the chamber.
No locusts arise,
The lord suffers no vexing delusions.

**Bǐ**
Shun ascends, Great Yu,
In the wilds of Stone Yi.
Marching to the king’s gate,
They are charged with governing water and earth.
*(See* Qián’s *Zhōng Fú, Yù’s* Xiǎo Chù, *Duì’s* Cuì.*)*

**Xiǎo Chù**
Crow chicks and magpie fledglings
Always dwell with their mother.
Of a kind, they gather in flocks,
Never leaving their nest.

**Lǚ**
Yi shoots where the land is level,
Every shot finds its mark.
Adding to the granary’s hawks,
Two crows are both taken.

**Tài**
The zhen-bird’s patterned wings,
Soaring high to seek warmth.
Passing blindly through our town,
Yi gains nothing.

**Xiǎo Chù’s Gé, Dà Guò’s Yù, Dà Zhuàng’s Zhèn.**

**Pǐ**
Six joys, three fortunes,
Southward to the land of delight.
With joy they share in mirth,
Praising my pure virtue.

**Tóng Rén**
Fighting dragons break their thighs,
The sun thus fails to shine.
From without they seize control,
The younger brother strikes his elder.

**Dà Yǒu**
Veiled in blessing, receiving fortune,
There is gain to be had—
No advantage in venturing beyond the realm.

**Qiān**
Barbarians and tribes of the wilds,
Gathered where the Great Yin prevails.
The waters freeze in bitter cold,
The noble man does not endure.

**Yù**
Fearing darkness, they lurk in hiding,
Awaiting the hour of brightness.
At last they meet the white sun,
Becoming lords of glory and rank.

**Suí**
Waters flow downward,
Seeking the Eastern Sea.
They seek what I possess,
To buy sturgeon and carp.

*(See* Zhèn’s *Pǐ, Sǔn’s* Xū, *Yì’s* Wú Wàng.*)*

**Gǔ**
Donning cap and sash, he journeys south,
To meet with fortune and joy.
Bound for a land of splendor,
Freed to encounter the times.

**Lín**
The shama thrush shakes its wings,
Becoming the gatekeeper for the youngest son.
Freshly bathed, he dusts his cap—
The elder father rejoices.

**Guān**
Entangled in flowing waters, drowned in slander,
Bound fast by calumny.
Zhou and Shao fulfill their duties,
Their persons receive great fortune.

**Shì Kè**
Field mice and wild quails,
Ever eager to flee.
Confined in cages and pens,
They cannot stir or sway.

*(See* Yāng’s *Qiān, Xū’s* Suí, *Xùn’s* Suí.*)*

**Bì**
Dwelling on Mount Hua’s peak,
Wandering to watch the floating clouds.
Though rain falls, it does not soak—
The heart delights, free of care.

**Bō**
Leaning perilously, about to fall,
Chaos cannot be sustained.
Heroes take up their work,
Yet the house languishes without end.

**Fù**
The heart’s desire is fulfilled,
Now at last the time is met.
Preserving my gains and fortune,
Unseparated from arms and armor.

*(See* Duì’s *Jiǎn.*)*

**Wú Wàng**
The sacred tortoise dwells on land,
Hesitating, losing its place.
Yi Yin retreats to plow,
Later enfeoffed in Xia.

**Dà Xù**
West of the Weak Waters,
There is the Queen Mother of the West.
Born without knowing age,
She abides with Heaven,
No profit in travel.

**Yí**
Clutching jade, seeking gold,
Sitting in lament as dusk falls.
Through life’s end and the year’s close,
No success is achieved.

**Dà Guò**
Laughter and words not yet finished,
Sudden grief strikes like a storm.
The body is bound in fetters,
Beaten, shackled, and bound.

**Kǎn**
Hoping for favor, it does not come,
The literary work remains unfinished.
The prince pursues the hare,
The hound stumbles and fails.

*(See* Wèi Jì’s *Duì, Huàn.*)*

**Lí**
Trembling in fear,
Beset by dread and caution.
The road is fraught with obstacles,
One cannot take a step.

**Xián**
The male fox slinks slowly,
Ascending the rugged mountain.
Proclaiming his manifest merit,
Great fortune truly arises.

**Héng**
Fire rises behind me,
Gladness roasts my offering.
The Azure Dragon holds water,
Scooping it to moisten my pillar—
Though troubled, there is no misfortune.

**Gòu**
Sitting in peril until dusk,
Requests go unanswered.
Sweet rains do not descend,
Governance turns harsh, the people turn fierce.

*(See* Bō’s *Yí, Xū’s* Yí, *Jiàn’s* Kǎn.*)*

**Dà Zhuàng**
In early spring, the harmonious qi,
The hawk seizes the osprey,
The flock of sparrows trembles in fear.

**Jìn**
Lax laws foster long-standing treachery,
Injustice cannot be redressed.
Drowning in wine, losing restraint,
The lord suffers the consequences.

**Míng Yí**
Fish and turtles greedy for bait,
Die in nets and hooks.
Endangered by favor,
They bring calamity upon themselves.

**Jiā Rén**
The golden essence flares in anger,
Sword at his side past noon.
Wandering the high plains,
Lodging in the mountain valleys.
Two tigers face each other,
Bows and arrows fill the field.

*(See* Zhèn’s *Yù.*)*

**Kuí**
Four eyes meet in gaze,
Their essences close, their light shared,
Sitting together, they play the flute.

**Jiǎn**
Eating magic mushrooms and yellow elixir,
Drinking wine and jade nectar.
Communing with the spirits,
Ever free of woe and misfortune.

**Xiè**
Seeking the fine land of the roebuck,
The venomous serpent does not appear.
Returning from Bīn’s Qi mouth,
Back to his own bed.

**Sǔn**
The gates of Heaven and Earth,
Dark and unseen—
One knows not where they are.

**Yì**
The lame man rests his shoulder,
Hesitant, wary of difficulty.
A city of iron walls, bronze gates,
Barriers and screens stand guard—
Peaceful rest, no peril.

*(See* Lǚ’s *Yùn.*)*

**Yāng**
Three wild geese fly together,
Longing to return to the rice ponds.
Crossing mountains and marshes,
They are struck by arrows.

**Shēn**
The ford runs deep, hard to cross,
Soaking my robes in vain.
The prince plies his oars well—
No mishap shall befall.

**Cuì**
Drunk and sated with wine,
They leap and scuffle, fighting.
The elder is wounded, the younger falls—
The family prepares the funeral.
*(See* Bǐ’s *Dǐng*, *Dà Xù*’s *Jìn*, *Yì*’s *Méng.*)*

**Shēng**
Lame and stumbling, he cannot rise,
Losing profit at the late market.
Disaster and misfortune befall him—
He fails to gain the fawn.

**Kùn**
The celestial station descends to *Jiànglóu*,
The *Jiàn* star stands at the center.
Let the child not journey far—
Abroad, he stumbles and falls,
Meeting his end in the end.

**Jǐng**
The merchant’s wind summons bandits,
Who call forth foreign thieves.
Plotting with an inside ally,
They fight against me.
They seize my maidservant’s treasures—
The master cannot prevail.

**Gé**
Sweet dew and sweet springs,
The mechanisms of Great Peace.
Virtue’s resonance moves all—
The year is joyous, the people at ease.

**Dǐng**
*Jì Zhòngzǐ* bursts in,
Forcing the gate in haste.
Disaster arises, the son is wounded—
The younger brother attacks his elder,
And *Zhèng* languishes long in decline.

**Zhèn**
Rebellious steps falter,
Lagging behind, they cannot reach shelter.
Sleeping exposed in the marsh’s shallows,
They lose their fine robes.

**Gèn**
Wolves and tigers conspire,
Gathering to form a stronghold.
They lie in wait for oxen and sheep,
Bringing ruin to my merchants.

**Jiàn**
Illustrious virtue, keen and capable—
*Chónghuá* is recommended for office.
The merit of *Fàngxūn* is employed,
The Eight Sages receive protection.

**Guī Mèi**
A wretched ghost guards the gate,
Shattering my pots each day.
Breaking jars and smashing urns,
Empty and childless.
*(See* Sǔn’s *Bō.*)*

**Fēng**
Heaven’s mandate descends with the red crow,
Marking the time for military might.
Marching to punish the lawless,
*Jīzǐ* roams free.

**Lǚ**
Bound and imprisoned by force,
Falsely accused by others.
*Gāoyáo* judges fairly,
Breaking the shackles to free him—
He returns home unharmed.

**Xùn**
Sheep startle at tigers and wolves,
Ears pricked, they huddle in fear.
No strength avails against might—
They are torn by fangs.

**Duì**
Though first they weep and wail,
Afterward, they rejoice and laugh.
Radiance shines forth—
A hundred blessings align.

**Huàn**
The horse’s legs are long,
Well-suited for good trade.
Blessed by fortune, paired in harmony,
He gains fivefold gold.

**Jié**
The inner gate collapses,
The worthy are slain, the violent killed.
High and low alike rebel—
*Jǐnggōng* loses his throne.
Ever wandering, no home—
His wife does not return.

**Zhōng Fú**
Wielding an axe to split kindling,
He sends a matchmaker to seek a bride.
The two families unite in joy—
The bride’s kin pours the wine.
Fair as fur and fine as silk,
The mother-in-law delights, the father-in-law rejoices.
*(See* Xiǎo Guò’s *Yì.*)*

**Xiǎo Guò**
Two wheels turn with the sun,
Ascending the southern slope.
Four horses share the yoke—
No peril or hardship,
Laughing with *Yǔ* as they go.
*(See* Bì’s *Xū.*)*

**Wèi Jì**
A thousand pillars, a hundred beams—
Yet the hall never tilts or falls,
And the *Zhōu* house enjoys peace.

**Wèi Jì**
Slothful and unprepared,
With weak wine and meager jerky.
Approaching the spirits’ altar,
Seeking his desire,
He reaps great misfortune instead.

**Qián**
Born at dawn, dead by dusk—
Called the infant ghost,
Unfit for sacrifice.
*(See* Huàn’s *Dà Guò*, *Zhèn*’s *Kūn*, *Xiǎo Xù*’s *Shēng.*)*

**Kūn**
Striding boldly into the carriage,
Southward to the house of joy.

They gift me a fox-fur robe,
And fortune and joy arrive.
*(See* Jié’s *Guān*, *Dà Guò*’s *Kùn*, *Zhōng Fú*’s *Tài.*)*

**Tún**
In the west, many small stars,
Three and five in the east.
Rising early and traveling by night,
Toiling in vain.

**Méng**
Ice hides in the northern hills,
The nobleman’s heart grieves.
Bound by meager grain,
Ghosts startle at my gate.

**Xū**
Mountain springs rage violently,
Breaking beams and snapping pillars.
Travelers delay, beset by sorrow,
Lingering in distress.

**Sòng**
A four-winged paired fish
Comes to secure our land.
Fortune and joy ascend the hall,
Sharing my good fortune.
*(See* Sǔn’s *Suí*, *Tóng Rén*’s *Duì*, *Bǐ*’s *Lí.*)*

**Shī**
The swift hare darts,
A fine hound gives chase.
Male and female in flight,
Seized by the hawk.

**Bǐ**
Increasing salary and blessing,
Joy enters the chamber,
Dispelling sorrow and confusion.

**Xiǎo Xù**
Riding dragons and mounting phoenixes,
Ascending to meet the divine lord.
Péng Zǔ receives the needle,
Wáng Qiáo aids in communion.
Wū Xián takes his place,
Bowing for boundless fortune.
*(See* Jiā Rén’s *Bō.*)*

**Lǚ**
Heaven’s fire suddenly rises,
Burning my neighborhood.
Spreading to my home,
Emptying all my wealth.

**Tài**
Gold and silk, yellow treasures,
Fit for my market.
Marriage and trade prosper,
Profit a myriadfold.

**Pǐ**
A dwelling of ghosts and demons,
Inauspicious to stay.

**Tóng Rén**
Flying birds pursue the hare,
Each with its own fear.
Ospreys and hawks turn to thieves,
Disordering my house.

**Dà Yǒu**
Though first alarmed,
Afterward unharmed,
Receiving its blessings.

**Qiān**
Two metals strike,
Courage evenly matched.
Sun and moon clash in battle,
Neither broken nor flawed.

**Yù**
Dragging lines in rivers and seas,
Hanging hooks to catch carp.
The prince’s virtue brings gain,
To feast with Zhòng Fā *(or* Zhòng Yǒu*).*

**Suí**
Dogs fear tigers and wolves,
Seeking human protection.
Three men grasp halberds,
Crouching, daring not rise,
Yet remain safe and blameless.

**Gǔ**
Spiders weave their webs,
Lying in wait for travelers.
Green flies swarm in clusters,
Touching my snares.
Caught in the net,
Dying in its mesh.

**Lín**
What I await lies afar,
The noble charge arrives.
Polishing goblets and washing vessels,
Preparing food in anticipation,
Not for the youngest lord.

**Guān**
Sun and moon dwell together,
Ever dim, never bright.
Lofty mountains crumble,
Hills turn to streams.
*(See* Jiǎn’s *Xián.*)*

**Shì Kè**
Spring garments now complete,
Fresh blossoms bloom anew.
Stalks and leaves flourish thick,
Grains plump in ripened rows.

**Bī**
At Huáshǒu’s mountain peak,
The immortals’ Dao is roamed.
Favorable for dwelling here—
Ever free from blame or woe.

**Bō**
Three foxes wail and weep,
Lamenting loneliness.
No place to wander wild,
They die in barren mounds.

**Fù**
The scorching fire recedes,
Seeking ferns for sustenance.
Merchants fail to arrive,
The market stands empty.

**Wú Wàng**
Alone atop the peak,
Seeking deer to plow the field.
Grasses uncut, trees untamed—
Autumn’s hunger brings no yield.

**Dà Xù**
Though flames rage fierce,
They lie behind.
Though bandits draw near,
They stand to the right.
The self remains secure,
No peril, no fright.

**Guī Mèi** *(to Zhèn)*

**Yí**
Chatter and clamor rise,
Poor ghosts reproach each other.
No peace nor joy is found—
Nine knots tied in a day.

**Fēng** *(to Jìn)*

**Dà Guò**
Pursuing the fleeing foe,
Calling back the young uncle.
Reaching the mountain, he’s found—
Returned now to his home.

**Huàn** *(to Lín)*, **Xū** *(to Huàn)*

**Kǎn**
Bearing orders, shamed by the task,
Unfit to fulfill the duty.
Thus cast aside and fallen,
Made a petty clerk instead.

**Lí**
Adorned with pearls, clasping jade,
Bathed in virtue’s grace.
Answering the call to Táng’s land,
Its four gates serene and bright.
Rebels and thieves do not arise,
All evil hides in flight.

**Xián**
The mechanism jams—
No words may pass.
Yet sincerity prevails,
And man is wronged thereby.

**Héng**
The jar is cracked, the pot is chipped,
Lost on the southern road.
Wandering brings ruin.

**Lǚ**
Lips gone, teeth grow cold,
Roots rot with passing days.
Decayed and useless now—
A plague upon the self.

**Dà Zhuàng**
Bewildered and confused,
Knowing not east nor west.
The Dipper points the way,
Showing my lost center.
Favorable to halt—
Leave the state’s woes behind.

**Jìn**
Crows and magpies beat their wings,
To flee the hidden foe.
Thieves lurk near two maidens,
Yet fate grants them fortune.
Drought brings sickness—
The lord has no millet.

**Míng Yí**
Name achieved, virtue complete,
Yet the neck is never tried.
Duke Jǐng, aged and frail,
Confucius departs.

**Jiā Rén**
Words and heart deceive,
Westward journey, eastward gaze.
Gǔn dammed the floodwaters—
Flattery and treachery wrought disaster.

**Kuí**
Measures defy the rule,
Mustering troops at Jiāo and Huò.
Striking Hào and Fāng,
Contending with Zhōu’s might.
Great war chariots advance—
Decline reaches King Yí.
*(Alternatively: To secure our king.)*

**Jiǎn**
Three fires blaze bright,
Rain quenches their light.
High rank brings swift downfall,
Arrogance invites slaughter.

**Xiè**
Dark streams burst their banks,
Water becomes my curse.
My heart grows bitter,
Mother plants no hemp nor mulberry—
Dwelling here brings peril.

**Sǔn**
Dew soaks the dawn and night,
The path is thick with damp.
My robes and skirts are drenched,
Each step a heavy plight.

**Yì**
Fitting to trade in the market,
All sought is doubled in gain.
Embracing a child with joy,
Profit becomes a friend.

**Dà Guò** *(to Dàn)*, **Xùn** *(to Sǔn)*

**Yāng**
Yin shifts to yang,
Woman transforms to man.
The Dao of governance flows clear,
Lord and minister align. *(Huàn to Lǚ, Fēng to Jié, Zhūn to—)*

**Gòu**
The deep pool hides the male thorn,
A screen of brambles grows by the mountain’s side.
All foes become monks—
Who would welcome me?

**Cuì**
Seated on fine mats, riding grand chariots,
The virtuous minister holds power.
Yu Shu receives the mandate,
And the six directions dwell in harmony.

**Shēng**
Clouds rise to veil the sun,
Rain gathers upon grass and trees—
The year flourishes, the harvest ripens.

**Kùn**
The plum tree’s branches twist and break,
Parting from its mother, severed and unknown.
*(Travel to Great Exceeding.)*

**Jǐng**
Heaven’s drought dries the waters,
Withered and parched, no moisture remains.
Stranded among sand and stone,
No gain is to be had.

**Gé**
Jade tablets and pendants in hand,
He grasps the scepter to meet the king.
The bearded lord of a hundred *li*,
Answering the summons of Qi and Qin.

**Dǐng**
The dragon thirsts for drink,
Dark clouds gather in its wake.
The River Lord proffers sweet wine,
Kneeling to offer the cup—
The flowing stream pours forth abundantly.

**Zhèn**
Hail strikes the plum, its stem falls,
The heart broods in mounting resentment,
Disordering my spirit-qi.

**Gèn**
The deer seeks its fawn
West of the tiger’s den.
Tang Bo and Ji Er—
Greed denies me their consent.
*(Dispersion to Already Fording, Following to Negation.)*

**Jiàn**
Hollowing a gourd to hold water,
The reed-pipe’s mother kindles the fire.
Exhausted, strength spent,
Hunger and thirst bring calamity.

**Guī Mèi**
A dragon born in the horse’s abyss,
Long-lived and divine.
Dwelling in the Xuan Yuan’s halls,
Abiding in joy and enduring peace.

**Fēng**
The towering northern peak,
Heaven’s honored guest.
Warm, benevolent, upright, and just,
The lord bestows virtue’s grace.
Robes and caps never cease—
Heaven’s blessing is received.

**Lǚ**
Ghosts wail in the night,
Qi loses its state,
Betrayed by those below.

**Xùn**
Two governments, many gates,
The lord loses his authority.
Three houses seize control—
Disaster arises from the Ji Sun.

**Duì**
Hoped-for favor does not come,
The literary work remains unfinished.
Three sons chase the hare,
The hound’s quarry slips away.
*(Same as the Dispersion hexagram.)*

**Huàn**
Elder Tiger and Second Bear,
Their virtue and righteousness deep and vast.
They dispatch the Five Teachings,
And yin and yang follow their order.

**Jié**
Two feet, four wings,
Flying into the state and home.
My elder sister finds peace,
Reunited with her mother.

**Zhōng Fú**
Spring and autumn’s prayers and sacrifices
Dissolve misfortune, dispel sorrow—
The lord suffers no disaster.

**Xiǎo Guò**
Tending sheep in the rice fields,
Hearing the tiger’s roar in the thickets.
Trembling in fear, holding breath—
Yet no harm befalls.
*(Splitting to Decrease, Well to Negation, Following to Gradual.)*

**Jì Jì**
Great serpents and giant fish
Wrestle beyond the city walls.
Lord and minister are sundered,
The Duke of Guo leaves his hut.

*(Textual Variants and Emendations from *Yilin* as recorded in *Baizi Quanshu*:)*

1. Originally "司", emended to "喜" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
2. "危" in *Baizi Quanshu* reads "厄".
3. Originally "久", emended to "人" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
4. Originally "元", emended to "阴" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
5. Originally "阴", emended to "元" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
6. "年" in *Baizi Quanshu* reads "羊".
7. Originally "家", emended to "之" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
8. Originally "任", emended to "年" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
9. Originally "北", emended to "比" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
10. "福" in *Baizi Quanshu* reads "执".
11. Originally "利", emended to "困" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
12. Originally "温", emended to "淫" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
13. Originally "来", emended to "者" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
14. Originally "什", emended to "仆" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
15. Originally "缺", emended to "央" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
16. "任刀随身" in *Baizi Quanshu* reads "任力堕劣".
17. "怒肠" in *Baizi Quanshu* reads "恐伏".
18. Originally "具", emended to "昊" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
19. Originally "筮", emended to "噬" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
20. Originally "兵", emended to "邱" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
21. Originally "哀", emended to "齐" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
22. Originally "齐", emended to "哀" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
23. Originally "闭", emended to "开" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
24. Originally "属", emended to "齿" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
25. Originally "夙", emended to "风" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
26. Originally omitted "中孚之坤" after "母", supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
27. "酿" in *Baizi Quanshu* reads "缩".
28. Originally "夷", emended to "朱" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
29. Originally "荣", emended to "营" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
30. Originally omitted "荣庆受禄" after "得", supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
31. Originally "明夷", emended to "睽" according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
32. Originally lacking the *Sun* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
33. Originally lacking the *Yi* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
34. "傅" should read "博".
35. Originally lacking the *Yang* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
36. Originally lacking the *Gou* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
37. Originally lacking the *Cui* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
38. Originally lacking the *Sheng* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
39. Originally lacking the *Kun* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
40. Originally lacking the *Jing* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
41. Originally lacking the *Ge* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
42. Originally lacking the *Ding* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
43. "谁敢难者" should read "谁肯为倡".
44. Originally lacking the *Zhen* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
45. Originally lacking the *Gen* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
46. Originally lacking the *Jian* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
47. Originally lacking the *Guimei* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
48. Originally lacking the *Feng* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
49. Originally lacking the *Lü* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
50. Originally lacking the *Xun* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
51. Originally lacking the *Huan* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
52. Originally lacking the *Jie* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
53. Originally lacking the *Zhongfu* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.

54. Originally lacking the *Xiaoguo* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
55. Originally lacking the *Jiji* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
56. Originally lacking the *Weiji* hexagram and its *Yilin* verses, supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
57. "Yang" (*羊*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "three" (*三*).
58. "Jiejie" (*竭竭*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "jiejie" (*揭揭*).
59. "Ox" (*牛*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "year" (*年*).
60. Originally rendered as "shi" (*始*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "ni" (*妮*).
61. "Anxiety and confusion" (*忧惑*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "heart’s sorrow" (*心忧*).
62. Originally rendered as "gang" (*纲*)—amended to "net" (*网*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*. Alternate reading: "wai" (*歪*).
63. Originally rendered as "zhi" (*之*)—amended to "lacking" (*乏*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
64. "Red" (*红*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "boat" (*船*), as "red" is an alternate form of "boat."
65. "Heaven’s Gate" (*天门*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "Taiwei" (*太微*).
66. "Place" (*置*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "buy" (*买*).
67. Originally rendered as "think" (*思*)—amended to "person" (*人*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
68. "Woman" (*女*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "Lü" (*吕*).
69. "Our state" (*我邦*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "black and yellow" (*骊黄*).
70. Originally rendered as "monk" (*僧*)—amended to "hate" (*憎*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
71. Originally rendered as "master" (*主*)—amended to "king" (*王*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
72. Originally rendered as "call" (*唤*)—amended to "dissipate" (*涣*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
73. Originally rendered as "cut" (*切*)—amended to "merit" (*功*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
74. Originally omitted "become" (*成*) or "come" (*来*) after "person" (*人*)—supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
75. "Great" (*大*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "heaven" (*天*).
76. "Supreme" (*太*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "peaceful" (*泰*).
77. Originally rendered as "fur" (*裘*)—amended to "come" (*来*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
78. Originally rendered as *Kun* (*坤*)—amended to *Shi* (*师*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
79. "Meng" (*孟*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "child" (*子*).
80. Originally rendered as *Yu* (*豫*)—amended to "modesty" (*谦*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
81. Originally rendered as "strong" (*强*)—amended to *Lin* (*临*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
82. "Follow" (*从*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "await" (*待*).
83. "Unite" (*合*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "command" (*令*), though "unite" is deemed correct.
84. Originally rendered as "gather" (*翕*)—amended to "gather" (*翕*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
85. Originally rendered as "supreme" (*太*)—amended to "peaceful" (*泰*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
86. Originally rendered as "awn" (*芒*)—amended to "chopsticks" (*箸*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
87. Originally rendered as "pheasant" (*雉*)—amended to "young" (*稚*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
88. Originally rendered as *Jiaren* (*家人*)—amended to *Meng* (*蒙*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
89. "Urge" (*催*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "push" (*推*).
90. Originally rendered as "question particle" (*乎*)—amended to "level" (*平*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
91. "Ear" (*耳*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "chest" (*胸*).
92. Originally rendered as "stop" (*止*)—amended to "above" (*上*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
93. "Self" (*自*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "sun" (*日*).
94. Originally rendered as "center" (*央*)—amended to "man" (*夫*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
95. Originally rendered as "collect" (*收*)—amended to "herd" (*牧*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
96. Originally rendered as "moreover" (*且*)—amended to "dawn" (*旦*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
97. Originally included "hou" (*后*) after *Sheng* (*升*)—deleted according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
98. Originally rendered as "begin" (*始*)—amended to "ni" (*妮*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
99. "Pig" (*豕*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "roast meat" (*炙*).
100. "Muscle" (*觔*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "spin" (*纺*), with "spin" deemed correct.
101. "Act" (*作*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "forest" (*林*). Below, "rest" (*休*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "duke" (*公*).
102. "Sink" (*没*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "empty" (*空*).
103. "Cuckoo" (*鹃*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "chick" (*雏*).
104. Originally rendered as "obedient and cold" (*顺冷*)—amended to "Duke Zhi" (*公治*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
105. Originally rendered as "mansion" (*厦*)—amended to "emaciated" (*瘦*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
106. Originally rendered as "overturn" (*覆*)—amended to "millet" (*粟*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
107. "Calamity" (*孽*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "Xue" (*薛*).

108. "Move" (*徙*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "follow" (*从*).
109. "Not" (*不*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "child" (*子*).
110. Originally rendered as "sister’s appearance intimate and familiar" (*妹貌亲熟*)—amended to "sister’s appearance feverish" (*妹为貌热*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
111. "King" (*王*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "upright" (*正*), with "upright" deemed correct.
112. "Great" (*太*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "grand" (*泰*).
113. Originally, the character "explanation" (*解*) was omitted below the appended text "of" (*之*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
114. "Wei" (*卫*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "inherit" (*承*).
115. Originally rendered as "lack" (*缺*)—amended to "center" (*央*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
116. Originally rendered as "chariot" (*车*)—amended to "gradual" (*渐*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
117. Originally rendered as "chariot" (*车*)—amended to "gradual" (*渐*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
118. Originally rendered as "resist and roar" (*距哮*)—amended to "clamor and shout" (*喧唤*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
119. Originally rendered as "not yet" (*未*)—amended to "end" (*末*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
120. Originally rendered as "suitable" (*宜*)—amended to "doubtful interpolation" (*疑衍*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
121. "Other" (*他*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "it" (*它*).
122. Originally rendered as "cloud" (*云*)—amended to "need" (*需*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
120. Originally rendered as "field" (*田*)—amended to "boundary" (*畔*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
121. "Regard" (*顾*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "wish" (*愿*).
122. Originally, the character "observe" (*观*) was omitted below "tread" (*履*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
123. Originally rendered as "three" (*三*)—amended to "observe" (*观*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
124. Originally, the appended text "small" (*小*) omitted "error, the traveler’s ease" (*过，旅之豫*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
125. Originally, the character "separation" (*睽*) was omitted below the appended text "of" (*之*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
126. Originally, the appended text "state: one version reads valley" (*国一作谷*) was omitted below "forest" (*林*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
127. "Bo Shu" (*伯夙*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "Shu Bo" (*夙伯*).
128. Originally, the appended text "change’s husband" (*革之夫*) was missing below "assist" (*辅*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
129. Originally rendered as "bean" (*豆*)—amended to "good" (*良*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
130. Originally rendered as "water" (*水*)—amended to "come" (*来*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
131. Originally, the character "moreover" (*且*) was omitted; supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*. Below, "center" (*央*) was originally rendered as "lack" (*缺*)—amended to "center" (*央*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
132. Originally rendered as "field" (*田*)—amended to "use" (*用*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
133. Originally rendered as "sun" (*日*)—amended to "eye" (*目*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
134. "Its" (*其*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "with" (*与*).
135. Originally rendered as "child" (*子*)—amended to "trust" (*孚*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
136. Originally rendered as "come" (*来*)—amended to "not yet" (*未*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
137. After the hexagram *Observing* (*观*), the *Daozang* edition incorrectly places the twenty-two hexagrams from *Increase* (*益*) to *Not Yet Crossing* (*未济*) ahead, while the twenty-one hexagrams from *Biting Through* (*噬嗑*) to *Decrease* (*损*) follow.
138. "Exist" (*存*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "complete" (*成*).
139. Originally rendered as "lack" (*缺*)—amended to "center" (*央*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
140. Originally rendered as "obtain" (*得*)—amended to "know" (*知*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
141. "Resentful" (*愤*)—*Baizi Quanshu* also renders as "resentful" (*愤*).
142. Originally rendered as "lack" (*缺*)—amended to "husband" (*夫*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
143. Originally rendered as "good" (*良*)—amended to "keeping still" (*艮*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
144. Originally rendered as "timid" (*慑慑*)—amended to "whispering" (*嗫嗫*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
145. Originally rendered as "sixth earthly branch" (*巳*)—amended to "self" (*己*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
146. Originally, the character "one" (*一*) was erroneously placed before "lord" (*辟*); deleted according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
147. "I" (*我*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "with" (*以*).
148. "Tread" (*蹑*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "gallop" (*骤*).
149. "Change’s harm" (*革害*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "harmful drought" (*害早*).
150. Originally rendered as "revolve" (*旋*)—amended to "travel" (*旅*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
151. "Radiant rest" (*休明*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "Wang Liang" (*王良*).
152. Originally, the character "decay" (*蛊*) was omitted below the appended text "of" (*之*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
153. Originally rendered as "win" (*赢*)—*Baizi Quanshu* also renders as "win" (*赢*).

154. "Maintain" (*维*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "female" (*雌*).
155. Originally rendered as "unite" (*合*)—amended to "abandon" (*舍*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
156. Originally rendered as "two" (*二*)—amended to "duke" (*公*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
157. Originally rendered as "spy" (*伺*)—amended to "chest" (*匈*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
158. Originally rendered as "spread" (*铺*)—amended to "press down" (*镇*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
159. Originally rendered as "year" (*岁*)—amended to "assent" (*肯*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
160. Originally, the character "virtue" (*德*) was omitted below the appended text "its" (*其*); supplemented according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
161. Originally rendered as "fall" (*坠*)—amended to "troop" (*队*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
162. "Person" (*人*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "farmer" (*农*).
163. "Distant" (*远*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "run" (*走*).
164. "Roam" (*游*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "excess" (*淫*).
165. "Path" (*路*)—*Baizi Quanshu* renders as "block" (*遮*).
166. Originally rendered as "eight" (*八*)—amended to "enter" (*入*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
167. Originally rendered as "altar" (*社*)—amended to "strong" (*壮*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
168. Originally rendered as "heat" (*热*)—amended to "ripe" (*熟*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
169. Originally rendered as "great" (*太*)—amended to "peaceful" (*泰*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.
170. Originally rendered as "think" (*思*)—amended to "grace" (*恩*) according to *Baizi Quanshu*.

**Postscript**
Formerly, I saw records by Master Cheng of Shasui, which noted that in the early Shaoxing era, various scholars used the *Yilin* for divining contemporary events with remarkable accuracy, and they sought it for many years. In the Dinghai year of the Baoqing reign, I first obtained the book in Putian, transcribed it, and stored it away. Its verses were ancient and elegant, much like the divination phrases recorded in the *Zuozhuan*. I once tried divining with it, and it proved accurate, though I regretted that it contained many omissions and errors, with no other editions for correction.

In the Gengzi year of the Jiaxi reign, returning from Wumen to Xiaochuan, I happened to meet Prefectural Assistant Magistrate Wang Youdao, who borrowed my family’s copy. Though it also had many omissions and errors, by comparing the two versions, I corrected about eighty or ninety percent. Thus, both our family’s and Wang’s copies became complete. However, there were still many repetitions, with some verses shared by several lines, making verification impossible.

After collation, I returned the book to the Wang family and recorded the corrected version at the end of this volume. Written on the fifth day of the fifth month in the Xinchou year of the Chunyou reign, by Gui Yue of Xizhai.

**Postscript to the *Yilin***
The Dao of the *Yi* encompasses all things, yet scholars grasp only one aspect of it—none can claim it is not the *Yi*. The *Yi* existed in the Xia and Shang dynasties as the Three *Yi*. Later, the *Lianshan* and *Guicang* were lost, leaving only the *Zhouyi* in transmission.

By the Han dynasty, the *Zhouyi* had branched into three schools: the *Yi* of Tian He, the *Yi* of Jiao Gan, and the *Yi* of Fei Zhi. Tian He’s *Yi* was transmitted from Confucius, dividing the text into upper and lower sections, with the Ten Wings as Confucius’ work, complete with chapter-and-verse commentary. Jiao Gan’s *Yi* specialized in divination and observation, as seen in the *Yilin*’s sixteen volumes. Fei Zhi’s *Yi* excelled in hexagram divination, lacking chapter-and-verse commentary, relying solely on images, symbols, and the *Wenyan* to interpret the text within the eight trigrams.

By the end of the Han, the schools of Tian and Jiao had declined, leaving only Fei’s lineage. After Fei, scholars like Zheng Kangcheng and Wang Bi annotated it. In the Song, Cheng Yi of Luoyang based his commentary on Wang Bi’s *Yi*, which derived from Fei’s. Zhu Xi of Huian, following Lü Bogong’s *Ancient Yi*, grounded his *Benyi* in Tian He’s tradition. Thus, the *Yi* of Tian and Fei flourished, while Jiao’s *Yi* alone lacked transmission.

Recently, while examining books in the imperial archives, I discovered the *Yilin*. Its verses and rhymes were beyond later generations’ reach, resembling the divination phrases in the *Zuozhuan*. I transcribed and stored it, and on occasion, used it for divination—it never failed to prove accurate. Could Jiao’s *Yi* truly lack a foundation?

Alas, my shallow learning prevents me from writing a commentary to propagate it, aiding people in seeking fortune and avoiding misfortune. I record this to await a capable scholar. Written on the twenty-sixth day of the fourth month in the Guisi year of the Chenghua reign, by the later student Peng Hua of Ancheng.

**Afterword to the *Yilin***
The *Yi* embodies the four sagely Dao: those who speak value its words, those who act value its changes, those who craft tools value its images, and those who divine value its prognostications. These four are all transformations, the work of the numinous.

I read Jiao’s *Yilin*, which expands the sixty-four hexagrams into four thousand ninety-six, each with its own words, changes, images, and divinations. Its meaning is profound and probing, its language concise and ancient, truly revealing what Fu Xi, King Wen, the Duke of Zhou, and Confucius left unsaid, and unlocking the wonders of resolving doubts, achieving tasks, and comprehending aspirations. It is all laid bare upon opening the book.

Before entering office, I always regretted not having seen it. When I governed Wugong, Master Duishan showed it to me. After repeated recitation and reflection, I ordered its printing to broaden its transmission, privately noting the date here. Written in the second month of spring in the fourth year of the Jiajing reign, by Jiang En of Guang’an, on the publication of Jiao’s *Yilin*.

Those who discuss the *Yi* often regard Jiao’s *Yilin* as swift and accurate for divination and observation, yet lament its rarity. Recently, I obtained a copy printed by Prefect Kang of Duishan, entrusted to the magistrate of Wugong, and those who seek it now come in droves.

One day, while attending Military Commissioner Qi of Rongchuan during a judicial inquiry where doubts remained unresolved, he turned to me and said, "If only Jiao Yanshou were here—nothing would escape his detection." I withdrew and performed a divination, obtaining the hexagram *Lawsuit* transforming into *Following*, with the verse:

*"Jia, Yi, Bing, Ding—*
*All return to my court.*
*Three Chou, six sons—*
*Entering the gate, they see their mother."*

Five days later, the case was solved. Upon checking the date, it was the day *yichou*, and those involved in the lawsuit numbered precisely six. Those who heard of this marveled even more at the book, and requests for copies never ceased at my door. Thus, I reprinted it for completion, wishing to guide those who grapple with doubts toward proper action and avoidance, so that Jiao’s *Changes*—long a hidden secret—might once more be widely disseminated. Written on the tenth day of mid-summer in the *jiawu* year of the Jiajing reign, by Ma Lin of Chang’an.
